The Red Page
The Making of Australia
Bernhard Ringrose Wise, in The Making
of the Australian Commonwealth (Long-
mans, Green, per Sydney Book Club), bril-
liantly tells the story of Federation. His
book is dedicated “to the 71.9(35 electors of
N.S.W. who voted ‘Yes’ on June 3,189 ST
It is no histoire documentee that he gives
us, but the record of an eye-witness of, and
participator in, the pregnant events of the
critical period from 1889 to 3900. Begin-
ning with Parkes’s Tenterfield speech for
federation in the former year, Wise carries
the drama of the- fight for the Common-
wealth to its triumphant conclusion, and
brings the chief personalities in that great
contest vividly, before our eyes. Great
names figure in his cast of characters ; his
heroes are many; and all ready to succor
and defend the lone heroine, Australia, with
her qs yet unborn chee-ild. But the play
has its villain, a grotesque villain, “con-
sistent only in inconsistency.” a gibing,
sinister, genial FalstalT. playing in turn "the
part of open enemy, candid friend and en-
thusiatic supporter, contradicting himself in
each character with imperturbable serenity.”
And even the playwright finds it difficult
to probe to the inner heart of his villain.
"It was difficult to understand him at the
time.” he writes in his stage r directions.
“Posterity may find this impossible. He is
the enigma of the federal movement, who
played a pgrt which historians cannot
ignore, but (he explanation of which may
prove a formidable difficulty.”
But Nemesis was waiting for him—and,
dramatically, it waited till the fifth act.
Twice, in crises of the federal movement,-
its leader was sacrificed to faction, because
he urged a too exalted wisdom on an unappre-
ciative public. Mr. Barton lost his seat in
1894 for having dreamed that he could sway
a hostile Cabinet to the support of Federa-
tion; and, in the next year, Sir Henry
Parkes was driven out of public life, because
he counselled a truce to party warfare in the
interest of a higher patriotism. Each of
these events had turned to Mr. Reid’s advan-
tage, at a time when lie was still waiting
upon Providence ! Now. when he had done
finally with doubt and indecision, ironic
Fate was to deal him the same shrewd
blow !
Reid, as Premier of N.S.W.. would have
got the mandate to form the first Australian
Ministry ; but, with that prize almost within
his gras?>. he was hutled from office. But,
though Wjse rings down his curtain o” the
defeat of villainy and virtue triumphant,
life, even political life, is not like that. If
a villain is only sufficiently rotund and suf-
ficiently resilient, the harder you hurl him
down the higher he will bounce. This vil-
lain bounced so high that he landed in
London as High Commissioner.
Though dignified in treatment of his great
subject, Bernhard Wise is never dull, llis
comments are pointed: and bis attitude
will commend itself to all Good Australians.
Thus he sums his results :
Australia entered upon a new era of
political and material progress. It is too
early yet fully to appraise the greatness of
the change. The breaking down of provin-
cial barriers and the establishment of a pro-
tective policy have stimulated every in-
dustry, and a cycle of good seasons and high
prices has diffused and increased a general
prosperity. Provincialism, however, died
hard, and is struggling still in its death
throes : but the wider horizon of a national
life will never contract, and the responsibili-
ties of nationhood are being accepted with
courage and cheerfulness. If Parliament has
not fulfilled all expectations, it is neverthe-
less superior to the legislative bodies of the
States; and it must be remembered that its
usefulness has been impaired by unexpected
decisions of the High Court against the
validity of many of its most important meas-
ures. No reflecting person would return
willingly to the old provincial divisions. The
remedy for the defects of the Constitution
is to be found rather in the extension of
federal powers, -with an extension of local
government by subdivisions of the larger
"States. Only by this means can he secured
that “enlargement of the powers of self-
government of the people of Australia” which
was the declared object of the Constitution.

My Climbing Adventures in Four Con-
tinents. by Samuel Turner (Unwin. 55.), a
rather egotistical record by a climber who
perhaps has earned his egotism, has interest
for us in his graphic account of the first—
and likely to be the last—traverse made
across Mt. Cook. Turner regards the M.L.
heap of rock and ice as one of the most
difficult mountains in the world to climb—
and he has climbed nearly all. “It is a
question whether if it is possible to find a
mountain outside the Himalayas where
there is 10,000 feet of sheer climbing from
the valley. Mountains that are 18,000 feet
m most ranges in the world' are gradual
slopes. Most peaks can be walked up to
within 4000 or 5000 feet from the summit.”
Turner discreetly expresses his doubt
whether Zurbriggen really reached the top-
most of the three summits of Mt. Cook;
and the published statements of that famous
guide indicate that “some mistake had been
made.” The illustrations from photographs
are numerous and remarkable. It seems a
pity that Turner did not add Kosciusko to
his little list. He could have done it com-
fortably in a motor-car. ,
The Love-Leiters of [?]
Bronte
There are just four of them, and they have
just been handed to the British Museum by
the son of iTotessur lieger, at whose school
in Brussels Charlotte was employed as a
governess. Biographers have fought over
the question whether there was an "affair”
between the mairied professor and the girl
—he was the M. Paul Emanuel of Villette.
Evidence from her works has been adduced
to prove, and also to disprove, this piquant
allegation. The question is settled, not only
by the tenor of these letters, but by the pen-
cilled note made on the last one by the
highly respectable professor. Charlotte fell
in love with him ; but he gave her no encour-
agement. And the heart-broken Charlotte
sends him from England four letters at long
intervals, since “you have authorised me to
do so.” She complains that she cannot write :
her sight is too weak. Otherwise, “I should
write a book, and I should dedicate it to my
literature master—to the only master I ever
had —to you, Monsieur.” But he did not
replv and the love-sick girl writes again:
“Day and night I find neither rest nor peace.
If I sleen I am disturbed by tormenting
dreams, in which I see you, always severe,
always grave, always incensed agaiust me.
....If my master withdraws his friendship
from me entirely. I shall be altogether with-
out hope ; if he gives me a little—just a little
—I shall be satisfied—happy: I shall have a
reason for living on. for working. . . .1 have
a hidden consciousness that some people, cold
and common-sense, in reading it would say,
‘She is talking nonsense,’ I would avenge
myself on such persons in no other way than
by wishing them one single day of the tor-
ments which I have suffered for eight
months. We should then see if they would
not talk nonsense, t00....1 tell you frankly
that I have tried meanwhile to forget you.
. . . .Why cannot I have just as much friend-
ship for you. as you for me —neither more
nor less? Then should I be so tranquil, so
free—l could keep silence then for ten years
without an effort.”
And on the edge of this letter the pro-
fessor made some commonplace notes in
pencil. One of these notes was the name
and address of a shoemaker! If Charlotte
had but known, what a climax this might
have made for one of her novels !
Will Dyson satirises the modern English
Sawdasty School of Acting .
The Non-Eternal Feminine
“Everything around woman has altered.
It would be inconceivable that her aspira-
tions alone should remain unchanged and
immutable. The supreme, law of life is the
adaptation to the surrounding environment.
The economic situation of woman being
radically modified, she instinctively makes
her preparations for an effectual struggle
with the surrounding conditions.... Femin-
ism, with its political and social demands,
is, in short, only the unfolding of the in-
stinct of preservation which compels woman
conform to the altered conditions. Her
former mode of life has failed ; her entrance
into factories and the lessening of her op-
portunities for founding a family have
created for her other conditions of existence,
and we are witnessing their logical develop-
ment.... The limits of her requirements,
soonetvor later, will reach the extreme limits
of the rights of man....To prevent woman
from passing through the cycle of her evolu-
tion, it would have been necessary to enclose
society within immovable frames. The idea
appears paradoxical, but that of woman
fixed in a type or types created by the past
is no less absurd.... We believe the eternal
feminine immovable, while it is developing
incessantly before our eyes.... The woman
of our times is not identical with the woman
of the days of our ancestors, and probably
she will nipt resemble the woman who will
be born in'the 25th or the 30th century.”
Upon this thesis Jean Finot, in Problems
of the Sexes (Nutt), basis his loosely scien-
tific plea for feminism. “The ascent of
man,” he declares, “has been arrested by
the immobility of woman.” Her revolution,
when consummated, he calls “the most mag-
nificent social transformation that has been
realised since the fall of the Roman Em-
pire.” And he sees no signs of a barrier to
the complete triumph of feminism in France.
With justice he points out that civilisation
is slowly feminising the male, and that as
the modern man is aiming at qualities that
woman has long possessed, she will find
herself more and more justified in claiming
equality with the bloke who is trying to
borrow her virtues. Rut. against the bio-
logists, he asserts that creative genius and
intelligence have no sex, forgetting the
radical influence the sexual glands, from
infancy, have upon every portion df the
body and brain. And among the achieve-
ments of woman he makes this ridiculous
claim : “Gall try-hoe, a young girl of Sicyon,
was the first person to make a sketch in
pencil, representing her betrothed husband.
And this originated the art of drawing" !
Finot wastes a lot of space in disproving
the alleged inferiority of woman, since the
male and the female have different functions
to perform, and no status of comparison can
exist between, say. woman’s capacity for
maternity and man’s creative genius. But
iu a chapter dealing with passion the French
author draws some highly interesting con-
clusions. Thus, for instance, he finds that
the sexual disadvantages under which all
women suffer are balanced, in the economy
■f the forces of the two sexes, by the cor-
respondingly serious effect of sex-attraction
upon the male. The female spider murders
and eats her husband: the modern human
female is almost as implacable.
Will the New Woman be beautiful?
Finot. in an interesting survey of the sub-
ject of feminine beauty, stresses the fact
that feminine beauty resides not in the
female, but in the mind of the spectator of
the female. Ideals of beauty have shown a
constant change, chiefly enforced by the
work of great artists. Botticelli painted a
consumptive as his Florentine Venus, and
so imposed upon a whole period the worship
of sloping shoulders, a long neck, and a
narrow, sunken chest. “Thin or fully de-
veloped women, with expressive or vacant
looks, succeed each other in modern art.”
“The change in feminine education will
give us other incarnations in beauty. Woman
will undoubtedly he stronger and larger, hut
she will not on that account he less beauti-
ful. A few incomplete specimens of this
future beauty are already seen among the
flower of the “four hundred” of New York.
They afford for us. like certain profes-
sional beauties in Europe, types of charm,
tact, strength and intelligence. But their
beauty is not that which the chisels of
Greek sculptors have engraved on our mem-
ories !.... The brain of woman, containing
more Ideas, will change its form, and. thanks
to the law of co-ordination, will cause a
modification of all the facial angles ; her in-
telligence, strengthened by the contact with
life, will give to her countenance a different
expression; her body, thanks to physical
exercises, will become more harmonious;
her grace will be blended with strength.”
The beauty of woman will assuredly
chnnge—but the ideal of feminine beauty
will with equal pace be modified in the
mind of the man. But she must first emanci-
pate herself from the dictates of the un-
known gods who prescribe her fashions for
her. ‘fWoman is always beautiful in spite
of. not on account of. her costume
Woman must be really beautiful if she
has been able to resist the assaults of fashion
during so many ages: for not only does it
render her ugly, but it injures her health. .. .
Her dress, in an equal degree, prevents her
from becoming a mother, from nursing her
child, and from being strong, robust and
graceful.”
However, she is going to change all that.
She is going to change herself.
Will Dyson shows us the Dalians at Home ,
including Bernard Shaw.
The supremacy of Will Dyson in the ait
of caricature is evidenced by the reproduc-
tions from. The Daily Herald, the London
Labor paper, of a series of his Cartoons. It
is obvious that in this later work of his lie
has been much influenced by the German
school of Jugend and Simplicissimus : but
his -work is essentially his own. essentially
Australian. The two reproductions shown
here necessarily suffer from a reduction in
size.
The Jew
Alias wer writesln reference to your
interesting article (4/9/’lSt;) entitled “Obit
“ary— I The Jew,” 1 wish to draw your atten
tion to one point. u
“There seems every chance of a total
merging of the Jews into Christendom"
11ns is wrong. There is a possible— perhans
probable—merging of the Jews into the
Lmversal religion of the future, the religion
that Rationalism lias founded, the religion
with one dogma. “Live well, do well, think
well" without the idea of propitiating the
Divine power by doing so or of exciting his
wrath by not doing so.
Rationalistic thought has cleared off all
theories of Divine interference in human
affairs, and is a step towards the Utopian
period when men will do right because it is
right, and not from the ulterior motive that
they please God by doing so or the baser
one that he will punish them for their sins.
But in doing this. Rationalism has struck
deep blows into the roots of all religions
Islam, Christianity, Judaism have all suf-
fered. Census figures count for naught. A
man is counted as an Anglican, or a Catho-
lic, or a Methodist, if lie be born as such,
except he distinctly revokes his religion. He
is not a Jew except he has undergone cer-
tain rites. Melbourne has not seating ac-
commodation in its churches for 25 per
cent, of its Christian population. What
religion are the other 75 per cent.?
I only wish to prove the same cause
that is attacking Judaism is attacking all
religions with set forms and dogmas.
One last word and I am finished.
Dr. Ruppin says that with the abandon-
ment of Yiddish —a kind of Lingua Franca
—comes the death of Jewry. Not so. In
all parts of Jewry there has been a wonder-
ful revival in the use of Looshan Kodish
(pure Hebrew-) as a living language. This
language is spoken by more to-day than
ever-before in the whole life of Jewry. I
board two little Australian-born children in
Ballarat talking to each other in this lan-
guage. And note, this is about as far from
the hub of the nation as can be.

“The honor paid to Mr. Bridges,” says
London Times, in reference to the new poet-
laureate, “is an honor paid to poetry.” It
terms Bridges "a poet and an earnest stu-
dent and practitioner of the art and science
of poetry. lie has studied profoundly and
practically the origins, the values, the phil-
osophy of words, of rhythms, of metres.
Probably no man living knows more of these
matters than Mr. Bridges.” Also he has ex-
hibited “a lofty passion for poetry.. He can-
not be said to w r rite for the public ear”; but
that large and useful organ will receive from
his poems, “not only a rich gift of beauty,
but an example of special value in days
when poetry shows a tendency to run wild —
the example of work which looks back to the
old, while commanding the allegiance of the
new.” In other words, the poet-laureate’s
Pegasus is, as suggested by this Page, no
buck-jumper. The Atiien.eum speaks of
Bridges as a careful and dignified artist,
without much appeal to the general public.
John Bailey, in an article in the Quarterly,
advocating Bridges’s claim to the job. admits
that his actual poetic achievement does not
include any single poem of the first magnitude.
“He has done nothing which can be brought
into comparison, either for quantity or for
quality, with the principal works of the
great 19th Century poets. ...His is a some-
what small voice of occasional intermittent
utterance, incapable of the sustained flights
of song which belong to men of higher and
more continuous inspiration. No sane ad-
miration of his work w-ill deny that it is that
of a minor poet. But within these limits it
may be said to be both fairly abundant and
almost invariably of fine quality.” Yet his
chief recommendation to his critic is that he
is so entirely English, so essentially an
untravelled and untroubled Islander. “No
poet, perhaps not even Wordsworth or
Tennyson or Cowper, has done more to fill
English fields and lanes with poefr.v.” But
even Bailey, trying hard not to damn with
faint praise, admits that the only part of
Bridges’s work that matters is the compara-
tively snfall section of lyrics. The' New
Age says that Bridges’s influence upon the
post must be for the worse, “for the affected
simplicity of his verse belongs amongst the
foolish things that confound the wise.
1 lowever, he is good enough for his day.”
The July Quarterly has an able and
stimulating article by W. L. Courtney upon
“Dramatic Construction and the Need for a
New Technique John Bailey analyses the
poetry of the new poet-laureate: and the
Bengali mystic. Ranindranth Tagore, whose
Song Offerings were noted here, is the sub-
ject of an informative article.

The thoroughbred lines of a Remington are

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Down the Dim Years.
Clean running wave and sunward soaring
flower,
The great.hot sky, the colors of the wood
troubled with shadow, and the sudden
shower
Of heavenly fire across the solitude
Ut some green praying-place with steps of
stone
As sorrowfully white as winter moon,
thin, far calling of a trumpet blown
lo dead men hunting in the afternoon.
The pink-faced boy who kissed the crying
witch
Upon her wet, flat mouth, and wailing
went
Down into Hell. .. .while she, a snuffling
bitch
This way and that, bewrayed the tasselled
bent
Howling the pain of Doom.
The quiet Christ
Leading His children surely through the
dark
By the clear lantern of his wounds, to
tryst
W ith God, of men their refuge and their ark.
N.s.w.
HUGH McCRAE.
A Day-Dream.
Ono morn, where fresh sun-buds
Danced o’er the glad grass
And birds sang. I waited
For True-Love to pass.
The blue sky was smiling
With lips cool as wine;
I dreamed in half-slumber
That True-Love kissed mine!
In sweet lands of wonder,
Impatient; with fate,
I waited, I waited ;
But True-Love was late !
Night came with star-lanterns
And pitched her black tents
’Neath xhe-oak and gum tree....
How True-Love torments !
Stars peered through blue curtains,
Winds moaned, and soft dew
Closed tired eyes of sun-buds—
Sweet, True-Love’s, and blue.
The fairies came peeping
And rustling the grass ;
I thought that on tiptoe
’Twas True-Love did pass!
I dreamed till a shadow
Bowed down from the skies
And touched me....’twas lustrous
As True-Love’s sweet eyes.
Within, a white ladye,
Grief wreathed on her head.
Moaned. “Birds have ceased singing,
Sweet True-Love is dead !”
“The glad eyes of sunbuds
Shall open no more ;
The court that delighted
Queen True-Love is o’er!”
My heart was nigh bursting.
So heavy it weighed :
'Mid clover-spread grasses
Dead True-Love was laid.
I lay down beside her.
And weeping did crave:
“Death, kiss me and leave me
On True-Love’s fresh grave.”
Then Night struck her black tents.
Watch-lights died away.
Leaves stirred and I fancied
’Twas True-Love at play.
Dawn came with rose-banners,
Buds perfumed the air ;
The green grass wept softly.
For True-Love lay there !
At length came a shadow
’Twixt me and the skies....
I smiled in the love-light
Of True-Love’s sweet eyes!
Dav-dreams! they are nothing
Now True-Love is nigh!
But —oh, the world’s heart-ache
If True-Love should die !
N.S.W.
W. M. WHITNEY.
A Moral Toiler.
I love to wander round o’ days,
With joy I fairly boil
To see long lines of clanking drays
Removing yards of soil,
What time swart men of heft and garth
With crowbars, spades and picks
Are hitting good old mother earth
Most shrewd resounding licks.
To me the rip-saw’s honest voice—
The anvil’s merry ring—
Is sweeter than when birds, rejoice
With chirrupings of spring.
I thrill to hear steam sirens blare,
And ah ! what hymns of Trade
Resound from those brave fact’ries where
Tin canisters are made!
The gas men delving in the street —
Train fettlers juggling rails
Provide for me a daily treat,
Though it the thought entails
That I to watch must be content.
I’d join the bless&d band,
But, ah, the pick’s an instrument
I do not understand.
I am not of that brave array
Of men who handle tools,
But I can watch them day by day—
My zest for that ne’er cools.
And oft the thought occurs to me,
When home to rest I’ve gone,
That p’raps my moral sympathy
As ’twere, has cheered them on.
JNf.S.W.

THE SIDE-STEPPING OF JOE.
AUSTRALIA: “I want to know, Joe, what you propose doing with any recommendations for increased Protection that
may be made by the Inter-State Commission?”
JOE COOK: “I shall, of course, treat all classes alike, and submit any insignificant detail of that kind to my
esteemed friend here.”

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(Iu Advance, Only.)
Per Annum . . .. ..£l6O
Half-yearly .. .. .. 0 13 6
Quarterly .. . . . . .. 0 7 0
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty -
Six Shillings Sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
n o\v to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of
MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 25, 1913.
A LARGER OUTLOOK FOR NEW
SOUTH WALES.
On a certain recent day Allen Taylor, a
member of the N.S.W. Upper House, spoke
to that rather tired and aged institution
about the opening up of some of
N.S.W.’s neglected deep-water ports for
over-sea commerce. He was seized by the
simple, obvious idea that it is costly and
absurd to drag exports at great cost 300
miles by rail to a port, when there is a good
port 40 or 60 or 100 miles away, which
only wants a scrap of line and a wharf or
two to start it on the way to being a great
commercial centre. And the ex-Gaud Mayor
of Sydney had right and common sense and
justification behind him. He was even
threatened with brains. Presumably I RO-
vidence —which isn’t wholly an ass, what-
ever the Sydney Tory press may think about
if; —created such places as Port Stephens.
Jervis Bay and Twofold Bay for use, just
as much as it did Sydney harbor. Seeing
that the county which contains Sydney har-
bor —the place that has all the railway com-
munication that is going—has over 458
people to the square, mile (according to the
census of 1911), while the much more fer-
tile county which contains the equally good
harbor of Port Stephens has less than seven
people to the square mile, and considering
that there is no visible reason for the dis-
crepancy save that Sydney has denied Port
Stephens railway communication and other
facilities, it really seems that Sydney is
carrying the congestion policy too far. Also
it seems that it is carrying a great deal too
far the idea that Providence is an ass.
* * * *
Of course the proposition of Allen
Taylor, M.L.C., roused the Sydney Tory
press to excitement. The S. M. Herald
was angry. The Sydney Telegraph was
furious. The Evening News, which is the
last and final embodiment of the Congestion
tradition, was very mad indeed. Some ex-
tracts from the News’s complaint follow: —
The making of a port is an endless job, and though
perhaps three millions or so would “open” a couple
of harbors like Jervis Bay and Port Stephens, there
would be a heavy annual outlay chargeable to capi-
tal, and also a weighty running cost. Dredging,
lighting, depreciation, repairs, new public buildings
and a big salary list would have to be provided for,
and therefore it is fair and reasonable to ask the
supporters of the scheme that these projects would
pay.
It seems to be forgotten that decentralisation was
tried at Twofold Bay, Jervis Bay and Port Stephens
long ago, in the days when cargo, wool, tallow and
hides could be put aboard the old wooden ships and
sent direct to the Old Country. But to-day the task
is ten thousand times more difficult, because Sydney,
with its huge population, is such a market that it
can draw to itself all produce that seeks early sale.
It is possible to imagine that the new ports would
be useful for the export trade. But take wool for
instance. Does anyone suppose that wool-growers
will turn aside to Port Stephens or Jervis Bay when,
by trucking their bales a comparatively few miles
further, they would have the benefit of the vast
organisation of the Sydney wool market. The same
thing applies to most other products, but in a far
greater degree than to wool, for the population of
Sydney must be supplied, and the producer would
like the option of selling his goods either in the
local or the foreign markets. It is, therefore, in-
cumbent on the advocates of decentralisation to give
some proof of the amount of business that these new
ports would do. And that business, it must be
observed, should carry its share of the cost of the
facilities which would be needed to deal with it.
The fallacy underlying much of the arguments ad-
vanced in favor of decentralisation is that a shorter
line of railway must necessarily mean cheaper
freights.
And much more to the same effect.
**■ * *
The Tory papers, whose main interests
lie in Sydney, hate the idea of a penn’orth
of trade going anywhere except to their own
doorsteps. So they set up the theory that a
big commercial and manufacturing city is
a dead loss to the community—at least,
such a city on Sydney harbor is a great
profit, but a similar city on Port Stephens
or .Tervis Bay or Twofold Bay would neces-
sarily be a great deficit and a bad debt. In
fact, there would be no cities at these places,
for railway facilities and wharves and deep
water and plenty of building space don’t
make cities. The one thing that makes a
city, so far as N.S.W. is concerned, is the
fact of it being Sydney. Therefore the
News asks wildly if people would dream of
sending their wool to Port Stephens or
.Tervis Bay when Sydney was at hand, being
quite unable to realise that new Sydneys
would arise, to the vast profit of N.S.W.,
if the chance was permitted them. Yet
almost in the same breath it admits that in
the days when chances were equal, places
like Port Stephens, Jervis Bay and Two-
fold Bay were useful for oversea commerce.
That was in the time of small ships and
road traffic. Then ocean-going vessels
traded to and from the now-deceased ports
of the Ma State, and these outlets held their
own. Any port with a road and a wharf was as
good as any other port. But when road-
haulage was superseded by railway haulage,
and Sydney was the only place to which
railway haulage was permitted, the other
places naturally died. If Port Stephens or
Jervis Bay had got the exclusive railway
advantages, then Sydney would have died
instead.
As matters stand, the county of
Cumberland, with its outlet at Sydney har-
bor, and abundant railway communication
therewith, has over 458 people to the square
mile. If the county of St. Vincent had
equally good railway communication with
the equally serviceable harbor of Jervis Bay
it might have 458 people to the square mile,
whereas now it has less than five. If the
county of Gloucester had similar railway
facilities for trading with the similarly good
harbor of Port Stephens it might have 458
people to the square mile, but at present it
has less than seven. If the county of Auck-
land had the same railway facilities for
dealing through Twofold Bay as Cumber-
land has for dealing through Sydney, it
might have 458 people to the square mile,
but unfortunately it has less than six. In
two of these three counties the population
is decreasing. Their harbors are shut up
for lack of railway connections, these con-
nections being denied to them through the
miserable jealousy of Sydney, and so far
as commerce goes they might as well be in
the middle of Africa. Therefore there are
hardly any people alongside three of the
best outlets in the State to do business with
Sydney. There is hardly anybody in these
three counties to buy the Evening News,
or to advertise in it for a lost dog, and the
News doesn’t seem able to understand what
a chance it is missing. If the information
went out to the world that three new Syd-
neys were starting, with just as good pros-
pects as the old original Sydney possessed,
a million British emigrants, all quite as
good as the old lot, could be got to fill them.
And then the doddering Evening News
might start three new offices, and three new
papers, and make a stupendous fortune, and
be a worse public disaster than it is now. On
the other hand, it might acquire a larger out-
look and get something dimly resembling a
mind. The things it is most in want of are
an outlook and a mind.
A FEW REMARKS ON GOATS, ASSES
AND THE DEAD HAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Ministry had effected one act of restoration—-
it had restored, with all its signs and portents, the
mace, that sign and portent of the Government of
ancient days, the Government of the fourteenth cen-
tury. (Laughter.)— Anstey, M.H.R.
I don’t mind kings and dukes and things;
T don’t mind wigs or maces j
I don’t mind crowns or robes or gowns
Or ruffles, swords or laces —
But what I do object to, and some others
more than I,
Are the mad old, bad old practices these
baubles signify.
Good friends, brother Australians and fel-
low voters;
I think that you will agree with me that
few of us are doters
Upon the customs, practices, fooleries and
tommyrotics of the mouldy past;
Nor are we apt to cast
A reverent eye behindward upon ancient
precedent;
Nor do we consent
To let the cold, clammy and usually mud-
dling Dead Hand
Control the destinies of this our native land.
Nay, rather do we stand
Tiptoe upon the summit of the Present,
peering out,
With faces eager and expectant eyes, into
the mystic Future. Have you a doubt
That in Progress, Business-like Procedure,
Common-sense Habit, and Up-to-Date
Method we are all earnest believers?
Is it not so?. . ..
Well, I don’t know
So much about it. ’Twere easy to prove,
good friends, that we are, in the lump,
followers of Make-Believe, triflers with
; Humbug and inane self-deceivers.
’Twere easy to prove that our ass-like at-
r tribute indeed surpasses
That of innumerable and intensely asinine
asses.
And here, good friends, I extend to all of
you my blessin’,
And conclude, amidst great applause, the
first lesson.
Secondly, my brothers, —
Right-thinking persons, men-in-the-street,
common-sense individuals, and people
who call a spade a spade, and others —
There are full many of us who deeply de-
plore
The use or display of those gauds, decora-
tions, baubles and trappings that be-
long to the unpractical, superstitious
and quite unfashionable days of yore.
We deride, for instance, the notion that
the caudal appendage of a deceased
horse
Perched upon the cranium of an erudite
justice can add to his dignity or give
to his remarks more force.
In short, we class as mere bunkum, bosh,
flapdoodle and other sludge
The contention that the hind end of a horse
can in any way assist the fore end of a
judge.
The wig, the gown, the staff, the rod. the
mace.
We regard as obsolete and entirely out of
place.
If there is one thing more than another upon
which we pride ourselves it is, I sup-
pose,
The fact that we scorn to wear grandpa’s
old-fashioned clothes.
The poor old gentleman’s pantaloons, his
shirts, his cravat, his fob-chain, his
frill-whiskers are all anathema to us.
Good friends, why all this fuss?
Why waste all this precious energy in de-
nouncing the wig, the gown, the mace?
They may be, in a sense out of place ;
Yet, why should these things shock you?
Believe me, they are perfectly innocu-
Ous, and furthermore, friends,
They serve their ends ;
For why deny these toys
To that large, mentally-bogged, and much
misunderstood class of elderly girls and
boys
Whose state demands some sign or symbol
To push an idea or a principle into their
heads, even as the thimble
Thrusts the needle into the cloth?
Then why so wrath?
Heed ye, good friends, the parable of the
beam and the mote.
Nay, I crave your pardon, but I have known
a not particularly intelligent goat
To view materially essential matters with a
more discerning eye; to possess, so to
speak, more innate perspicacity
Than you—that is to say, us. Nay, gasp
not at the seeming audacity
Of these few remarks ; for perfect perspicu-
ity
.Attends them, and I like not ambiguity.
As thinking machines the ass, the goat, good
people, are preferable; at least, so it
appears.
And here, the ending of my second lesson is
attended by your deafening and appre-
ciative cheers.
My worthy friends, ye who scorn to wear
poor grandpa’s clothes,
Get down from your pedestals, O ye modern
intellectual giants ; let each decline his
scornful and uptilted nose.
Deride, would ye, grandpa’s ancient mace?
Abolish it, would ye, and hunt it off the
place ?
What’s the matter with it? It’s not eating
anything, is it?
And it might prove handy if a masked bur-
glar, or a Trust or a mad dog paid the
House a visit.
Gird, would ye, at grandpa’s wig, at his
gown trimmed with the overcoats of
late lamented rabbits?
But, Oh ! my up-to-date brothers, what have
ye to say about grandpa’s and great-
grandpa’s and great-great-grandpa’s
ridiculous customs, absurd precedents,
inane systems and obsolete habits?
What about that musty, dusty, mouldy, mil-
dewed, hoary, Tory, injurious, time-
wasting, insane, inane, self-ridiculed,
unwieldy and utterly unprofitable sys-
tem of Party Government? Great-
great-great-great-grandpa’s cherished
System, good friends!
Does it serve our modern ends?
Or is it, think you, obsolete and absurd?
I pause for a reply. . . .What! Not a word?
Do -I hear you raving to have it abolished?
Yearn ye to see this thing demolished?
Go to the ass, ye dullards ! tie doesn’t eat
mouldy sawdust when there’s good hay
about.
And here, kind friends, I pass to “fourthly,”
flattered by your encouraging shout.
Friends, countrymen and fellow-voters of
this fair land,
All ye smart, up-to-date people who scorn
dear grandpa’s raiment, are you feeling
his dead hand?
Think ye that ancient fist should interfere
so in the vital affairs of to-day?
Or are ye so apathetic that you don’t care
a tuppenny curse either way?
’Tis cheap and easy to scoff at grandpa’s
gauds and trappings and to the Devil
send ’em ;
But have ye ever seriously considered such
things as elected Ministries or the
Initiative and Referendum?
Not you! -You shirk, good friend, you shirk
That means Work!
Friends, I am done. . . .1 know not what yr
intend to do about it, and I haven’t
much hope ; but, for my part,
I say unto ye, in a spirit of true brotherly
love, and with my hand upon my heart
That I have enjoyed the acquaintance of
asses who were never fooled by musty
precedent. Aye, and intelligent
Who scorned the jam-tin diet of their fore-
bears when there was good grass about
- —but then had no votes.
And what is a goat without a vote?
VICTORIA.
DEN.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Liberalism, Loyalty and the Beef
Trust.
According to a cable which was published
(10/9/’l3) by the Sydney Telegraph:—
In the House of Commons, the Colonial Secretary
has intimated that he would ask the Federal Gov-
ernment for information regarding the alleged inten-
tion of American firms to make a monopoly of the
meat supply from Australasia to the United King-
dom.
The Bulletin first drew attention to the
designs of the American Beef Trust (which
is to say the firms of Swift & Co., Armour
& Co., Cudahy & Co., and Morris & Co.)
upon the Australasian meat trade about
two years ago. There were shrieks of de-
rision from the ranks of Toryism. This
paper has reiterated at intervals ever since
the view that the Beef Trust was out to
corner the local meat trade, just as it has
cornered the trade of Argentina. Liberalism
remained unconvinced; or at any rate it
said it was unconvinced. Its rallying-cry
during both Referenda campaigns was that
there were no objectionable trusts in Aus-
tralia. Platform allusions to the “Trust
bogey” were always sure of a cheer and
much laughter in any well-conducted
church-going suburb in those days. Little
by little the conviction grew in the public
mind that there was something fallacious
about the No-Trust gag, just as the con-
viction had already grown that the Anti-
Defence cry was all wrong. Cable after
cable came from London telling of shrink-
ages in the world’s meat supplies (or the
supplies made available by the Beef Trust),
and of the perturbation of thinking British
people at the position. As a matter of fact,
a realisation of the situation didn’t call for
much capacity for thought. Britain pro-
duces very little meat, and its inhabitants
are habitual meat-eaters —that is to say,
the inhabitants who can afford it are. For
the imported meat supply of the nation to
come under the control of a United States
combine would be an unspeakable calam-
itv. That calamity has now come
very near. In a footnote to one of its
cables, a week or so ago, the Sydney Tele-
graph, which used to be the loudest asseve-
rator of the yarn that there was no such
thing as an injurious trust in Australia,
had the following to say :
There appears to be no question about the accu-
racy of the oft-repeated statement that the American
Beef Trust has secured control of the major propor-
tion of the world’s meat supplies. The Argentine
and Australian meat exports alone seem to show in-
creases, when compared with the figures for pre-
vious years. Certainly the Argentine and Austra-
lasia are the two centres of supply which have not
yet passed directly under the entire control of the
Meat Trust. On the other hand, the Trust has im-
mense interests in Argentina, and is admitted to
have acquired important interests in Queensland.
Liberalism claims to be, politically, the
“loyal” section of the Commonwealth. It
is great on the inexpensive ceremony of
saluting the flag. It does not mind how
much cheap energy it expends while singing
the National Anthem. Strictly economical
demonstrations of devotion to the Mother
Land, such as refusing to sit on the same
bench as a J.P. who has mentioned that it
is possible for a British King to
be a fool, are a matter of habit
with members of this party. An
opportunity of offering the Mother Country
something more than service by means of
mummery now presents itself; and it will
be interesting to see how Australia’s alleged
loyalists rise to the occasion. The Beef
Trust can never be collared so long as
Queensland —where, as the Telegraph
mentions, the Trust has “acquired im-
portant interests”—or any other State re-
tains such complete control over its internal
trade and commerce as to be able to bid
defiance to Federal anti-Trust legislation.
The objection to so amending the Constitu-
tion as to give the Australian Parliament
power to pass an effective anti-Trust Act
lies, so far as Liberalism is concerned, in
the fact that any such change will amount
to an interference with State Rights. The
problem that obtrudes itself therefore is:
Is the doctrine of State Rights or the right
of the United Kingdom to avoid being black-
mailed in connection with an important
article of diet, the more important? The
handling of it will constitute a more prac-
tical test of genuine loyalty than the cele-
bration >f a whole aeon of Empire Days.
Sydney’s Small-pox.
From a person who should know, with a
new argument in favor of compulsory vac-
cination to wind up with: —
“Good morning, Mrs. Giles,” says. Sister. “By
the way, how long have you been here?”
“Goin’ on three weeks, me dear,” mumbles
Granny, who usually leaks into the landscape when
any doctor or charge-nurse appears.
“'Three weeks! Why, its nine weeks on Mon-
day,” announces Sister, looking up from her note-
book. “Come up for inspection to-morrow.”
Granny swears into her knitting, and says what
she thinks of hussies who interfere.
Many merely walk through the hospital enclosure
on their way to the convalescent area. And yet
they are kept six and seven and more weeks in
quarantine, for no better reason than that they have
a little roughened skin about the legs and fore-
arms. In dozens of cases this roughness has been
caused by the use of hard, cold water or by tn
piercing winds that frequently scarify the More
Head station. . ~

abunclant. To those of us who have been in conta

o far I have not heard of any examination of
heads ’which in many cases are loaded with dand-
ruff and small scabs, and—and other things.
1 sphere j s a humorous side, too. One historic case
is that of a man, one of the undesirables, who, after
evading the doctor for several weeks, at last was
dragged up for inspection. He got 12 days added
to his loafing-lease by producing what was after-
wards alleged to be a mosquito bite, slightly
scratched.
In the matter of contacts one wonders exceedingly.
Mothers with the disease are sent in accompanied
hv three and four children as contacts, many of
these children being quite old enough to be left
with friends or relations, as they would be if their
parent were in general hospital. The husbands are
let loose on the public.
The social aspect has been considerably improved,
though, as one of the police pungently remarked:
«\Ve will never get Respectability till all the
women are at one end of the place and the men at
the other.” The single girls are housed in Lyne’s
Building, which is strictly out of bounds to the men.
As to fumigation, cases inspected and passed for
discharge are rushed off by the first launch leaving,
which gives very little time for a medicated bath
and the fumigation of personal gear. The bath is,
in fact, frequently evaded.
One hears that the plague outbreak cost £140,000
in actual cash. This small-pox epidemic, at the
present pace, will run into much more. The cost of
maintenance per head is so tremendous as to be in
fact scandalous. The treatment of pustules with oil
and ointments and the rubbing-off of roughened skin
with pumice-stone is left to the convalescent patients
themselves. If they really want to get away thev
work at their spots. But there are scores who don’t
want to get away. By complete and daily medical
supervision, the average length of the quarantining
might be reduced from a good many weeks to 21
scarlet fever, or tvphoid, or any other con-
tagious disease that any of us may chance to con-
tract. small-pox can be reduced to a name bv pre-
ventive vaccination. If people contract the di=ea«e,
the State is in duty bound to segregate them. Under
the most careful management, that means expense:
under management that isn’t as careful as it should
he it means tremendous expense. This is where the
State comes in. It must pay the piper. Then surelv
it has the right to call the tune. And the first note
in that tune is, of course, Vaccination.
--
An Anti-Decentralisation Witness Who
Fails Painfully to Carry Conviction.
The chilly nerve with which a partisan
newspaper is capable of pulling the legs of
its readers beats that “two o’clock in the
morning courage,” which Wellington ac-
claimed, right out of sight. The latest
example is afforded by the Sydney Tele-
gkapii’s handling of Allen Taylor’s decen-
tralisation proposals in the N. S. Wales
Legislative Council. Allen Taylor took
the point that Port Stephens, Jervis Bay and
similar great natural harbors should be
opened up to trade. Sydney and Newcastle
were both equally scandalised at a scheme
which would deprive them of business, how-
ever much it benefited the State at large.
The Telegraph was concerned to show that
opposition to Taylor’s proposal was in no
wise biased; that local interests were not
involved in the matter; that all men of
intelligence were ranged against it, even
though it might mean profit to them indi-
vidually. And this was the best that the
deceptive sheet could do :
Touching upon that portion of the scheme which
concerns the conversion of Port Stephens into a
serviceable port, Mr. Earp, although he represents
large coal-mining and railroad interests on the South
Maitland coalfields, declared that the project would
be nothing more than a “wicked and wanton waste
—decentralisation run mad.”
One would think, to read that, that Mr.
Earp was some splendidly altruistic patriot
who, reckless of his “large coal mining and
railroad interests on the South Maitland
coalfields,” had thrust aside the question of
personal aggrandisement and come out with
a clarion shout —not to say a howl —on be-
half of the public weal. Wha.t are the
facts? George Earp, M.L.C., is a New-
castle storekeeper of sorts. He is also a
Newcastle property owner. Practically all
his interests are in Newcastle. The Greta
mine, in which he is a shareholder, will not
be affected much one way or the other, by
the opening up of Port Stephens. _ But his
Newcastle interests —his store, his house
property and so forth —stand to be smitten
most violently by any Government action
tending to create a new city on the shores
of the magnificent, though at present com-
mercially dead, Northern port. A cold, grey
truth, which is known to most Australians
of intelligence to-day is that Newcastle,
regarded as a modern port, has no real right
to live. It is a bar-harbor, and a bar-harbor, in
this epoch of big ships, is an anomaly. And,
more than that, it is an exceptionally rotten
bar-harbor; a ridiculously false-alarm
haven of refuge, where ships were unable to
refuge only last winter, on account of the
wind blowing the wrong way, or the tide
being unfavorable, or the channel having
fallen down the coal hole, or something
similar. Far be it from this kindly and
unsuspicious family journal to suggest that
Mr. Earp was speaking in a disingenuous
spirit when he proclaimed, last week, that
the conversion of Port Stephens into a ser-
viceable port would represent “decentralisa-
tion run mad.” Still less would it dream of
asserting that his personal interests in any
way colored his views on the matter. How-
ever, the fact remains that he is an unsatis-
factory witness on the question of decen-
tralisation —just as a bookmaker would be
a poor witness on a question involving the
extirpation of racing, and as a parson would
be the same sort of witness on the issue ol
whether or no religion should be utterly
abolished. The newspaper that calls a man
situated as Mr. Earp is to show that 1 ort
Stephens ought to be allowed to remain m
its aboriginal state of deadness, is capable
of calling a professional politician to give
an impartial expression of opinion on the
advisability of abolishing payment ot mem-
bers, Works Committee billets, and free rail-
way passes.
Where the Landlord Conies In.
In a report just issued, Statistician
Knibbs heaved another clod at the already
bruised and disorganised Fat Party which
claims that, notwithstanding the increase in
wages, the worker is no better off than he
was when he had to toil for ss. a day or
thereabouts. Knibbs’s report summarises
mean rents, wages and purchasing P ( >y pr
of the sovereign, as far as concerns Mei-
bourne, in this fashion :
Rent.—A householder paving £5O a year in 1911
paid £37 16s. in 1901 and £52 7s. in 1912 — in-
crease, 1,0 per cent. .
Purchasing power of £1. —-That which cost 1/s. 3u.
in 1901 cost 18s. lOd. in 1911 and 20s. lid. in
1912— increase in cost of goods generally, 21 per
cent.
Groceries and Food. —That which cost £5l 12s.
in 1901 cost £57 16s. in 1911 and £5B 10s. in
1912 —increase in cost of food articlts, V, per cent.
Those figures look unpleasant, not to say
alarming, but Mr. Knibbs reassures the
citizens:—
Average wage-earner’s income in 1901: £202 ;
in 1912, £263 10s.
Put iu another way, for each 20s. lid. the
wage-earner gets now, he got only 16s. in
1* For the things that now cost him
-Us. lid. he then had to pay 17s. 3d.; so he
is nearly Is. 3d. in the £1 better off. And,
as the Savings Bank figures prove, the aver-
age householder is doing precisely what the
I at Man told him he ought to do, and is
thriftily banking most of the 15 pence he
is to the good. But there is one other point.
Whereas the cost of food stuffs has increased
by 14 per cent., and of goods generally by
-1 per cent., and notwithstanding that the
average wage-earner receives 31 per cent,
more and is on the whole 8 per cent, better
off, the landlord is advantaged no less than
40 per cent. —the landlord who takes no
more risk and does no harder toil than be-
fore. And while the cold, statistical report
nroves him the colossal cormorant of the
whole bunch, nobody makes half so much
noise about the “rapacity” and “greed” and
“unreasonableness” of the working-man—
the working-man who, for all his wages
boards and strikes, has still managed to
collar 8 per cent, less than the horrified
cormorant has got away with himself.
Concernins a Measure that has been
Badly Needed for Fourteen Years.
It was announced the other day that —
At the conference of the delegates from the various
Chambers of Commerce of Australia a resolution with
reference to uniform company law was adopted.
And so the futile old business of ploughing
the commercial sands, and pulling the collec-
tive leg of trade, and wasting the valuable
time and money of the community goes on.
It is an anomalous circumstance that the
Australian Parliament, which can take steps
to prevent the community from being infected
with small-pox, has no power whatever to
nrotect it from the ravages of the fraudulent
company promoter. Commercial delegates
may meet and blither and pass resolutions,
and politicians may make the usual glozing
promises, but the fact remains that as long
as the Australian Parliament lacks the power
to interfere with the internal trade and com-
merce of individual States it can no more
present the Commonwealth with a uniform
comnany law than it can provide the New
Protection or a valid Trade Marks Act. And
this is a reform that Australia needs very
badly indeed. It is not alone the prominent
members of the business community who are
suffering from the lack of a stringent
measure that will insist on the same proce-
dure as regards, for example, company-pro-
moting in every State; its absence renders
every unit of the population liable to finan-
cial loss. The most clamant example of the lot
is N. S. Wales —-the State which turned down
the Referenda proposals by the largest
majority. N. S. Wales has one of the laxest
set of company laws on record. They could
not be more favorable to commercial fraud
had they been drafted by habitual robbers
of the widow and orphan. As a natural re-
sult, the Mother State has for years been the
pleasaunce of bogus company promoters.
Hardly a week passes without the wooden-
headed N.S.W. investor being hit full in the
midriff: by some astute swindler or other.
Owing to the fact that there is no obligation
on the promoters of a N. S. Wales company
to set out in their prospectus a detailed and
accurate account of the position, the most
astonishing ramps are possible. The writer
knows of a case, for example, where a syn-
dicate bought a quantity of land, allegedly
suitable for residential purposes, at £5 per
acre. It immediately sold the property to
another syndicate for £5O an acre. That
syndicate re-sold at £5OO an acre; and the
last syndicate lumbered it on to the suffering
public at £lOOO an acre. Nothing during all
this time had occurred to make the property
worth a farthing more than the £5. per acre
for which its owner had been delighted to
let it go in the first instance. The public
which bought at £lOOO per acre were not in-
formed by the promoters that the price, not
long since, had been £5 per acre. Nor was
it disclosed that the allegedly different syndi-
cates were in reality the same old syndicate,
to a great extent; and that though cheques
for impressive sums had passed between the
parties, the fatal formality of clearing the
documents had not been gone through. The
same sort of thing is occurring every day in
connection with mining, commercial and a
host of other promotions. It needs no demon-
stration that a change is called for. One
way in which some measure of reform might
be achieved would be by the States coming
together and agreeing to pass simultaneously
six Company Acts in line (say) with the
Victorian one, which is fairly satisfactory in
most respects. But the experience of years
goes to show that the States never will do
this. The only really effective change will
be made when the Australian Parliament is
enabled to pass one Company Act for the
whole of Australia. The worlds model, in
this line, is the English Company Act of
1900 It was a powerful measure when it
was first framed. It has been amended three
times since then, with the effect always of
making the path of the commercial brigand
and spieler more and more unprofitable and
perilous. The introduction of such an Act
to the Commonwealth would save the class
of investor who most needs protection un-
numbered thousands a year. It is a point
that the electors, and more especially the
N. S. Wales electors, should bear in mind
next time they are called on to vote on a
constitutional amendment issue.
In Camera.
The recent Privy Council decision, that no
divorce cases can be legally tried in a pri-
vate and confidential manner will con-
firm a good many people in their opinion
that the law is a hass. .On the face ot the
matter there is no necessity why any divorce
suits should be heard in nublic, and cer-
tainly there is no reason why all spicy evi-
dence should not bo kept out ot the news-
papers bv order of the Court. Divorce cases
have only a scandalous interest for the
world at iarge. No question of public policy
is involved in the publication or suppression
of what the chief witness for the petitioner
saw when he, or she, peeped thiough the
keyhole A jury—with a Judge to order a
new trial if the verdict looks dishonest-
should be as good a guarantee of a fair trial
of the respondent and co-re. as the world can
desire When the divorce suit is a friendly
one, and both parties want a quiet hearing
by a Judge only, where is the objection to a
divorce being as private as a marriage is
permitted to be? The world accepts an
allegation of Mrs. Brown’s marriage on the
strength of an advt. or weaker evidence than
that, and would put as much faith in the
advt. of her divorce, published among Law
Notices for the month. As regards how
many times she went week-ending with
Lothario Jones the world had better be
kept in ignorance. People talk too much
about other people’s domestic concerns as it
is. The respectable Law, as far as one can
see at a glance, has no reason for hanging
the whited sepulchre of any non-criminal
person on a telegraph wire for daws to peck
at. (All letters commenting on the mixed
character of this metaphor will be firmly
dropped in the waste-paper basket.) The
publicity given to divorce is no preventive
of misconduct, but it tends to deter the
wronged party—especially when the
aggrieved person is a woman —from apply-
ing for the dissolution of an unhappy mar-
riage. It is the innocent sufferer who in
many cases shrinks from the open scandal
of a divorce suit. Where there is no mis-
demeanor to be exposed, but merely a
married couple’s secret to be disclosed —as
in the case of Mrs. John Buskin’s divorce
—the Law hitherto has considered the feel-
ings of both parties. The Privy Council’s
decision would make the Law an inconsider-
ate brute.
Britain’s Poverty.
Dear Bulletin, —J. Ellis Barker, in the
August Fortnightly, discusses Britain’s
poverty and its causes. He boldly disputes
the assertion that Britain is the richest
country in the world. About last June the
British Board of Trade issued the report of
its first “Census of Production for 1907,”
and a little later appeared the United States
“Census of Production for 1909.” The writer
remarks bn the tables given in these two
reports:—
Careful consideration of the tables given shows
that, measured by value, the American industries
produce, as a rule, not a little less than twice as
much, but from three times to five times as much
as does this country. . . .If we now compare the num-
ber of wage-earners occupied in the industries of the
two countries which have been enumerated in the
tables, we find that the U.S. industries produce from
three to five times as much as the corresponding
British industries, although the number of their
workers is only slightly larger, and is sometimes
smaller than the number of the workers employed
in this country. For instance, in the United King-
dom 3865 workers produce £862,000 worth of
matches, while in the United States 3631 workers
produce £2,270,600 worth of matches.
The individual worker produces £223 worth
of matches on an average in Britain, and
£625 worth in the States. On the same
basis of calculation, the individual worker
in Britain makes £l7l worth of boots and
shoes: in the U.S. he makes £516 worth.
There is no secret about the cause of the
advantage the worker in the U.S. has over
the one in Britain. It is in the use of labor-
saving machinery. In Britain the boot and
shoe industry uses 172 horse-power of
machinery per 1000 workers, but in the U.S.
1000 workers use 486 horse-power. The re-
sult is that while the British worker pro-
duces only 9s. 6d. worth of goods a day, the
man in the U.S. produces 295. 6d. worth :
The wealth of a nation and of the individuals of
which it is composed depends on its yearly income,
and its yearly income depends upon its yearly pro-
duction. Owing to its industrial inefficiency the
yearly income of Great Britain is far lower than
that of the U.S., and it is probably considerably
lower than that of Germany.
In the British Census of Production the
entire annual income of Great Britain is
estimated at £2.000,000,000; while the UIS.
Census of Production gives the income of
the U.S. from the production of commodities
alone at £6,209,380,000 a year; and its whole
income should exceed £8,000,000,000 a year.
Compared with these figures the estimated
return of £100,000,000 that Britain is esti-
mated to get yearly from its foreign invest-
ments, and the probable profit of some
£60,000,000 from its mercantile marine are
the merest trifle. Although the population
of the U.S. is only twice that of Britain its
income is four times as great. There is
economy in high wages, as the U.S. have
found. Their manufactures have been forced
to instal costly labor-saving machinery to
increase production. But this is Ellis
Barker’s account of what happened in
Britain: —•
In the first half of the nineteenth century some
economic theorists and some short-sighted business
men succeeded in persuading the British nation that
a high cost of production and high wages were a
disadvantage and' danger to the country. Owing to
their agitation Freetrade was introduced. Free
corn was to keep the cost of living low, and the
free importation of manufactures was to level Bri-
tish wages down automatically to the level of wages
prevailing in the competing countries. Necessity is
the mother of invention. When, through the intro-
duction of Freetrade, the stimulus to mechanical im-
provement had been seriously weakened, and when
British labor had become relatively cheap, British
manufacturers were no longer compelled to provide
the most efficient labor-saving machinery in order
to maintain their position.
Apparently civilised and manufacturing man
has two alternatives. Lie may be a power-
ful animal who makes things by sheer force.
Or he may be a mind who invents the
machine which makes things, and orders the
machine to start by blowing a whistle. Ellis
Barker’s theory is that the Briton has been,
of late years, as compared with his two lead-
ing competitors, overmuch of the mere mor-
tal who makes things by hand, and not
enough of the god who blows the whistle. —
Yours truly, The Old Soldier.

The Blessedness of Party.
Party Government tactics make the Aus-
tralian Parliament a scene of disorder these
days. If the Cook Government, by strenu-
ously keeping Spruce Myth up to the
mark, and by the aid of the Speaker’s cast-
ing vote, can get through the Representa-
tives some measure which the huge Labor
majority in the Senate is bound to throw
out, then it may get a start on the road
towards a dissolution of both Houses —a
thing that has never happened since the
Conftmonwealth was founded. A new elec-
tion for both Houses could hardly make the
Cook Government’s position worse, and it
might become better. So the object of the
Opposition is to prevent any Government
measure getting through the Reps., to estab-
lish the deadlock that Cook is aiming at.
Therefore the Opposition makes strange,
sad noises when the Prime Minister tries to
do something with a Bill —noises like the
long wash of the eternal sea on a rocky
shore, or like a cow looking at a green land-
scape, or ham being boiled in the next
county. The party that is now nominally
in power thought any kind of obstruction
perfectly legitimate in the days of McMil-
lan and Reid and Pumpkin Smith, when
the interminable Freetrade stonewall was
driving Kingston, the best Australian of
them all, into a premature grave, and mak-
ing good old George Turner a physical
wreck. Now Cook is hurried to frenzy by a
small sample of his own crowd’s old tactics.
He is a man with a brief temper and a jumpy
disposition and a peck of nerves. If he
went straight on with his speech, whether
he was audible or not, he might arrive
somewhere, but when lie turns aside to bark
at every person who interrupts, he becomes
a sort of aggravated dog kennel or mad-
dened political hound. In the end he re-
fuses to speak any more, and gives a sort
of howl, and sits down. There is a great
future, in the Party Government system,
for a leader who will take no notice of in-
terjections, and who will drill his followers
into doing the same. The supreme Party
leader would assume that the other party
was dead and would act accordingly. And
he would have a very high precedent to go
upon. The Almighty takes no notice of
interjections.
The Australian Natives’ Association has
propounded an idea that some arrangement
should be made for the acquisition of New
Caledonia from France, so that it may be-
come a dependency of the Commonwealth.
It seems an unwise and earth-hungry pro-
position. The great day of trouble in Aus-
tralia is likely to be the day when some big
Asiatic Power tries its hand at annexation.
Conquest by France, Germany or Holland
might not be pleasant, yet at the worst it
would be merely a transfer from one high-
class European civilisation to another. Eng-
land was conquered by Germans, by Scandi-
navians, by Frenchmen and by Dutchmen,
and it continued to progress. But Asiatic
conquest means the sort of experience that
Russia had with the Tartars and Greece
with the Turks. In other words, it means
Sheol. One of Australia’s streaks of good
luck is that the Germans in Papua, the
Dutch in Java, the Yankees in the Philip-
pines, and the French in New r Caledonia
stand to some extent between this country
and the probable source of disaster. The
Germans, French and Dutch don’t own very
much land that would be of value to Aus-
tralia, but they represent a heap of national
pride and a tremendous fighting capacity.
The idea of persuading any of these races,
ouf of mere vanity and earth-hunger, to give
up its present position as a bulwark be-
tween us and Asia looks an ill-advised pro-
ceeding—this century anyhow.

“Hamer” : The Sydney undertaking combine
has had a horrible effect on the business of
the small suburban planter. His profits were
nothing much, even when his patron saint,
Death, worked hardest on his behalf. Under
the new arrangement they are smaller than
ever. In the good old pre-combine days the
petty buryer was able to endure a prolonged
period of abnormally good public health with
a certain amount of fortitude. He knew
that when the boom came he would be able
to hire burial paraphernalia from one or
other of the big firms, and so handle the
remains with the customary ghoulish pomp
and expense. That is all ended now. An
outbreak of diphtheria or typhoid in his
district leaves him quite cold and unenthu-
siastic. The practice of helping the small
man by hiring to him four-horse hearses,
etc., has been ruled ouf by the combine.
The best thing the indigent body-disposer
can do when a sudden rush of interments
transpires is to nut the surplus ones in the
way of the combination and try and collect
a commission. He has fallen from the proud
position of principal to that of a mere
scuffling, pettifogging agent. There is very
little money in it, and it has the further
unpleasantness of making him feel rather
like Lucifer after the latter was flung out
of the cemetery.

“Melb” : The most important thing in the
Manufacturers’ Show, at the Exhibition
here, is not a manufacture. It is an appeal
and a warning—a placard from the Vic-
torian Forest League. It mentions that in
Warburton 12 acres recently yielded £5OOO
from trees, or over £4OO per acre. This
kept a saw mill going nine months, employ-
ing 24 men, and furnished 3400 tons of
freight to the railway. One tree at Neerim
yielded 10,000 six-foot palings, worth £ll5.
Myriads of such trees have gone up in
smoke, burned off to make farms not worth
more than £5 an acre. Then there are the
bu?h fires to aggravate the wrong. A
single one of them has done £lOO,OOO of
damage. The Forest League urges that
grazing licenses, whose holders are respon-
sible for many of these fires, should be
abolished. They bring in only about £lO,OOO
a year. It is time we woke up. Victoria
consists of 56,000,000 acres, of which
40,000,000 were originally forest. Now we
have but 7,600,000 acres of forest, and only
4,000,000 of them are reserved. Germany,
with 135,000,000 acres, has 35,000,000 acres
of carefully-preserved forests —a hint as to
what the most wide-awake country in the
world is thinking and doing.
Financial note by “H.H.”
A great many people have a suspicion that the
Holman s Government’s device of a Superannuation
Fund for the N.S.W. Civil Service is much the same
old bankrupt ahd shoddy election device as many of
its predecessors. The old N.S.W. Civil Service Fund
went on the rocks. The Police Fund went broke.
Wade s Railway Fund was, at its very beginnings, a
lunatic jest. Every now and then, in the unlucky
Cornstalk State, when an election isn’t far off, some
Government leaps up with a scheme, by which every
public servant is to pay 2d., or some other amount,
into a fund, and then retire on a perfectly solvent
pension of £3OO a year or something. A burst of
gratitude ensues among the unthinking, and the
Cabinet throws itself on the bosom of the country
amid a burst of fireworks. So the fund is established,
and the young and middle-aged start the long, dreary
job of paying in, while a handful of aged get to work
early on the cheerful job of drawing out, and remark
what a splendid idea it is. Of course, pensioners
multiply, and generally, before the time when the
young men of to-day get in sight of their retiring
allowances, there is only 3d. left in the Treasury.
The new proposals look less outrageously insolvent
than some that N.S.W. has had, but even they don’t
look sound. To quote one single point, women are
to pay much less than men for their annuities. Yet
an ordinary insurance office makes a woman pay
ntoT*:. simply because she lives longer on the average,
and draws her allowance longer. This concession to
women is not business, but philanthropy. One-half
the cost of the Civil Service pensions is to be paid
by the State. The State had over £1.000,000 short-
age last year, and it expects another £500,000
deficit by election day. Its position being prac-
ticaHy incurable already, it takes on the new lia-
bility with a light heart. Practically everything in
the way of a corporation which the State possesses—
shire councils, municipalities, the Sydney Art Gal-
lery, the Museum, the Grammar School knd all the
rest—are to provide pensions on the same scale as
the Government. And as they can’t float a £3,000,000
loan any day in London, some of them feel bad
about it.
Premier Holman, of N.S.W., went down
to Dubbo, the other day, and told a patient
country audience how his crowd, or mob. or
rabble were dealing with the decentralisa-
tion problem by duplicating the main rail-
way lines. If Holman saw a man fixing up
some new waterspouts so that more rain
would run into an already-overburdened
water tank, he would say that the plumber
was a fool. And if the plumber told him
that he was doing it with the idea of reliev-
mg the bursting tank, Holman would con-
wue t , h - at he was eithe r a liar or a fool.
What kind of a plumber does Holman call
himself when no one is listening?
There is not supposed to be any racing
at sea; but there are occasional “speed
trials when two new steamers of equal
power happen to be going in the same direc-
tion. The Katoomba and the Canberra, two
of the newest inter-State boats, lately in-
dulged in a trial,” and one morning paper
excitedly recorded the exact times at which
they passed various coastal stations, and
how foaming competitor had gained 25
minutes on the other during the long, fierce
run down the N.S.W. coast. It isn’t racing,
but it s so like it that there is plenty of
opportunity for betting. Then, of course, if
anything happens, the respective ships were
only their best to get their passengers
from one port to the other as quickly as
possible, regardless of coal bills or adver-
tisement. It’s astonishing how little ship-
ping companies care for coal bills or adver-
tisement.
AN EASY WAY TO REMOVE
SUPERFLUOUS HAIR.
IF. the benefit of our lady readers who
suffer from the humiliating disfigurement of
hair on face, lips or chin we publish here-
with a formula which has the advantage of
being simple and inexpensive, as well as
marvellously effective. It is not claimed by
our informant that one application will per-
manently destroy the hair roots, but it is
claimed, and the claims are borne out by
actual tests, that one application completely
lemoves the hair, and its regular use, one or
two days apart, will quicklv and perma-
nently destroy the hair root itself. If you
have hair which you wish permanently re-
moved, ask your chemist to mix 4\ drams
Pulverised Sul thine Concentrate with 2I
drams Zinc Oxide, P. 8., and 1 dram of Pow-
dered Orris Root, and put in a wide mouth
bottle. For use, mix a little of the powder
into a naste on a plate by adding a few
drops of water. Apply with a narrow knife
blade and remove at the end of two minutes
with the back of the knife, when the dis-
solved hair will come with it. Repeat at
intervals of two days. Each time the growth
will be less, until it entirely disannears. A
good, non-greasy cream, such as Creme To-
Kalon, may be used after each application.
CAUTION. —This must not he used as a
substitute for shaving, as its repeated use
completely destroys the hair roots.*

A modern drama was produced at Sydney
Criterion on Saturday night. It ends at
the curtain of the first act. After that all
is melodrama. Bayard Veiller began with
a big subject—the minimum wage and the
urgent necessity of this Socialistic measure
for the overworked, underpaid shop-girl. An
employee in Edward Gilder’s emporium is
wrongfully convicted of theft from the shop.
The stolen property is found in her locker.
In New York, where the law is specially
constructed for the opportunity of the play-
wright, the employer is allowed to influence
the judge ; and on his insistence she is sent
to gaol for three years. But, instead of
proceeding per Black Maria to prison, she
is allowed to come, handcuffed to a detective,
to interview the boss in his office. She pours
forth an impassioned heap of invective,
painting the dreadful life of the underpaid
employees, and launches an hysterical
threat that when she comes out she
will get even with the Fatman. The em-
ployer makes a strenuous defence. He pays
the usual wages in the business. He even
does more for his employees than other
rival shops. He ostentatiously sends big
cheques to charities.
* * * *
Three years in gaol evidently wipe all
intention of fulfilling her threat from Mary
Turner’s mind; for we learn that she has
made every effort to get back among the
ranks of the shop-employees, only to find
that she is “marked” as a convict. Instead
of becoming a leader in an asitation for a
minimum wage, she goes for pure revenge,
the achievement of which will leave the
underpaid shop-girl exactly where she was.
First, she is rescued from the river by a
crook, and joins his gang; but reorganises
its methods. She has found —though she
does not explain where or how—that there
are many ways of making money “Within
the Law.” With the aid of the best legal
advice, she proceeds to build up a black-
mailing industry, keeping always carefully
on the right side of the statutes. In fact,
her business is run exactly on the lines of
a Stock Exchange, a dairy farm, or a Wild
Cat directorate. She marries Richard Gil-
der, son of her old employer. When the
police and the father protest, she reminds
them of her identity, and swears she has done
this for revenge. But the boy refuses to give
her up. So Police-Inspector Burke (who
keeps his hat on in drawing-rooms, takes
possession of private dwellings, smokes cigars
incessantly, bullies and is fooled —in fact,
behaves exactly as the New York policeman
behaves in detective-stories), intervenes.
By means of a “stool-pigeon,” he prevails
upon Mary Turner’s gang to burgle the
Gilder residence, against Mary’s orders, since
such a job is not “within the law.” He
also secures her presence at the
burglary. _ But har husband suddenly
appears in the Gilcfer library. The gang
becomes suspicious of the “stool-pigeon.”
and one of them shoots him with a noiseless
pistol. The police discover Richard and
Mary with the dead man. the rest of the
crowd having got away. The next act finds
both Mary and her husband under arrest
for murder; but the Insnector manages, by
a series of “third degree” expedients—some
of them clumsy to a degree—to discover the
real offender. Mary’s innocence is estab-
lished, and presumably she finds that she
has loved Richard all along. But there is
no hint that the conditions in her father-in-
law’s emporium are alleviated.
This is the melodrama that got five
curtains for the first act, 10i for the second,
seven for the third, and the usual speeches
from the principals before the audience
would leave. This is the melodrama that
is going to run from three to five months
in Sydney. Why? First, because it is
packed with thrills; secondly, because it is
brilliantly acted. The stage devices,
from the silent pistol shot to the
revolving beam of light from a New
York building, which comes at the correct
moment to disclose to the Inspector the mur-
dered man, from the methods by which
“crooks” communicate with each other
through the telephone, without speaking, to
the dealings of the police with suspects, are
admirably managed and often novel. The
Fatman, in the hands of Reginald Wykeham,
fails just to express his unctuous belief in
his own goodness. Winter Hall and Her-
bert Ford appear as two carefully studied
representatives of the law. The “stool-
pigeon,” played by Harrv Sweeney, is not
ouite convincing. Boyd Irwin possesses the
face of the ideal detective, and plays up to
it. Eric Maxon, as the young Gilder, has
one moment of drama, where he expresses
his belief in Mary Turner’s straightness, and
contrives to force into that moment all that
the author intended. Lincoln Plumer’s im-
personation of the Police Inspector is a bril-
liant piece of work. The acting of E. W.
Morrison, iD the part of a crook, is ouietly
effective. The traeic note struck .by
Miss Gertrude Boswell, the real, and after-
wards. repentant, thief, carries conviction.
In Miss Muriel Starr we see an emotional
actress of a rare order. Miss Mary Worth,
in her comedy, is an admirable foil to Miss
fetarr. As a confidence woman, with an
innocent face and a cheerful supply of mixed
guile, good-nature and bad language, Miss
Mary Worth stands on a high plane of
genuine light comedy.
