“The Butterflies” continue to delight big
audiences at the Sydney Little Theatre with
their humorous and musical absurdities.
“Married to the AVrong Man” is again on
the bill at Sydney Adelphi. “The Matri-
monial Misprint” is much the same as it
was on its last visit, and on the visit before
that, and it will doubtless be the same on
the next visit; also, it is pretty well the
same as any other bellowdrama that ever
was or apparently ever will be. This par-
ticular specimen of the genus, by one Fred-
erick Melvillain, deals mainly with the do-
ings of two men, one girl and a corpse. The
two men and a girl are distinctive features
of the species. A third man occurs at inter-
vals, and when he does he leaves a dent,
lhe girl in the case has been left to his
tender mercies; also a small box belonging
to her, and supposed to contain the usual
missing will. Taking the will for granted,
and with an eye to business, he forces the
girl to marry his son, to find after the wed-
ding that the box contains only a scrap of
jewellery, market value about 4d. Natur-
ally, instead of looking for work, the disap-
pointed husband proceeds to murder various
people and make things generally unpleasant,
until he bumps with a loud nose against
tne last act, and then everything is OK
except for the dead. Cyril Mackay and
Miss Nellie Fergusson stand out conspicu-
ously as the principal upholders of Right-
eousness and A^irtue: Herbert Leigh is a
most accomplished bad egg ; and Miss Gwen-
doline Dome is well ahead of the rest of the
field at the end.

More ragtime! A perfect avalanche of it
this time. If the new outbreak has done
nothing else it. has found employment for
thousands or millions of variety artists who
otherwise would have had to get work The
latest dervish in Sydney is Gene Greene,
labelled The Emperor of Ragtime.” How
or when or by whom he was appointed Em-
peror doesn’t transpire; still he speedilv
made mmself the most popular song-merchant
the Tivoli has had for a long time; and
that s all the Tivoli wants. A far more
spectacular turn is that of Reynolds and
itonegan, a pair of expert skaters who have
brought the sliding art to perfection. Fan-
ton s athletes provide a shuddersome display
of aerial gyrations, and Bellora seems
capable of imitating to a remarkable
degree almost anv bird or animal call. The
lour Qs, Willette Whitaker and the
toaxones help to make very attractive bill.

A note from the Rev. George Walters'—
I have read, with pleasure, “Norbell’s” interesting
reminiscences of my Biblical play, “Joseph 4
Canaan, in which, as he confesses, he was one of
the Brethren. After a lapse of 18 years he mav
surely be pardoned for a few mistakes. He says that
Rignold had incense burnt in the theatre in order
to help out the dim religious idea. He may have
but certainly I have no recollection of it. Aeain’
Norbell” says: “One scene lasted 20min., and B not
one word was spoken. It was merely a spectacular
blaze of sacerdotal ceremony in the Temple of
Osins, at On.” But, during that scene, a good
many words were sung or chanted. The solo parts
were taken by Miss Clayton, as Sersa the Priestess
and Mr. Geo. Boulais, as Merenra, the officiating
Priest. There was also the chorus of priests and
priestesses. I have just referred to the prompt-
book, and found that there could have been very
little of that scene when singing or dancing ‘was
not going forward. Sometimes both. The most
serious error, however, is where “Norbell” says that
Rignold “ruthlessly eliminated every line or word
suggestive of humor or levity, and inexorably blue-
pencilled whole scenes of comedy which Mr. Walters
had evolved.” That is incorrect. I did not write
any comedy, and none was eliminated. Surely this par-
ticular “Brother” must have (like Joseph) had a dream
—a vision of a blue-pencil moving in rhythmic lines
across the Non-existent! Having very humbly, or
Uriah-Heepily, made these corrections,'let me shake
“Norbell” by the hand, agreeing with him that
George Rignold “was a Good Australian and a Big
Chief.” 6

The Tenka troupe of Japanese is making
its first Australian appearance at Adelaide
King’s. Sleight-of-hand and magic are its
strong points, and the trick of making water
spring from anywhere and nowhere—includ-
ing' (apparently) a man’s head—is as strik-
ing as it is novel. The Tivoli is strong in
American turns, the four musical and jolly
Primroses being now supplemented by six
Hoboes. Also, Billie Reeves shows how an
intoxicated gentleman can be funny without
being vulgar, and Lolo Krasavina sings songs
at various inoffensive members of the audi-
ence—which is an error in tactics. Any
well-known, middle-aged citizen who has paid
for his seat is liable to be merely annoyed
at being singled out and addressed publicly
as a Great Big Beautiful Doll. The Wil-
loughby-Marlow Co., at the Royal, has re-
placed “The Beggar Girl’s Wedding”—a poor
thing even of its kind —bv “From Convent to
Throne,” which goes much better. Cross
and Crossley, and T. W. Lloyd and Vera
Remee, carry off the thing with a dash.
West’s now show the Rainey wild-animal
pictures, and Wondergraph presents a big
Yankee motor-race. Both are experiment-
ing with a lecturer to explain the films as
they go. The idea is of dubious value, any
way, and some lecturers who cannot get the
pitch of the house are of less value even
than that. Other shows include Shaw’s
“Candida,” revived really well by clever
amateurs; the Lyric Club’s “Spectre
Knight” operetta ; and concerts.
* * * *
“The Confession,” staged at Melbourne
King’s on Saturday, is another illustration
of the might of the confessional and its in-
violable secrecy. Father Bartlett, having
received Joseph Dumont’s confession of a
murder for which the Reverend’s brother is
later charged, has to decide between respect-
ing the penitent’s confidence and seeing his
relative hanged by the neck till he shall be
dead. The old, familiar problem of Duty v.
Affection is handled by the author as subtly
as an elephant might play with a boulder.
By deliberately straining the probabilities
he brings brother Tom within easy distance
of the gallows, the while his Reverence be-
comes a perambulating picture of internal
unrest. To get him out of his difficulty
Dumont is conveniently smitten with gallop-
ing consumption, and after about the short-
est illness on record, makes open confession

%* * *
“Bobby Burnit,” presented at Melbourne
Bijou last Saturday, doesn’t compare favor-
ably with its stable companions, “The For-
tune Hunter” and “Brewster’s Millions.”
The name character is an amazing creature
of the theatrical world. Suddenly shifted
from a life of luxurious loafing to the
management of his deceased father’s
Stores, Bobby takes a hand at high finance
with an optimistic belief in his own ability.
In about 20 minutes of actual stage time, his
easy fatuity and prodigal recklessness ruin
him utterly, and he is faced with a future
of dismal indigence. But in the supreme
moment of his humiliation, rendered more
trying by the receipt of letters left behind by
his defunct papa, he suddenly changes from
a kindergarten financier to a Jav Gould.
The transition, as portrayed by Hugh Buck-
ler, is positively violent. The difference
between his idea of Bobby the amateur and
Bobby the expert is all the difference between
boyish inconsequence and domineering beefi-
ness, with voice and habits to match. He is
more attractive in his first phase. Reynolds
Denniston plays Sam Stone, a “grafter,”
moderately well; and Kenneth Brampton
strives to make Biff Bates an effective por-
trait. As Samuel Johnson, Arthur Cornell
is most lifelike when he has nothing to say,
and the impersonations of Charles Lawrence
and Kingston Hewitt are dull all the time.
Miss Lilian Lloyd is noticed among the
ladies, and Miss Violet Paget takes a mean
advantage of the critic’s susceptibility to
feminine raiment; for after she has changed
her frocks two or three times, he forgets to
consider the unreality of her characterisa-
tion.
At West’s Melbourne Palace the Battle
of Waterloo is raging with all the noise
that West's minions can manufacture on the
premises. The picture is a creditable
attempt to realise the historical scene as it
probably looked if it didn’t look otherwise.
The principal characters are very good so
hir as we are able to tell, seeing that Time
has done a lot of obliteration since the days
when these eminent deads were in the flesh
and cooked hat of reality.

Galsworthy’s “Strife,” staged at Melb.
Athenaeum Hall last Saturday, has a wider
reach of human interest than any of Gregan
McMahon’s previous Repertory Theatre pro-
ductions. Dealing with the last day of a
long strike at the Trenartha Tin Plate
Works, the story alternates between the
gloomy comedy of Capital sighing for divi-
dends and the gruesome tragedy of Labor
craving for food. The secretary of the Tin
Plate Workers’ Union (which has ceased to
assist the unfortunate strikers) is the mat-
ter-of-fact angel of peace who hovers around
the proceedings. Practically the play tends
to glorify trades unionism whilst damning
the firebrand David Roberts. He has car-
ried , the men beyond union control.
Supported by strike pay, the workers would
have arrived at the same settlement in a less
unfed state. As it is, the incidental death
of Roberts’s wife, due to a weak heart and
an empty larder, is chargeable to Roberts
only, for he rejects in the first place the
Fnion’s strike-pay conditions, and secondly
he refuses to let the sick woman be fed by
the daughter of the chairman of the Tin
Plate Co.
Various types of character on both sides
—obstinate and conciliatory, coldly selfish
and hotly vindictive, reasonable and fanatical
—are forcibly presented by Galsworthy.
The author suggests that “class” sentiment
is at the root of all industrial trouble, but
he offers no cure for it. The chair-
man’s gentle daughter, who is the manager’s
wife, complains that the envy and hatred of
the workers repel her sympathy. Roberts,
though he commands respect for his pluck,
makes one tired with his rancorous refer-
ences to motor-cars and champagne. _ Finally,
when peace is declared, against their oppos-

h, b e striker salute one another,
the presence of compromise they feel

George Bryant, the picturesque bad man
ot ™ ar ,V v a melodrama, helped to make
‘‘Strife" an impressive entertainment.
Bryant was, in fact, the backbone of the
performance. His study of Roberts was a
notable work of art. He presented a path-
etic picture of the unlovable rebel,
whose fondness for his own sick wife is so
inconsistent with his want of consideration
for the woes or all the other women. Miss
Hungerford had a lot to do as the manager’s
young wife, and she did it charmingly. Miss
Dorothy Hiscock made a spiritedly venom-
daughter of a mild old striker (Lyall
Christian), the old man being also praise-
worthy in all respects save his mock-Welsh
accent. Messrs. McMurtrie, Walker, Hay-
bittel, Trotman, Cussen, Newell and others
ranged from quite good to very passable,
whilst F. B. Reeve, as the obdurate chair-
man, talked staccato from the pit of his
stomach in a strangely-grimaceful manner.
He never spoke from his lips. Apparently
his idea of expressing determination of
character was to turn his mouth violently
down at the corners like a fish, and leave the
words to get through anyhow. To-night
TThursday) and to-morrow Bernard Shaw’s
“Major Barbara” will be the Repertory bill.
All the Quinlan Opera events done in
Melbourne since last Bulletin were re-
peats, with the exception of “Carmen” on
Saturday. Edna Thornton is the Carmen
of the co., and people who had not seen her
and other members of the cast in characters
better suited to them are perhaps rating
those quick-change artists below their value.
“Hoffmann,” “Aida” and “Tannhauser,” in
that order, are billed for the last three nights
of this week. Next week “Girl of the
Golden West,” “Traviata” and “Trovatore”
will happen for the first and only time this
season.
The popular puerilities of Doonedrama
are proceeding at Melbourne Princess’s as
before. Until Friday right “The Parish
Priest” (who is not “The Village Priest,”
as the programme thoughtfully mentions)
will keep Allen Doone employed on the holy
fatherly job of attending to the loves of Dr.
Walsh and Helen Durkin, whilst restoring
Miss Cassidy to the confidence of Mr.
Dougherty, and doing what he can to bring
doleful James Walsh to terms with Miss
Corrigan, begorra! Doone’s present play
is as soft as an underdone muffin. Also his
pale, thin warble of “Old Fashioned
Mother” lasts fully five minutes by the
clock. Seeing that large audiences delight
in the gentle drones of Doone and all things
appertaining thereto. The Bulletin pre-
dicts financial success for a three nights’
revival of “A Romance in Ireland,” begin-
ning next Saturday (27th), and for “Molly
Bawn,” which is booked for an equally brief
sprint.
This is the sixth week of “The Fortune
Hunter” at Melbourne Royal, but the last
nights of Nathaniel’s regeneration are not
yet announced.
Merian's Acting Dogs arrived at Mel-
bourne Opera House last Saturday, and
achieved a number of humorous effects, with
the aid of scenery and dressing. Frank
Parish, who would probably take first prize
in a barrel-jumping competition, is another
newcomer; and the same remark applies to
Julia Gonzales on the trapeze. In the rest
of the bill the clever Golden Troupe appears
for 20 minutes every evening until further
notice, whereas the De Bruns are dancing
on the verge of the precipice of departure.
The third of the present series of Clara
Jtsutt concerts comes due to-night (Thurs-
day), and again on Saturday the noble voice
will be heard at Melbourne Auditorium.
AT POVERTY POINT.
The visit of the Clara Butt concert co. to Ballarat
last month was an exciting experience for the local
Star gazer. He started his p»an of praise in this
key: “The world famous contralto and her hus-
band need no introduction to a musical audience.
Their reputation still stands out with pristine splen-
dor, and what is more remarkable and pleasurable
to note is that they still have the same power of
moving their hearers by their pure and marvellous
vocalism.” Having thus stated, as a remarkable
fact, that Mr. and Mrs. Butt were still worthy of
their undiminished reputation, the critic went on to
say that the husband’s best effort in marvellous
vocalism was “a charming Irish comic song.”
Quinlan has to pay £lOO a night royalty (to
Wagner’s son) on each performance of the “Ring ’
operas. The royalty on “Parsifal” is said to be ridi-
culously high—several hundreds of pounds—conse-
quently Melbourne is not getting any “Parsifal.”
For that matter, Melbourne didn’t rush the second
round of the “Ring” series. At Quinlan prices the
reproduction of “The Valkyrie” drew only a moder-
ate crowd, though years ago six consecutive per-
formances of that opera (by, Musgrove’s German
co. at the Princess’s) filled the house every time.
Pianist Vincent O’Brien didn’t accompany John
McCormack and company to Bananaland ; Lawrence
Godfrey Smith has gone instead It came about this
way. Sunday before last, O Brien, who was week-
ending at Rose Bay with the McCormacks, suddenly
heard the call of the surf. It was so insistent that
he couldn’t wait until the tram stopped. So he
“lepped,” and a damaged hand and arm took him
off to Lewisham Private Hospital that evening.
Beaumont Smith, back from the Wide World, made
a few remarks over his glass: “How far has Tiny
Town been? Well, count it. We started in Ade-
laide, went through Australia, Maoriland, Africa up
to Rhodesia, England (for a rest),, through Canada,
and now back to Maoriland, opening in Auckland.
This time we will travel under canvas. ... How much
money did we make outside Australasia ? Not as
much as the Federal Bank has in the safe. In
Canada we battled into 10 of about the biggest
circuses in the world —Barnum and Bailey (with a
tent to swallow 30,000 people when they were avail-
able), Buffalo Bill, Pawnee Bill, Rinlings (practi-
cally owned by the Buffalo Bill crowd), Sanger’s, and
several others. They were all fighting for the circus
supremacy of America, and we couldn't hear our-
selves eat soup on account of the noise. Buffalo
Bill went up for 200,000d015. in the middle of it,
60,000d015. being for printing. You can sleep with-
out being anxious as to whether it was strenuous.
I had a Maorilander in advance, and also went ahead
myself. After breakfast, say, we’d be charmed to
see no signs of a circus, and reckon to be on velvet
with the posting. Half an hour or an hour later a
mob of hurry-ups would attract our inquiry and
illicit the reply: ‘Them ? That’s No. 1 car of the
circus posters.’ I’ve seen eight of those cars hit a
town, empty out hundreds of bill-stickers, and cover
the scenery for 50 miles round. Our modest lPtle
show couldn’t get a poster in edgeways. We struck
those human locusts everywhere, eating .up the
advertising space and cleaning out the pubs. We
made a mistake, too, in going into the country dur-
ing the summer time. That’s the worst-paying
season. Then the jumps we had to make! Some
were equivalent to a journey from Adelaide to Bris-
bane. .. .Profits? In Canada what we in Australia

“Petramos”: All Wowsers please leave the room.
Since, in dancing, the exhibition of legs is per-
mitted, I rise to propose the abolition of tights.
r «o'’''’'ded, as regards male extremities, by Oscar
cne on behalf of the Asiatic Actors’ League, and
supported bv Hamlin MacTavish, representing the
Presbyterian Flingers.] I wish it understood that
I move the abolition (within reason) of women-
dancers’ tights. Chief witness in support, the man-
ager of the Hansa Theatre, Hamburg, where I found
the experiment failed to raise a blush, mostly because
the absence was not immediately noticeable. Real
legs (the brother at the keyhole will kindly desist)
are, in my modest opinion, sightlier than legs which
are shut up in silk. The silk is liable to sag ; and,
further, it is often a fit subject for a laundry bill.
Moreover, a dimple on a pretty knee (all other
efforts having failed, the Sergeant at Arms will
please block up the keyhole) is a sight not only
for the gods, but for the circle and stalls as well.
Anyone doubting the value of evidence by the man-
ager of the Hansa Theatre is hereby informed that,
in Germany, that theatre has a reputation for afflu-
ence second only to the plutocratic Berlin Winter-
garten, and is the most Wowsery amusement resort
in Kaiser Willie’s domains, starting nightly at 8
o’clock and concluding with a prayer for Bill at
10.30.
“Ex-Manager”: The Bulletin “Sundry Shows”
(11/9/’l3) instances Diver Peart as a man who
tossed dice with death and lost. Since Peart’s day,
however, divers go much nearer sudden death for a
living, and take longer odds. I’ve a letter in my
possession from an American who offered to dive
70ft. on to a hard board incline twice daily, and do
triple parachute descents of 3000 ft. or so in his
spare time for £7 per iveek. He didn’t even want
Sundays off. It is amazing how hard some mer.
will work to escape toil.
Dancing was to be a feature of Beerbohm Tree’s
“Joseph and his Brethren” in London. For this
part of the business Tree had secured the services
of a man who has “made a special study of scriptural
dances and their evolution through the ages.”
According to this authority, some of the Biblical
dances have evoluted queerly. For instance, the
American cakewalk “can be traced to the Biblical
celebration of the festival of ripe fruits.” And as
Miriam, the sister of Moses, did a sort of double-
turn before the Ark—-she danced and played the
tambourine —Irving Sayles seems to be supplied with
Scriptural warrant for his capers. ..
“0.K.”: Sooner or later the Williamson firm is
certain to form a touring Grand Opera Co. on the
lines of the Quinlan organisation, and then the one
trouble will be to get a Quinlan who can be trusted
with a ruling rod of iron. There is no insubordina-
tion in this invading army of 175 or more—the
actual figures are said to be 181. Quinlan’s word
is law. His strength lies mainly in the fact that
none of his most versatile principals are “star”
attractions, and nobody can prevent an advertised
performance from coming to pass by refusing to
appear. The co. carries no indispensables. Every-
body capable of playing a leading part has been
carefully prepared to come to the front if required,
and the leading figures in the chorus can look
forward confidently to their coming chances as minor
principals. Very few of the Quinlan operas are ever
repeated with exactly the same cast as they had for
the previous performance, and no cast is weaker
than another in all respects. Moreover, when the
cast is weakened after a first production, the orchestra
and chorus are likely to be all the better for their
previous experience. “Tales of Hoffmann” is seem-
ingly the most useful card in Quinlan’s present pack.
He advertised it at the outset for one performance
only, but has repeated it several times, with vary-
ing principals, because it draws the crowd that is
most easily pleased. But there has always been a
lapse of several nights between the revivals. Con-
stant change is essential to the success of a Quinlan
season, and the strictest discipline is necessary to
keep it going at that high pressure.

“Frank C.”: Joke Cook boasts 37 “fol-
lowers” in the House of Reps., yet couldn’t
rake up a quorum of 25 last Friday. lie
complains that the Opposition won’t help
him, but, if he has a majority, why doesn’t
he run the House? When the bells were
calling for the necessary members, several
liberals who should have been there—lce-
berg Irvine among them—were late. Pigott
and De Laune liyrie were playing tennis
outside, and stayed to finish the game. When
they did condescend to answer the call it
w r as too late, the House had been counted
out. It’s a dreadful thing to be the leader
in a great Liberal revival and have a work-
ing majority that won’t work. By the way,
this count out draws attention to the solemn
fact that Cook is so poorly supported in the
Senate that, without Labor’s assistance, he
would be always counted out. He hasn’t
even a quorum. Fancy having to depend on
the Opposition to keep a House for you !
“Jeames,” who is this paper’s accredited
Court Correspondent, discourses on a
Government House Matter :—■
Governor Strickland, of N. S. Wales, holds the
world’s record as an A.D.C.-loser. A.D.C.’s fall off
him, in a manner of speaking, like water off -a
duck’s back. The two latest to go the way of an.
Gentlemen Helps that enter the Strickland house-
hold are Captain Ronald Forbes and Captain Talbot.
This amiable pair of young warriors arrived in the
Mother State full of hope and loyalty and good in-
tentions some six months ago. On Monday last, after
many bitter trials and innumerable excursions and
alarms, they discovered that the billet was over the
odds, and that none but a brace of Jobs could hold
it. So Captain Talbot leaves for Epgland on the
11th of next month, while Captain Forbes has severed
his connection with State Government House, and
will follow in the wake of his colleague so soon as
the lease of the cottage which he and his wife have
taken in Sydney is up. Meanwhile the distracted
Strickland, despairful at the prospect of having no
one to trail behind him in a uniform, is casting
round feverishly among che local forces, naval and
military, and, at short intervals, cabling wild re-
quests for assistance and help and succor, and so
forth, to the Mother Land. It is all very dreadful,
and the position at Cranbrook at the time of writ-
ing bears many points of resemblance to the condi-
tion of things that led up to the Guerre folle, while
in other important respects it suggests the classic
row which is inalienably associated with the name
of the late Cato. The only light on the whole de-
plorable situation gleams fitfully from the fact that
Governor Strickland is not unused to being without
A.D.C.’s. He has tried it before, and the Empire
has not burst. In fact, he is more often without
these ornaments than otherwise. Victor Hood stood
the Cranbrook atmosphere for three months. Then
the daily newspapers, ever informative—-in the sense
that they always supply the public with the informa-
tion that they consider good for it, and no more—
announced that Mr. Hood was ill. He was so ill
that he went to Victoria and joined the staff of Sir
John Fuller, where he has abided contentedly ever
since. To Westralia, when Strickland was Governor
there came one Kerr-Pearse, of the Rifle Brigade, and
his friend, Selby-Smyth, also of the Rifle Brigade.
They had chucked their billets, and were streaking
back home, before Perth knew what drinks they
liked best. After feverish efforts to get a fresh
supply of A.D.C. from the country which is the
chief exporter of such goods, Strickland was fain to
settle down with a local man named Wilkins. One
of the many reasons which are advanced darkly in
the servants’ hall to explain all this unpleasantness
is edifyingly typical of the Government House point
of view. The idea is that the Governor has made the
mistake of hiring A.D.C.’s out of his own class. Hood
is the son of a Bridport, Talbot a scion of the Norfolk
family, Forbes a member of a great and incredibly
haughty Scotch clan, and so on. Strickland is a mere
semi-Maltese bourgeois. For such a one to give orders
to a Kerr-Pearse, or any other double-barrelled
social circumstance, is equivalent to a member of
the Union Club being hectored arrogantly by a Paris
House waiter. Hence all sorts of misunderstand-
ings and heart-burnings. Let us, in conclusion, dear
friends, cheer ourselves up with the reflection that
two benefits at least emerge from rows of this sort.
Firstly, the liners on which A.D.C.’s mostly travel do
business both outwards and inwards, that, were a
Dudley or a Denman at the helm, they would have
missed. In the second place, the local article in the
A.D.C. line is given a show. So, take them for all
in all, these regularly recurring uproars are rather
by way of being in the public interest.
THE DOVE IS A FOOL!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Just over the skylight of the Divorce Court a
pair of doves have established their home, and
yesterday, while his Honor was pronouncing the
dissolution of the marriage of two highly-placid and
wealthy people, the softly penetrating “coo-coo” of
the brooding dove almost induced the court orderly
to cry “silence.” —Sydney Telegraph.
I hate the soft coo of the dove
That comes from the branches above;
1 think it’s all tripe—that monotonous pipe
Of the bird who is always in love.
It brings back the days when, imbued
With a fatuous passion, I wooed
In a garden infested with doves who contested
For kisses and foolishly cooed.
1 loathe the dull coo of the dove!
With poles I would savagely shove
Each soft, downy nest where the rotten things
rest
And eternally moan about love.
I wish they could chatter or snort,
For they coo till I’m quite overwrought;
And they’re billing and mating where lawyers
orating
Are breaking Love’s bonds in a court.
I think there’s no fool like the dove!
No sillier passion than love!
Our fatuous coos give us Shivers and blues
When we come back to earth. But above
The d—d bird keeps on making the row
That enrages us all. He’s a cow
Is the bird that keeps cooing,
And pecking and stewing,
And playing the fool on a bough!
N.S.W.
JOHN FURIOUS.

FERDINAND: “Haw! Why do you call your

“Scoffer”: How empty are the boasts of
the lovesick! A young Sydney man who
had informed his lady, with the usual
flourish of the pen, that he “would go to
the end of the world with her,” was, last
week, sued by her for common, ordinary
breach of promise. He may have been ready
enough to go to the world’s end and look
over the edge, or make the pilgrimage to the
Holy Land, or Manly; but the plain job of
establishing a home in the suburbs and
keeping it going in an unsensational way
with his wages was apparently too much
for him. What suggests these absurd poet-
ical bursts to the inflamed mind of the
lover? Is it the drawing-room song, with
its wild and meaningless howls about win-
ning a world’s applause, and tearing
down the eternal stars that guide the
mariner and the lone explorer, so that she
may be saved the expense of tortoiseshell
hair-combs? I guess it must be. Observe
the intense reverence with which the young
man in love listens to any such musical ex-
travagance. He agrees with every word of
it, and the female for whom he would dis-
turb all creation is the ordinary-looking girl
on the other side of the room who is so pre-
occupied with her bangles and frizzy back
hair.
The latest proposal, moved by Hutchin-
son, M.L.A., for a referendum on the time-
worn question of Bible teaching in Vic.
State schools, is not likely to lead to busi-
ness. Hutchinson submits two questions for
the voters. Firstly, are they in fa,vor of
unsectarian Scripture lessons being given to
the youngsters during school hours _by
teachers who have no conscientious objec-
tions. Which is tantamount to _ a sugges-
tion that a matter of public policy should
be left to be decided by the “conscience”
of individual teachers in more than 50 per
cent, of the schools. In 1249 schools all
the instruction is given by one person only,
whose individual faith would thus become a
matter of grave concern to all the local
parents who take religion seriously. Sec-
ondly, Hutchinson would ask the voters
whether they approve of introducing clergy-
men or their agents into State schools, “un-
der conditions similar to those existing in
N.S.W. and Queensland.” The average
voter in Victoria knows nothing of the con-
ditions aforesaid, and is equally ignorant
as to whether they are giving good results.
Between the two rival systems of State
school education —strictly secular and
somewhat otherwise —the issue slmuld be
decided on crime statistics. If religious in-
struction has raised N.S.W. and Queens-
land to a higher moral plane than Victoria,
where the Government has shut the parson
out of its State schools, Victoria will be in
favor of adopting it. If not, any infringe-
ment on the straight-out secular system
would seem most uncalled for. What Vic-
toria wants before considering any refer-
endum questions is a report from an inter-
state Commission of Police.

Melbourne Criminal Court was invaded
last week by a badged female who has formed
herself into a Society for the Protection of
Child-bearing Women. She is organising
secretary, president, financial committee, and
all the other members of this society, and
she proclaims her identity with a band round
her bonnet marked, “Protection of C.B.
Women.” The Court, not being in an inter-
ested condition, ordered the intruder to be
slung out.
The British legislators who are rushing
around Australia will take home some
gloriously hazy notions about this land of
milk and honey. One day last week they
were captured by the Melbourne University
authorities and made to listen to a speech
of welcome by John Madden, after which
the very unlordly Sheffield arose and stated
that the Sydney University should be very
proud to have a man like John at the head
of its affairs. No doubt any University
would be proud to be connected with John,
but exactly what Sydney in particular has
to do with it isn’t obvious. It’s a moral
certainty that these hurried legislators will
distinguish themselves when they get home
by the learned way they discuss the beauti-
ful harbor of Melbourne and the fine, wide
streets they noticed in Sydney.

The case of one Gleeson has moved on a
peg. In 1904 Gleeson joined in a Govern-
ment gamble or lottery or financial ambus-
cade for a piece of land near Dubbo
(N.S.W.), and drew the winning marble.
The Land Board confirmed the application
and he entered into possession. Then one
Toynton butted in and argued that the selec-
tion awarded to Gleeson was, under a sec-
tion of one of Ma’s pyramid of Land Acts,
wrongly staked, and as Gleeson wouldn’t
take his word for it, he had the law on him.
That long-eared creature brayed and blew
the flies off itself with its tail for 18 months,
and ended up by telling Gleeson to get
work; also, it charged him £2OO for the
advice. But there was nothing effete about
Gleeson. He leaped up and pursued the
animal that keeps what brain it possesses
from evaporating with a horsehair lid, and
demanded his money and what legal ven-
geance might be available. For eight whole
and entire years Gleeson and the enemy
periodically hove up over the horizon of
things, and dashed past in a cloud of dust
and impious language. One of these speed
bursts brought up in a Select Committee of
the Assembly’, and, last week, the umpires
recommended that Gleeson should be handed
back the £2OO, so that peace might be upon
the land and the loam be allowed to settle.
The quaint condition of the municipal in-
tellect was manifested when Richmond
(Vic.) council called itself together to con-
sider the awfulness of Mayor Webber in
neglecting to drink whisky to the greater
glory of George V. Councillor Street brought
the Almighty conspicuously into the discus-
sion. He offered prayers to the Almighty,
and called on God to bless everyone; and,
according to the Argus report, the assem-
bled Bumbles greeted every such reference
with “laughter,” “loud laughter,” “renewed
laughter,” and other manifestations of deri-
sion. But they were all venomous towards
Webber for refusing to drink. Yet even if
Webber drank himself right up to the beery
gates of perdition, Geo. V. would have been
no better off. Those people who reckon that
a king can’t prosper unless he is drunk are
a quaint crowd. This writer wouldn’t be
one of them on any account. A whole bottle
of cocktail wouldn’t tempt him.

A Melbourne jury acquitted Arthur
Parker, who was charged with shooting at
one McKenzie with intent to slaughter.
McKenzie had philandered with Miss
Parker, and dad pointed in the direction of
the altar with a business-like revolver. The
young man thought over the matter and
decided to make a bolt for it, whereupon the
other tired a shot which came within an ace
of outing an innocent shop-assistant. The
jury took only 10 minutes in deciding to
believe Parker’s yarn that he had fired
merely to frighten McKenzie, so things are
just about as they were. Parker is un-
quodded, McKenzie uninjured and the lady
unmarried. At the same time, it is up to
someone to issue a warning that the sticking
up of bachelors with a demand for their
freedom or their lives must not be allowed
to develop into a habit on the strength of
some Yankee fiction about an “unwritten
law.” When this country wants a law
observed it is going to write it down in
black and white ; and when it hasn’t written
the alleged law down, the average Aus-
tralian jury is pretty certain to remark
coldly that the thing isn’t a law because
the people never intended that it should be
one.
Another familiar old Collins-street build-
ing is a jumble of bricks and broken mortar
—Scots’ Church parsonage, erected in 1851.
Pastor Iletherington was in charge there for
a long series of years. His church was a
square brick tabernacle, rather larger than
that of the Independents, on the opposite
corner. Iletherington’s church was later
plastered over, and a spire built, but it
proved to be crooked a-top; so church and
spire were both demolished, and the present
house run up from designs by Reed and
Barnes, architects of the Town Hall and
Exhibition. Meanwhile the Independent
church had been replaced by the present
empty magnificence. The Scots’ parsonage
goes to make way for the new Assembly
Hall. The Presbyterians also own the Audi-
torium, on the site of their old .Assembly
Tlall, which was St. Enoch’s church. Ihe
parsonage has not been occupied by the pai-
son for a whole generation past; the city
clergy these days live in luxurious suburbs,
and only come in to their place of business
when the bell rings.
THE MANAGER AND THE CLERK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sir, —In Monday’s issue you reported Mr. Cook,
the Prime Minister, as having stated that “We
wanted plenty of weddings in this young country.”
I would like to draw attention once more to the fact
that there are numerous young men who are willing
and otherwise prepared to get married, but unfor-
tunately they are debarred by the institution to
which they belong. Some nine or 10 months ago,
Mr. Fisher had inquiries made as to what banks
and insurance companies prohibited their officers
from marrying under a certain salary. When the
paper was laid on the table, it was found that all
the most important banks had a rule that their
officers were not to get married unless they were in
the receipt of £2OO per annum.
—Extract from a sorrowful cleric's letter.
He sits upon a padded chair;
I perch upon a high cane stool ;
Grey is his face and grey his hair;
He says I am a “d —d young fool.”
I send Her notes —I take her out,
But with my love I may not wed.
(The grey chief’s wife is stern and stout,
And wears large feathers on her head).
Sometimes he comes and groans to me
When she has hit him for a cheque.
(“Now there you are, my boy! You see
How you will get it in the neck !
“Take my advice and keep your cash,
The single life’s the one for ease;
This marriage settles all men’s hash,
And women are d —d hard to please!”)
I cannot think that She —My Own—
Would ever be so grim and fat,
And drive me out to curse and groan.
Time cannot be as harsh as that!
I cannot see her, fiercely fat,
Come rustling to my desk in town,
With fearful feathers in her hat,
And shrill demands for cash —cash down.
Maybe I am the fool he says;
But, high upon my slavish stool,
I yearn to win the chair that pays
And be the richer sort of fool.
x.s.w.

The Vic. Cadet Forces are petitioning the
Minister of Defence for butter, marmalade,
and other aids to appetite with their even-
ing meal at next annual encampment. Dry
bread has hitherto been the rule at tea, anil
the young warriors reckon that bread and
jam would be more supporting. After all.
there is no special reason why the horrors of
war should be anticipated in the commis-
sarirt department. .Tam and treacle are
not subversive of military discipline, and n
national defender doesn’t need to be edu
cated up to a Spartan menu.
ANNOYANCE
IN THE
SACRED EDIFICE.
London, Monday evening.—The suffragettes, Winnie Wallace and Flora Smith, who were arrested and
remanded on a charge of assaulting the Prime Minister, Mr. Asquith, at the Lossiemouth golf links,
Elgin, sat opposite the Premier's seat in the Duffus church, and stared at him throughout the service.
After the service the women made offensive remarks as the Prime Minister passed.— Cablegram.
The “All Australian” Exhibition in Mel-
bourne (which is almost entirely Victorian
in its commercial aspects) scored an at-
tractive advt. for the show at the expense
of other people’s feelings. The com-
mittee of the Exhibition withdrew
Norman Lindsays picture, “the Crucifixion
ot \ enus, from the art-gallery exhibits,
whereupon the N.S.\\. Society ot Artists de-
manded an explanation, llie explanation be-
gan and ended in a surmise that Lindsays
clever drawing might ‘give offence to a cer-
tain section of the community. After fur-
ther consideration of this surmise, the com-
nnttee decided to replace the withdrawn
work, the announcement of which fact was
followed by a rush of inquiring citizens who
had not previously taken any live interest
in the display at Melbourne Exhibition
Luilding. Norman Lindsay can now count
himself among the best friends of Australian
industry and commerce.

lectures. A sum of £2OOO was raised in Melbourne
as endowment, and Moorhouse himself gave a fur-
ther £lOOO. The lecturer, who must be an Angli-
can bishop or priest, gets the interest on this for
one year, and in return has to give six lectures on
some religious or sociological subject, and to pub-
hsh the lectures in book form within six months,
retaining the copyright. No lecturer can hold office
more than twice, and at least 10 years must elapse
between his first and second tenure. The appoint-
ment rests with the Anglican bishops in Australia
and Primate Nevill, of Maoriland. So far the lec-
turers have been Bishop Mercer, of Hobart; Dean
Stephen, of Melbourne; Bishop Green, of Ballarat;
Archbishop Clarke, of Melbourne; and Dr. Radford,
of Sydney.' Radford—this year’s lecturer—is the
° n iZ • °, ne has £ one to an Australian printer and
publisher for the production of the book required in
terms of. the lectureship; all the others published
abroad.’ An English church paper, reviewing
Archbishop Clarke’s book, published in London,
asked pertinently: “Are there no publishing firms in
Australia?” Radford’s lectures, entitled “Ancient
Heresies in Modern Dress,” were a bitter disap-
pointment to those who expected them to deal with
the X-ray skirt and peek-a-boo blouse. Headlam,
who comes to Melbourne next Easter, cuts much ice in
English church circles as scholar and Biblical critic,
and has had the training of a large number of the
younger Anglican clergy. Incidentally, he follows
the inky way as editor of the Church Quarterly
Review. Radford, his predecessor in the lecture-
ship, edits a similarly, named Australasian quar-
terly. Headlam will explain how prophets made
axe-heads float, what kind of whale it was that
Jonah swallowed, and what language Balaam’s edu-
cated donkey spoke. At least, the subject of his
lectures is advertised as “Miracles.”
Forty-seven American boys who are tour-
ing the world under Major Peixotto, who
was here in 1909 with a former batch, are
now in South Aus. They had three weeks
in London, and a few days in Paris, and have
put in eight weeks all over Westralia, most
of the Australian boys who went to the
U.S.A. last year having gone from that
State. They played 18 football matches while
there —Australian rules —and won seven of
them. From Adelaide they go to Tasmania,
Victoria and Sydnev (small-pox permitting),
and so up to Manila and Japan. The major
says this education-by-travel idea is catch-
ing on so well that the next tourists in
America will be a party of French boys.
WIFELY ECONOMY.
“ Now, look here, my dear , this sort of thing
has to slop. Are you aware that it takes
three-fourths of my salary to meet your
dressmaker's bills? ”
"Good heavens, George! What do you do with
the rest of your money?”
“C.E.”: The U.S. Government is nego-
tiating a treaty with Nicaragua for the sole
right of cutting a canal, to serve as a second
string to the Panama ditch. Before the
U.S. took up the latter in earnest, it par-
leyed with Nicaragua, and obtained a con-
cession, which, however, lapsed. It was
said at the time that the Nicaraguans had
over-reached themselves, with an idea that
the Panama Canal was not feasible. Now,
better terms are expected by Uncle Sam.
The Cuelebra cut is a weak link in the big
Panama job. Landslides have been per-
sistent, giving the engineers much anxiety;
and there is always the dread of earth-
quakes. One way or another, the U.S. may
thus find the canal blocked just at a vital
period of trouble. The Nicaragua work
would not be of enormous cost. The lake,
of 70 miles,. would be taken in; also the
San Juan River. The total length would be
180 miles, of which 62 would have to be
cut- —comparatively easy compared with
Panama. Ships would, of course, take
longer passing through, but they would be
377 miles nearer New York, and as much
nearer Liverpool. The dividing ridge is
lower at Nicaragua than at Panama, but
Panama might, with great expense, be made
a straight sea-level cut, while any attempt
to do that sort of thing at Nicaragua would
cause great excitement. It is a serious mat-
ter to spill one of the biggest lakes in
America bodily into the ocean. If the
Nicaragua canal is attempted, of course the
little republic will drift into Uncle Sam’s
keeping, and add another to Samuel’s re-
sponsimlities and sleepless nights. It is a
stormy place. One history says briefly:
“Its %st ruler was a murderer ; the second
a murderer and rebel; the third murdered
the second; the fourth was a forger; the
fifth a murderer and rebel.”

“Afternoon Tea” : I forget for the moment
what satirist owns the socially ambitious
lady who “tried to found a salon but only
succeeded in opening a restaurant.” But
the salon which is also a restaurant really
has arrived in Sydney, and anyone who has
the money can go and enjoy high-toned food
behind a sign which is associated in most
people’s minds with highbrowing alone. The
butchering studio, the sausage gallery and
the beer embassy haven’t yet shown up. but
they may be looked for any morning.
“II.II.” : Sydney is enjoying an exhibition
which deals with the question: “VVhat to
Do With our Girls.” It may be a burning
problem, but for some years past Australia
has been mostly worried in the kitchen about
“How to Do Without our Girl/’ The ab-
sence of that female has got it down and
bitten it.
** + ■*■
“Bloore”: The uncertificated nurse still
thrives, and it is up to the powerful British
Medical Association to justify itself and
wipe her out —as it easily can if it really
wants to. I have just seen a communica-
tion from “St. So-and-so” (the “private”
hospital nearly always has its Saint, for
advertising purposes), and quote an ex-
tract :—-
Mary Jones, which I have in my care, was taken
to Hospital yestaday suffering from Plusary.
Still, when we see the miserable pittances
handed out to matrons of country hospitals,
one can hardly expect a wild rush for a
diploma. Thirlmere (N.S.W.), with an
average of 40 patients, advertises for a
matron and offers £7O a year!
The Smith family has, in the main, been
fairly respectable, but one Rev. _ Hans
Schmidt, of New York, is bringing it into
disrepute. It is alleged against him that
he married Anna Aumaller in complete dis-
regard of the Ten Commandments, perform-
ing the marriage ceremony himself without
witnesses —that is to say, that he. asked
himself: “Will I take this woman?” and
replied to himself that he would. It is also
alleged that he conspired with a disrepu-
table dentist; that they jointly and severally
killed the girl, disembowelled her, cut her in
various pieces, and scattered the fragments.
Further, it is declared that he is mad, that
he is a notorious swindler, a hardened
pickpocket, and an expert maker of
bogus money, and that he isn't the
xvev. Hans Schmidt at all, the real person
of that name having died some time ago.
And when it is found out that Anna was
somebody else, and that the shadowy dentist
is '’dually a blacksmith, the tale of this
unholy Smith of New York will be about
complete. Perlians, after all. there is no
such place as New York. It may be an
optical illusion.
The latest application to The Bulletin
for a job :
I wish to be on your staff, as District Representa-
tive or Occasional Contributor. I have written some
2000 arts, for 800 odd papers.
1 am member of Astronomical Society, Unitarian
Church, Sons of Temperance, Chess Club, Y.M.C.A.,
Engineers, British Maidens’ Club, Old Country Club,
Over-Seas Club, the Emmanuel Society, and regu-
larly attend all Organ Recitals and Royal Society
Lectures.
I have had a good industrial experience, having
worked for such firms as Armstrong Whitworth, Swan
Hunter, Wm. Gray, Furness Withy, Robt. Stephenson,
J. W. Spencer, Walter Scott, N E. Railway, Shear-
ers, etc. Worked in shipbuilding yards on the
Mauretania and warships; in Marine Engine Works ;
Dockyards, Locomotive Shops, Steel Works, Rolling
Mills, Paper Mills, Packing Factories, Plow Works,
Brass Shops, Claypits, Coal-mines, Contractors’
yards, Gunshops, and other departments of industry.
• I was two years reporting, for a daily, and served
six months to the printing in a jobbing house. Here
I did all the shipping, industrial and political and
religious work.
I have studied naval architecture, machine draw-
ing, mathematics, phonography, steam, astronomy,
electricity, geology and other subjects in evening
classes at Elswiek Works School, Durham College of
Science, Durham University, West Hartlepool Tech-
nical School, Newcastle Lit. and Phil. Soc., New-
burn Working Men’s Club, Walker Mechanics Insti-
tute, The Polytechnic, Natural History Museum and
Geographical institute.
For a while I was in business as a sewing-machine
repairer at Hartlepool, Haswell Moor and Haswell
Plough. I was connected with the I.L.P. Club,
Liberal Club and many societies aiming (and largely
failing) to reform humanity.

(1) Astronomy for Boys.
(2) Notes and Notions (Comic Comment).
(3) A Dicky Bird Society, conducted by Uncle
John.
(4) Ideals (one weekly: “Church,” “Newspaper,”
etc ).
(5) Adventures of a Rolling Stone.
I would be willing to do any other lines. Very
moderate payment. I am 35 years of age, single
and life-abstainer, and mix with all sorts and con-
ditions of people.

THE MULTIPLICATION CLASS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We have had examples that warn us. In a country
district there was a church of ours that gave
promise of a great spiritual awakening. Just at
that time a quadrille class was formed, and the
voung farmers were glad of something to relieve
the monotony of their lives. Within 12 months of
the formation of the class 12 children were born out
of wedlock in the district, and the moral lapses
were traced to the influence of the dancing That
sort of thing made them careful.
— Rev. John Adams, Methodist, of Melbourne.
Our young and careless nation
Is so void of population,
For its wooded plains and valleys, and its hills,
That we look to immigration
To relieve the situation ;
But there’s hope of sure salvation
In Quadrilles.
’Tis the dancing brings together
All the birdies of a feather,
And the Jacks they come a-courting giddy Jills ;
Shod with dew-defying leather,
Laughing gaily at the weather,
Couples wander midst the heather
From Quadrilles.
Ah! the maiden agitation,
The poetic palpitation,
The heart-to-heart confessions and the thrills!
Nature offers sweet temptation,
And a newer generation
Owes its subsequent creation
To Quadrilles.
True, the parson grimly glances
At the girls who go to dances,
But their minds ignore the doctrines he instills ;
For they need their brief romances
(Human facts are formed of fancies)
And they get delightful chances
At Quadrilles.
VICTORIA.

THE PARTY POLITICIAN’S DREAM.
THE CAT: “Do you remember drowning a kitten some time ago?”
THE PARTY POLITICIAN: “I do!”
THE CAT: “Well, I’m that kitten.”

GASPING FOR BREATH.
“Several times we nearly lost our son Bert with
croup, for he was choking and gasping for breath,”
writes Mr. P. Balmer, draper, Launceston, Tas.,
“and I have had to get up in the night and go for
a bottle of Chamberlain’s Cough Remedy. After a
good dose of this wonderful remedy there would be a
marvellous difference in Bert, and I have never
known one bottle to fail to completely cure him.”*

The will pf,“Dr.*’ E. H. T. Bottrell, the

From a Western pressman:—
Even if Henry Chinn wasn’t much of an engineer,
he might have pulled through but for his abominably
rude manners. Reporters in search of information
were hooted out of his office, and it was indicated
that only editors should approach the great man.
The newspapers therefore gave him cold steel, and
described him, as he showed himself —with many
failings and no apparent virtues. Added to all this,
O'Malley called him “Brother,” and boasted of his
prowess as a pugilist. When O’Malley sings a
man’s praises, he subtracts 75 per cent, of his real
value; and this left poor Chinn too small a margin to
work on.
The Rev. Cherub Bevan, formerly of Mel-
boure and now of Adelaide, possesses a rare
literary treasure—four volumes of MS.
records covering many centuries of the his-
tory of Exeter Cathedral (England). He
acquired them quite honestly from a dealer,
but a description of them in the Adelaide
papers has caused a stir in Exeter. The
Express and Echo of the little city
(26/7/T3) talks darkly of “carrying the
matter further,” and says: “It would cer-
tainly be a serious thing if the facts prove
to be as stated. The hope will be general
that they are not.” What is the exact quan-
tity of boiling oil allotted to. the offence of
being in possession of a mediaeval cathedral
record which the cathedral hadn’t sense
enough to hang on to?

“M,” on the fringe of the peerage:—
The Viscount Wolmer who has been gaining art
easy notoriety lately by getting into “holts” (in the
newspapers) with the fiery little Welshman, Lloyd
George, provides a very good example of how close
the Cold Country still is to the feudal system. Wol-
mer is the eldest son of the Earl of Selborne (who-
was a member of the last Tory Cabinet, and, later,.
High Commissioner in S’Africa), but is otherwise
perfectly innocuous. His career at Oxford was re-
markable for nothing but unsuccessful efforts to be-
come President of the Union. Almost as soon as he
went down from Oxford, however, he was presented
to a Lancashire constituency, which dutifully re-
turned him at the 1910 general election. He was-
then only 23 years old. His younger brother, R. S.
Palmer, has the brains of the family. Their con-
nection with the Cecils (the Cecil family is a King
Charles’s head to Lloyd George) is through their
mother, who is a sister of the present Marquess of
Salisbury. The first Selborne Earl (Wolmer’s grand-
father) was a Successful lawyer who became Lord
Chancellor, and rather an able one at that. Wolmer
did not inherit too much of his ability. Yet a con-
stituency can generally be found for any Earl’s son
who prefers politics to the attractions of the Em-
pire promenade.
Gone on her last journey, Mrs. Donald
Cameron, of Lowestoft (Tas.), aged 82, and
just back from Europe. She was a well-
known figure in Hobart Society, and a
woman who did things in
a land where very many
things are left undone. A
great tripper across the
world, she usually fetched
back something of inter-
est to stockbreeders. In
particular, she did mucn
to improve the strain of
Jersey cattle in Tas-
mania. She was also in-
terested in birds (she
introduced pheasants
and other varieties of
“game”) and dogs.
Colonel Cameron, of the
S’African war and the
Australian Senate, and
Norman Cameron, who
held the balance of power
last session in Tasmanian politics, are sons;
of hers.
Frederic Chappie, who has been head-
master of Prince Alfred College, Adelaide,
for 37 years, retires shortly. Four of his
sons and two of his daughters are graduates
of Adelaide University, while three of the
sons have also graduated at Cambridge.
The little bit of flummery which the
N.S.W. Full Court allowed itself when it
welcomed various members of the British
Parliamentary party to the bar of the State
recalls that the first barristers admitted to
practise in N. S. Wales were W. C. Went-
worth and Robert Wardell, Ltd. _ Both had
practised in England. Chief Justice Forbes
nodded them in, and didn’t make any fuss
about it either.
“T. 0.1.”: A Sydney daily recently pub-
lished an article on the Borough of Wool-
lahra, giving a long history of the present
Government House, Cranbrook. Mention
was made of James White, of racing fame,
who lived there, but not one word was said
about the pioneer who built the mansion—a
pioneer who was a big man, politically, soci-
ally and commercially. He was Cantain
Robert Towns, of Towns and Co. When
Cowper wanted to flood the. Legislative
Council, which had then .only a few weeks to
live, the President (Burton) resigned, and
Bobby Towns, calling out “Follow the
leader,” led the Opposition out of the Cham-
ber. The object of the flooding of the Coun-
cil on that occasion was to pass the Robert-
son Land Bill.
Maoriland is exporting brains as well as
butter. Some time ago the Hartford, New-
baven and New York Railway Company of-
fered 10,000 dollars for the best invention
calculated to control che speed of trains
whose drivers have lost their memories, or
•gone to sleep, and passed signals fixed against
them. Of -1554 dodges submitted from all
parts of the earth, only fiVe per cent, were
found to be at all like the goods wanted, and
the five was at last reduced to two, one of
which is the invention of W. T. Ashton,
machinist at the Egmont Box Company’s
mill, Ohutu, M.L. If Ashton doesn’t win the
HJ.S. “dollars,” his invention will be a dead
*efl.e>rihant on his hands in louland. There,
wheai a train bolts, the driver merely runs
sahea-d to the next station and has her side-
-tracked on arrival.
“F.H.” asks us to consider Norman Bayles,
Ticjtarian M.L.A., and Toorak’s only Parlia-
mentarian :
A youngish, mincing sort of person, with an
aide-de-camp’s moustache, a simper when applauded,
a pair of fluttery hands, and speech like cracked
ice shaken in a thin tumbler. The general impres-
sion he gives you is that his real name is Eric
Eauntlerov. Addressing a meeting at the Malvern
Town Hall prior to the last State elections, he
caused screams of laughter bv reading a letter re-
ceived from Watt, now Premier. “I wrote to Mr.
Watt,” burbled Norman, “and this is what I wrote:
‘Dear Willie.’ ” He got no further for a while.
When the noise subsided, the orator went on with the
letter, and then read Watt’s reply. The latter began
'with “Dear Norman.” More joyous uproar—at which
Norman giggled, and fluttered his hands, and giggled
some more. Hastily rising the other day to prove
his loyalty to Home and Mother, he referred to an
alleged statement of a “disloyal” member to the
•effect that he “never removed his hat when the
■National Anthem was being played.” Norpian: “If
he had said that in the hearing of my father, he
-would have had no inclination to sit down when the
'Anthem was being played.” It is one of
Norman’s habits to drag in his noor old parent in
the most unnecessary manner. When he was elected
-member for Toorak, he almost went as he offered up
thanks from the balcony of the Malvern Town Hall,
telling them that he deeply regretted that his d-dear
■old d-dad was not 'there to witness his triumph.
Norman’s departed dad is a card be loves to play,
, and he has played him so often that the old fellow
is getting quite frayed at the edges.
RICHARD
ECKHOLD,
the Wagnerian
conductor of the
Quinlan orchestra.
Tlie Rev. Dr. Fitchett has been lecturing
at the Vic. National Gallery on “The Litera-
ture of the Great War.” For a parson and
an apostle of peace, the Rev. Fidget has an
extraordinary literary craving for blood-
shed. Most of his books deal with dead bodies
and sudden death. His muse would appear
to fee a turbulent Amazon, with a torch in
one hand and a pig-sticker in the other,
inadequately disguised in a pastor’s coat and
■choker. What is this man doing as a meek
and lowly Follower? He should be a gun
designer for Ivrupp’s, or the leading spirit
at a thriving slaughter-house.

Jeremiah Melbourne, age 91, has just died
in the city that happened to bear his name.
For t>3 years Jeremiah was a leader in the
Christian Israelite Sanctuary, Fitzroy, where
the men have their long hair tucked up and
the women are in bonnets. In his- spare time
he was a stonemason. He was a trusty
associate' of John Wroe, the leader of the
Christian Israelites, Beardies or Wroeites.
John, who was the successor of Johanna
Southcotte. lived for some time at Fitzroy,
and his following was moneyed enough
to build for him Melbourne House, at Ash-
ton, Yorkshire (England). It seems the
New Jerusalem was to be somewhere about
there. Fisher, the “Nunawading Messiah,”
tried to get the succession to Wroe, but was
foiled by Jeremiah Melbourne and the rest.
So he founded the Church of the First Born,
with 120 men and women, incorporating the
theories of Christianity. Spiritualism and
Mormonism. That sect is dead, but the
Christian Israelite Church still flourishes in
moderation. One notable of the creed used
to be conspicuous in Sydney, but on a cer-
tain evil, wet and slippery day he stepped
by accident on his hair, and fell, and broke
his neck.

THE IMPERVIOUS ICEBERG.
[For The Bulletin.]
Mr. Greenwood (Pakenham) observed that what-
ever shafts were aimed at Mr. Irvine, he vvould re-
ceive them on his manly breast (cheers) and they
would fall dead at his feet. (Loud cheers.)
—Press report.
I saw him stand, a Polar man,
Cold anger in his frigid eye,
Facing a wild, unruly clan
Who poised their fiery shafts on high.
Strangely, his very coldness fed
The angry flame ’gainst such as he ;
For in his wintry face they read
Antarctic immobility.
The niveous hauteur of that face
Bespoke the brumal inner man ;
And, in its chill hyemal grace,
His pose was quite Siberian.
His and hibernal gaze
Seemed like twin icicles to strike.
The whole man was, in many ways,
Peculiarly cucumber-like.
Ilis algid and unruffled brow
Gleamed frostily, and, as he eyed
His raging foes, he seemed, I vow,
Gelidity personified.
His Greenland bosom bulged with pride:
A manly bosom ’twas withal—
And, as he breathed, with glacial glide
I watched his waistcoat rise and fall.
I marked the arctic arrogance
With which he faced his foemen bold.
Bleak was his mien ; clay-cold his glance.
Some vowed his very feet were cold.
I saw his savage foemen poise
Their fiery javelins on high.
(They made a fearful lot of noise.)
“Slay! Slay the Iceberg!” was the cry.
.And then, as by a single hand
Propelled, I saw the keen shafts fly,
And on that manly bosom land.
“This is his funeral,” thought I.
Nay, by my halidame! What’s this?
Upon his breast the hot shafts beat,
But with a fierce and baffled hiss
Drop all innocuous at his feet.
Unscathed he stood, the Man of Ice.
Each shaft, with torrid anger fired,
Just spluttered feebly once or twice,
Then ignominiously expired.
One look he gave them, that was all—
It made his shiv’ring foemen feel
That wintry blasts swept through the hall—
Then turned on his hyemal heel.
One word he spake, one icy word —-
No man among them stood exempt—
It froze each listener who heard
With hyperborean contempt.
It froze them with its brumal blast;
Like avalanches there it rang.
Then through the door he calmly passed,
And banged it with a snow-clad bang.
So, frigidly, he won the day.
And, when the blizzard blast was o’er,
Lo, nought remained to mark the fray
But shafts, deceased, upon the floor.
And, as I passed into the night,
I heard one baffled foe aver :
“ ’Tis sheer futility to fight
An iceberg with a lucifer!”
VICTORIA.
DEN.

“And please don’t misrepresent us, as usual,’’ says
the secretary, forwarding- the accounts in response
to a belated application. Well, this paper doesnlt
want to. Swallow and Ariell, Ltd., is a good old
institution which has done a lot for Australian
industry ; hence this paper is full of sympathy as it
approaches the operating table. But the sympathy
doesn’t last. Twenty-five years ago this co. was
formed; yet still taking its lead among the assets
is this entry: “Original purchase at cost from’ the
firm of Swallow and Ariell of freehold land, build-
ings, machinery, plant, etc., including goodwill,
£148,500.” Frankly, among the accounts of com-
panies of as good repute as Swallow and Ariell, The
Bulletin can’t recall a more unsatisfactory item
than this.. For 25 years, according to these accounts,
the original assets, including plant and buildings
as well as goodwill, have been kept in at their
original cost, and not one penny has been written
off them to cover depreciation. Additions made since
then are shown separately, and something—though
something that looks extremely moderate—has been
written off these newer assets on account of deprecia-
tion. If off the newer, then why not off the older?
It may be that the freehold land has increased
enormously in value. Very well; then revalue it,
drop it in “at valuation,” and use the increment to
write off the plant which has been long since
worn out and the machinery which has been long since
scrapped; and if there is anything over, use it to
write down the goodwill. For nothing is much
surer than this: If the goodwill is worth £lOO,OOO
to-day, the whole of it is due to the exertions of
the present co., and not to those of the old firm
which departed life 25 years ago. The old firm may
have made splendid cakes in 1880; but if the co.
hadn’t made good cakes during the past 25 years, the
old reputation of Swallow and Ariell wouldn’t be
worth two eggshells to-day. So it is about time
the old goodwill item was attacked. A good oppor-
tunity for a general cleaning-up offered when 20,400
pref. shares were issued during the year to share-
holders at par. They are 6 p.c. cumulative, and
are entitled to share with ordinaries in any division
beyond 6 pc. There is a fine, solid backing for the
capital of this issue, and the dividend prospects are
likewise good. The extra capital was certainly
wanted, for the co.’s pretty heavy debts have been
growing steadily as the business has expanded:—-
Liabilities.
„ 1905. 1911. 1912. 1913.

Profits are very irregular, and in the years of the
last big’ drought, when the co.’s supplies were extra-
costly, they were changed into a considerable loss;
nevertheless in the past 10 years a profit of £53,378
has been made in excess of the average dividend of
slightly over 34 p.c.; and £53,378 is equal to
another 34 p.c. Whether that profit is net —that is to
say, whether enough has been written off to cover
depreciation—it is not possible to say from the
(Published accounts. The reserves, it will be noticed
: are described as nominal. That, of course, is because
,of the inclusion of the hypothetical goodwill asset and
■because also “buildings, machinery and plant,” pur-
chased 25 years ago, are still taken credit for at
their original cost. There may be some real re-
serves; in other words, the tangible net assets mav
-now exceed the capital. But one can say nothing
. about this until the accounts are presented in a
,different form.

It’s an unsatisfactory way of putting it; but it’s the
best that can be done with the raw materiaL
In ordinary circumstances it would be possible to
compute the amount of dividend, even if the bonus
to customers had to be guessed at; but in this
case capital is being got in pretty continuously, and
as there is always a certain amount which carries
the dividend for only part of the year, the total
can’t be arrived at definitely. All that
can be said is that the 6 p.c. dividend
on the £150,000 of pref. capital will take £9000;
the ordinary-div. will call for about £13,600; and,
on a rough calculation, the various bonuses will
absorb £22,600. This will give £8049 to carry for-
ward, and will bring the reserves to £113,159. The
bonus may be a bit over-estimated, in which case,
of course, the reserves are understated.
The co. does all the usual business of a farmers’
co-operative co. It handles practically all that the
farmer produces for sale, and sells him practically
all his trade requisites. Of course this means money
—money for merchandise stock, and money for ad-
vances against consignments, etc. Fortunately, how-
ever, the co. hasn’t been starved of capital like the
corresponding S. Australian co. and some others, and,
partly no doubt on that account, it has been able
to show consistently good profits. Dividends have,
moreover, been kept down reasonably, so, besides
the capital, there is a decent reserve fund to work
on. The bonus varies; on purchases of merchandise
shareholders get as a rule 5 p.c. discount, and up
to SO p.c. rebate of commissions. Twelve years’
history is summarised in the next comparison:—
Liabilities.
1901. 1911. 1912. 1913.
Capital . . £60,988 £239,272 £308,382 £313,000
Deposits . . 74,199 208,423 206,391 242,967
Other liabilities 81,611 192,556 213,390 251,480
Assets.
Advances . . 99,523 285,456 302,806 351,011

Freeholds, etc. 33,824 112,146 120,908 132,630

Cash . . . . 11,947 52,139 74,844 27,825
Capital and reserves amount to, say, £425,000.
Liabilities total £494,447. The liabilities are thus
not at all excessive for this class of business, especi-
ally as more than half of them consist of amounts
to the credit of customers’ accounts—balances owing
on sales made on behalf of clients, and represented
on the other side mostly by book debts and cash.
No doubt there are risks in the business ; still, in a
fairly safe district, the farmer can put up better
security than almost any borrower in ordinary
, trade. Certainly a fool can lend too much on land,
ajjd make, tremendous losses thereby; but that’s bad
management, and over a long spell of years this co.
has dope so consistently well that it evidently
hasp’,t got that sort of management.

When this co. was 20 years old it had a deficit of
£6084. That is to say, very nearly one-third of its
then capital had disappeared. Since then, though it
managed in one year to make a loss equal to more
than one-half of its capital at the time, it has struck
patches of the other kind of luck, and has £24,832
of a surplus. Or rather it had or believed it had that
muclf on June 30 ; what it has now may be another
matter. Wheat-dealing is the trouble. The co. is a
co-operative concern in a sense; but the wheat farmer
whose stuff has mostly to be sold in Europe—in
prolific seasons, anyway—won’t or can’t wait till the
slow trains have gradually, shifted the stuff to port
and the slow ships have gradually shifted it on to
London or Liverpool. The co. has to buy straight
out, as a rule; and though it may sell in London
the. next day, there is so much gambling in this
business that even then heavy losses or profits may
very easily be made. Four or five years ago Clement
Giles, who had been a director and one of the main-
stays of the co,, was sent to England to handle the
wheat business there—really to guess what the market
was going to be like to-morrow and the day after
and the month after that. It may be on this account
that, though Giles's first year in London showed a
heavy loss, there has been a run of three profitable
years since. Considering the risks of the business
and the turnover, the capital was far too small, and
efforts to get in more money had poor results. Lat-
terly, no doubt largely because of the fat profits
in 1911 and 1912, money has been more plentiful,
and £50,585 is now paid up; but that isn’t half
enough. As at June 30 the co. owed £300,295 on
various accounts, and against that it held wheat,
produce, hills, etc. It is now 25 years old; and a
co. which has to borrow or owe so much money in
order to hold stocks liable to such fluctuations, and
take the other risks of the wheat trade, ought at
that age to have a reserve fund of £lOO,OOO. The
Bulletin doesn’t know why it hasn’t got £lOO,OOO.
Probably because the competition in the business is
too keen. Still, a tidy reserve should by this time
have been built up out of the other business of the
co. ; and that there was less than no reserve at all a
few years ago seems fair proof that the wheat busi-
ness had been on the whole very unprofitable. Things
may be better now ; still this paper has grave doubts
whether a co. of this sort should touch wheat at all
except on strictly co-operative, non-speculative lines,
hven if it dropped this part of the business, sup-
posing it absolutely can’t be worked on true co-
operative lines, there would still be plenty of jobs
for the co. to do —especially in the supply of corn-
sacks, manures and other merchandise, and in hand-
ling wool, skins and stock, for which the buyers are
on the spot.

The reserves are really more than that. During the
past half-year some further £1 shares were offered
to shareholders at £1 premium, and the balance-
sheet shows this item: “New capital and reserve
subscribed to date, £22,210.” Fortunately the
High Court the other day blew out the preposterous
claim of the Queensland income-tax people that
premiums paid to a co. for its shares were profit
liable to income-tax, so there is no reason now why
capital and premium shouldn’t be separated in these
accounts. Allowing that the new capital proper is
£11,105, the total stands at £24,989 —amazingly
little for a concern doing such a business as “the
Stores.” Here you get a view of the turnover and
a few other things:—

The prompt-cash sale is, of course, the king-pin of
the business ; at July 31 customers’ debits amounted
to only £8751, owing, no doubt, mainly on the com-
paratively few monthly accounts. The discount
allowed on these accounts is 9d. in the £ ; on cash
purchases the discount was formerly 2s. 3d., but, on
account of increased costs, it has now been brought
down to 2s. Even that seems a shade too much to
pay, since it allows of a very poor lay-by. No doubt
for general purposes a small addition to reserves is
sufficient in a case of this kind, inasmuch as there
are no bad debts to provide for, and practically no
investments to protect. Still, a reserve is a handy
thing to have about the premises, and especially
handy in a quickly growing business, which wants
not only to increase its stock, but also to pay cash
for that stock and get every penny of discount it
can lay its hands on, so that, in their turn, share-
holder-customers may have every penny of discount
that cash trading will yield. As a of fact,
trade has moved along a bit too fast for the C.O.D.
principles of the co., wherefore one way and another
it owes now £36,591, whereas 10 years ago it owed
only £24,116 ; and this despite two small' increases
of capital since 1903. The co. could, in fact, have
done very well with larger payments to its reserve
all the time it was handing out its too-lordlv ,2s. 3d.
in the £. The co. owns no freehold, but has put a
good deal of money into property held on a long
lease. These items (irrespective 0f... furniture and
fittings) stand at £20,975, and are being steadily
and properly reduced, the profit thus applied being
used to wipe out a mortgage ( £12,250) on the
leasehold. In the first table, by the way, it has been
assumed that £425 of the bonuses on purchases will
not be collected. As a rule, it works out to about
that much.

The Bulletin hadn’t proceeded far into the ;in-
nards of this co.’s accounts before it got a shock;
the directors had actually used £lOOO of profit in
1908 to reduce an overdraft, and had then done the
correct thing by writing down assets to make the
accounts square. This paper’s subsequent explora-
tion was therefore proceeded with in a friendly
spirit, and nothing whatever was discovered to turn
that spirit sour. Certainly the co.’s overdraft has
grown, but it is still not burdensome-; it amounted
to £5015 on June 30, and other liabilities totalled
£74lB—together £12,433. On the other side, apart
from three factory premises, machinery, and such
fixed assets, the co. had at that date £5115 in cash,
£5284 in book debts and £3581 in stock—together
£13,980. Right through, premises, plant, etc.,
have, been kept very well written down to cover de-
preciation, so that the co. with a present capital of
£8445 has probably more than the surplus or re-
serves of £6037 mentioned above. Moderate bonuses
have been paid to ,the suppliers; but they are ap-
parently not such a "greedy and short-sighted lot as
many alleged co-operators are —they haven’t insisted
on grabbing every available penny of surplus profits,
but. have permitted the co. to get into a strong
position. This, of course, not only allows the co. to
do more for the industry and for shareholders, but
also gives the suppliers a valuable guarantee that
no ordinary disaster will affect future payments.
Altogether it is one of the most cheerful and satis-
factory histories supplied by any co-operative butter
co. in Australia.

Visible profits, which took a big jump in 1911-12,
fell away again during the past year; and the old
explanation is given—incrSised costs. The position
is simply that for a while -this co. got an advantage
from higher wages and more abundant work without
having to pay out its own share of higher wages,
etc. Now what it will have to look forward to is
this: when work becomes less abundant and the
beer-purchasing power of the State falls, the higher
wages will still have to be paid by this co. for a
while at least. So, just as the 1912 profits were
abnormally high, the time will come when under
opposite conditions they will be abnormally low; and
many other trading concerns will find themselves in
the same boat. Meantime, however, the handful of
beer-manufacturers in Brisbane have come to an
honorable understanding with the object of passing
on some of the extra cost. They fought like Kil-
kenny cats a while ago, but all is peace now.
This, like every other Brisbane brewery, got into
trouble during Queensland’s last big borrowing
boom, and found itself loaded with assets at such
inflated values that when sanity returned and a
re-valuation was made, about half the Capital had
to be written off. Even then the co. was none too
sure that it had got to rock-bottom; wherefore it
dropped dividends altogether, and when it resumed
them did things modestly. The result is partly
shown by this :

The hotel property has been almost all cleared out
—a wise thing, no doubt, especially in these days
when the Cold Water lion goes up and down seeking
liquor licenses to devour. But if he devours licenses
and shuts hotels, he doesn’t make much progress in
reducing the thirst for beer; so the outlook for the
co. that sticks to the brewery business and doesn’t
speculate in pubs might be a lot gloomier. There
are 150,000 shares in the co., all of 12s. 6d., about

Like most of them, this co. got its lesson after the
boom, when securitjes were thrown on its hands
wholesale. It nursed the properties as long as it
decently could without formally counting up its
losses ; then it re-valued them, and wrote off about
half of its capital to make good the deficiency As
it happened, the re-valuation was made when the
tide was well out; and when it started to come
back again in 1905 or thereabouts, it brought some
flotsam with it. This is shown as “profit on pro-
perty sales”—really surpluses over the written-down
values. As these surpluses accumulated, some of
the written-off capital was restored, which was a
reasonable thing to do. There is not much of the
old wreckage left, .so not a great deal further can
be expected from surpluses; the total now is only
£15,947 —little more than one-fifth of what the
society had on hand when the re-valuation was com-
pleted in 1901. As the properties have been sold
new advances have been made. This shows the
movements: —
July, July, Jan., July,
1901. 1912. 1913. 1913.
Liabilities £37,015 £42,244 £40,950 £3B 657
Advances.. .. ."19,998 105,317 106,509 105,957
Foreclosures .. *-72,305 17,990 16,744 15 947
Cash, etc. .. 2,778 171 163 ’284
The liabilities consist mainly of deposits; but the
society is doing vfery little new business in that way,
either because the money isn’t available or because
the directors are not satisfied that they can safely
lend other people's money on the security of pro-
perties at their present high values. So long as the
society can make its present excellent profits while
guaranteeing so little of other people’s money, share-
holders may reckon themselves very lucky; not many
other concerns in this line of business can do it.
And, of course, it is a very pleasant position for
-Such depositors as there are, for they have the
satisfaction of holding a first mortgage on assets
worth more than three times the debt.
