Having constructed the tabJe, The Bulletin con-
fesses that it doesn’t think much of the job. Of the
alleged profits made the past year (£6521),
£4021 has nominally beet! added to reserves; and
these additions are dispospcf as follows; To depre-
ciation fund (for works on leasehold), £700; to
general renewal fund, £2720; to meter renewal
fund, £7OO. Total, £4120, which means that it
has been necessary to draw on the other reserves
(consisting of unappropriated profit) to the extent
of £99. In other, words, if it has been necessary
to make these appropriations to special reserves in
order to counterbalance depreciation, then the net
profits have fallen £99 short of the 10 pc. div., and
instead of the true reserves being added to, they
have been actually drawn upon during the year.
Apparently nothing is written off against deprecia-
tion ; these reserves are the only set-off against that
wear and tear. Certainly the balance-sheet says—
“ Mains and services at cost (less depreciation),” but
this apparently means, “Mains and services at cost
(less depreciation written off to December, 1908),”
which is a very different thing. From another point
of view the business looks a trifle better:—
June, 1908. June, 1913.
Liabilities .. .. £2,677 £4,979
Fixed assets . . . . 30,751 43,483
Liquid assets . . . . 5,579 8,651
Thus in five years the surplus of more or lees liquid
assets has been rather better than maintained, and
about £13,000 of revenue has been put into pro-
perty and plant, most of it nominally as a set-off
against depreciation. There is no telling for cer-
tain, but it looks like an ample provision. _ As for
the future, it is now a foregone conclusion that
Victorian gas cos. will be put under much the
same restraint as those of Great Britain have been
under for many years, and as the principal ones in
N. S. Wales have been since this year started. It
may be that for a start the standards as to price,
dividends, etc., will be made to apply to only one
or two big cos. ; but that must affect the others,
too, for once they do what the cos. which are under
restraint are not allowed to do, there will inevitably
be an outcry to bring the lot under the Act.

Amalgamated Zinc (De Bavay’s), Ltd-

at Broken Hill: — ' .

De Bavay was a Melbourne brewery chemist who, w
common with some other chemists, worked on
the suggestion that certain metals might be
separated from their gangue by fine grinding ana
subsequent flotation. The crushed stuff is mixed witn
water, and oil (eucalyptus oil is now used) ana
acid are introduced. The pulp is agitated; the acia
attacks the metal particle, and forms a balloon, witn
the particle as the passenger-basket underneath; aua
the oil helps the ascent. The metal, risen to the
top, is floated off as a froth. Hence the term flota-
tion process, or, as it is sometimes called, the frotn-
ing or scum process. There was plenty of money
behind De Bavay ; the brewery people stood to him.

A great mistake, when erecting new works or re-
arranging old ones, is often made in constructing the
building and installing machinery ere looking round
for means of transport of the raw material, half-
manufactured and finished goods. Messrs. Adolf
Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various
catalogues and leaflets, illustrating and describing a
few of the many transport systems and plants which
they have supplied, and which show the solution of
the transport question and the advantages of em-
ploying such appliances. W. and J. Lempriere,
Melbourne and Sydney, are the Australian representa-
tives, and would be pleased to hear from those in-
terested. A specially trained engineer is retained to
consult and advise.*

Messrs. Mephan Ferguson Proprietary, Limited, of
Footscray, Victoria, have secured the contract for
the supply of steel pipes required in connection with
the duplication of the Bombay Water Supply. The
contract amounts to over £226,000, and the pipes
to be used are of the locking bar type, of which Mr.
Mephan Ferguson is the inventor and patentee.
Engineers will be interested to note that this Aus-
tralian invention has been adopted in preference to
other classes of piping, after critical and searching
inquiries as to the merits of the various types of
water pipes made in the world. It is worthy of
note that all machinery required to carry out this
work has been designed and manufactured at. the
firm’s Footscray Engineering Works, and will be
shipped to Bombay, where the pipes will be manu-
factured.*

Also, the Baillieus, who had been interested in the
Zinc Corporation at an earlier stage, transferred their
affections to De Bavay, and a co. was formed to run
a mill. There was a lot of experimenting to be
done; when it had been finished this co. was formed
to run new works, the first unit of which was started
in March, 1910. Meantime, good contracts had been
made with the B. H. North and B. H. South for the
supply of zinc-bearing tailings.
It has been an amazingly profitable business during
the past 24 years, thanks largely to exceptional metal
prices. In the first half of 1911 spelter averaged
£23 14s. 8d ; for the next three terms the average
was £26 11s. 7d., £25 18s. 2d. and £26 8s B<l
Last half-year there was a fall to £24 11s. 5d and
the profits show the result. But the decline of
£62.542 was not altogether due to that. There was
a hold-up of all traffic on the Broken Hill railway
for a fortnight or so while 8000 or 10,000 men
argued whether three or four petty bosses should be
compelled to join the clerks’ union or not. The co
produces a zinc concentrate which it sells to Euro-
pean smelters, and the final adjustment of prices is
not made until some months after the stuff is pro-
duced. The co. takes credit for product not finally
settled for at £22 per ton. Last half-year it got so
much more than £22 that £32,840 fell into the
profits on account of the previous term’s output. This
current half-year, spelter has been below £22 and
at latest it was £2l. This means that in all pro-
bability last half-year’s profits were over-estimated
and consequently the current half-year’s profits will
be diminished by the amount of the over-estimate
If, for the current half-year, the average price should
be £2l, it would mean cutting approximately £ 9150
off this half-year’s profits. Reserves are, however
strong; the greater part of them has been built up
to meet any serious trouble of this kind. Dividends
may therefore not be affected so seriously as might
otherwise be the case. The bulk of these reserves is
liquid: liquid assets on June 30 were £96,127 in
excess of liabilities. These assets do not include
tailings paid for but not treated (496,324 tons
equal to nearly one year’s requirements), nor is
credit, taken for the co.’s interest in Minerals Separa-
tion and De Bavay’s Processes, Ltd., although a
growing revenue is being derived from this source.
* * * *
The old Union Steamship Co. of Maori-
land is dead and a new one is born. A funeral and
a birth offered the simplest way to a majestic bit of
stock-watering, though it is stock-watering of the
least objectionable kind. The co. for some years
prior to 1907 had a paid capital of £800,000;' but
its reserved profits were so large that it then capi-
talised £200,000, and went ahead paying 9 p.c. on
the £1,000,000. And still the reserves went' on
mounting—some of them visible, the rest of them
internal. Lately it was decided that the co. should
transfer another £1,000,000 of these undivided pro-
fits to capital, making the total £2,000,000; but
as the authorised limit of the old co. was £1,000,000
it was resolved to form a brand-new concern, nomi-
nally of £3,000,000 in £1 shares, reserving
1,000,000 to be issued as occasion requires. Thus
the old holders get one new ordinarv share and one
bonus share for every old share. The bonus shares
will carry a 54 p.c. cumulative preference. Says
managing-director Mills: “The increase of capital
will, of course, result in a lower rate of dividend
on the ordinary shares, but the combined dividends
on ordinary and bonus preference shares should cer-
tainly not be less than the customary dividend
hitherto paid.” The average profit for the past 10
years has been £122,700. This includes one or two
transfers of internal reserves to the profit account;
but it does not, of course, include the undisclosed
profits which have been transferred to the internal
reserves. A 54 p.c. div. on the new pref. shares
will call for £55,000. After paying that the aver-
age disclosed profit of the past 10 years would just
about allow of a dividend of 7 p.c. on the ordinary
shares.- There would, however, be nothing left to
add to visible reserves ; and it hasn’t been the co.’s
habit to pinch itself in that fashion. In fact, the
very great success of the co. has been due to the
tenacity with which the management has held to the
policy of moderate dividends, so giving itself big
reserves of undivided profit to be used in extending
the business. Thus in the past five years the div.
has never exceeded 9 p.c. on the £1,000,000 of
capital, equal to p.c. oh the capital as enlarged
to £2,000,000: h, -
In his report on the Carpathia. R. L. Jack
doesn’t indulge in any strenuous adjectives. For
the greater part the elaborate ~statement is a
description of the shafts, and alleged shafts put down
on the property. Some of these tidies, though called
shafts, are mere burrows. Ror instance, No. 3
shaft. It goes down almost - Vertically ■ for 12 or
14ft. Then the lode rolls to the sfcuth-west, and the
shaft, following it, becomes mow. like; a drive for
a few feet. At -about 40ft. the stone jgot very rich, t
and the alleged shaft accordingly opened out 'into
a chamber 9ft-. x 9ft. This, by the way, has'bedn
called “development work”-—there have been marly
humorists at Ardlethan.
The three statements of most value in the long
report may be put this way: (1) There are no ore
reserves-—that is, no ore has been exposed on three
sides. The shafts and burrows are in ore mostly.
Whether the ore goes back 2in. or 20ft. from the
shafts and burrows nobody knows. Jack gives it as
his “individual opinion,” notwithstanding the
absence of reserves and the impossibility of figuring
closely, that “the mines could easily repay in divi-
dends, from what is already known, the balance of
the capital subscribed, although it is always good
policy to lay aside a large sum for development.”
This, of course, doesn’t say much. The subscribed
capital is £52,035; paid capital only £38,814;
and £12,000 has been already repaid in dividends.
(2) Dr. Jack thinks there are (o) one main lode
running along a crush-zone at the junction of
porphyry and slate; (6) numerous lenses or pipes
in subsidiary fissures; and (c) lodes or lenses in
the porphyry and granite near their junction. (3)
The most important development work done so far
is the drive at the 100 ft. level. From the manager’s
notebook it appears that ttie ore in this bottom
working runs from 1.8 p.c. metallic tin (the low-
est) to 8.5 p.c. (the highest), and averages about
44 pc. Jack’s own samplings gave from one-tenth
of 1 p.c. to 17 p.c. ; but of six samples only the
one was over 5 p.c. This is no doubt payable even
under present conditions, but is a very different
Proposition from the 45 and 55 p.c. ore which has
hitherto been most talked of.
Below the gossan or leached zone the
Occidental, Cobar (N.S.W.), becomes heavily miner-
alised in places. Geologist Andrews, of the N.S.W.
staff, reported on the mine as a smelting proposi-
tion, but regarded the excess of silica as a diffi-
culty. Very silicious ore can, however, be concen-
trated, so there is still a chance that the copper will

been a case of too much top-hamper.

8., M. and Co. have their option eye on the
Sirdar, at Boogardie (W.A.). A big lode is visible
in the property, and values are alleged to average
405., or thereabouts. The shaft is, however, only a
little over 100 ft. deep, and while lodes often go
down values more often don’t. And they haven’t
shown any special eagerness to do so in the Boogardie
district.
o * * * *
The local inspector of mines reports that
the creep in the Associated Northern’s Ora Banda
show has been stopped. He thinks that so long as
the No. 2 level stopes are left alone no further
movement towards the shaft need be anticipated,
and he adds that the working of these stopes can be
considered later. The co. is pegging away at the
No. 5 level, and is still hopeful, and occasionally
sanguine, of picking up the continuation of the
rich stuff met with in the east end of the No. 4
level. At this depth values in the east drive ranged
from a few shillings to £5 for the first 40-odd ft.,
and averaged for that distance £2 per ton. From
there to 61ft. the grade was 755., and from 61ft. to
95ft. it was £4. Then came a sweetening patch,
which for some distance averaged between £lO and
£l2 per ton. From 32ft. to 113 ft. at the No. 5
level values have varied from £2 16s. to £4 55.,
and by some experts this is taken to mean that
the sweetening stuff does not last in depth.
Optimistic whoop from Yilgarn (W.A.) :
During 1911 the gold output from this field was
18,5960 z., and for 1912 it totalled 30,9320 z. This
•year, with four months still to go, it amounts to
over -51,0000 z., and it will average nearly 12,0000 z. a
month before the year ends. Bullfinch Prop., Moun-
tain Queen, Edna May and Corinthian North are at
present the chief sources of production, and with
the cyanide plant about to start a substantial in-
crease" may be looked for from the last-named almost
right away. Bullfinch’s profits are at present being
earned chiefly from the northern and southern makes
of ore, and "very little stuff has so far come from
the remnants of the original rich shoot.
Word is flashed from London that Golden
Horseshoe is on the sve of rejoining the list of divi-
dend payers. At the meeting in May the chairman
said there was every prospect of an early distribu-
tion, and since then it. has been whispered that the
co. was straining every nerve to pay out ss, a share
before the end of the year. On December 31 last
there was a cash balance of £35,602, and since then
profits have amounted to some £26,000. It would
take (including English and Australian div. duties)
£90,000 to make a ss. distribution.
Adelaide is credited with having raised
£l2OO to test the old Chaffinch lease at Bullfinch
(W.A.). The northern-lode series in the Proprie-
tary looks like trending into the Chaffinch, and
those who have found the money are gambling on
the chance of the drill striking good values at depth.

, W N ; Williams, manager of the Edna May
(W.A.), has been countering those who hold that,
because gramte bars have made a sick bird of the
(n am rich, on the same line of country, only a short
dt ls , rn iront of the Edna May. Williams’s rather
remarkable idea is that the granite intrusions are
responsible for the gold. Much more important is
it that the ore should average the expected 80s. per
tol i' e last crushing tapped out under the ounce,
and the most woolly-headed enthusiast won’t keep
warm if the expectations are watered down to that
extent, even though there be 6dwt. in the tailings
to draw on when a plant is available. So far,
manager Williams has got on all right with his shaft,
ana it the bottom isn’t troublesome he will soon
be able to talk about the 150 ft. Above the 73ft.
level the co. has 12 months’ ore in sight—-a good deal
less than the old florid estimate that bumped scrip
towards the fifties.
Hampden Cloncurry (Q.) isn’t happy,
and hasn t been happy for some time. When the
mines had undergone a careful preparation to enable
them to give the smelters a pretty good run, the big
new plant started in to chew up the reserves, and a
meteoric career was predicted. But discouraging
things happened, and the share market evidently ex-
pects more immediately ahead, for shares have fallen
five or six shillings in as many days. An official
report is issued by the co. to calm public fears, which
variously centre round the ore reserves, and the
working of the plant, and the water supply. But
the public distrustfully sniffs at the diplomatic ad-
missions. It never before was taken into the co.’s
confidence, and now feels strange. Well, there may be
no serious difficulty with the smelting plant, and
there may be nothing serious about the shortage of
water supply. Also, there may be the 255,000 tons
ot ore containing 25,000 tons of copper. How the
reserves are made up the last half-yearlv report
didn t reveal, so curiosity about the supplies of
basic ore can’t be satisfied. But the Cloncurry dis-
trlct is not too liberal with this needful commodity.
At first, the Hampden reckoned on a self-fluxing
mixture of Hampden and Duchess ore, but last half-
year the furnace charge contained over 30 p.c. barren
flux. The basic ore wasn’t as basic as it was thought
to be, so it mightn’t be as extensive, either. A co.
that has to scratch along, as this one has, to keep
out of debt, and to partly keep its promises, as a
rule does not start to treat its low-grade ore for
choice, unless that low-grade ore has some other
particular quality to recommend it. Last half-
year the extraction grade of all the ore treated was
11.7 p.c. copper, and under these conditions each ton
of copper produced cost £4B. As it contained £3 in
gold as well, the profit, with copper at £6O, was
£l5 per ton. The present reserves average about
10 p.c. copper, of which only nine-tenths at most
would be extractable. There is a big difference, in
the profit, between 11.7 p.c. and 9 p.c. ore; but
even allowing a profit of £l5, the copper in the
reserves would be worth £384,000, or under 225.
per share. If the basic ore gives out, any costs
might happen.
* * * *
Charters Towers has been enlivened by a
development in Kelly’s Queen Block, which reported
a new 3ft. make of “well mineralised stone” in a
footwall crosscut from No. 1 level on the St. George
reef (657 ft. vertical). Kelly’s last crushing gave
a disappointing average of 575. per ton. For six
months the total return was £1834 from 484 tons,
which did not pay expenses; hence Id. calls have
been regular. In view of this development the direc-
tors considerately extended the time limit for the
payment of arrears. Kelly’s has also a lease on the
Moonstone reef, but, generally speaking, Moonstone
developments have been disappointing.
Charters Towers can no longer claim an
average of loz. per ton. During eight months of
this year 18 mines crushed 53,188 tons for £173,110.
The local market gives the highest value
( £100,000) to Mills’ United (300,000 shares at
6s. Bd.). New Brilliant Freehold and the Brilliant
Deeps both carry 150,000 shares. At the moment
the Freehold is the favorite at about 9s. 6d., which
is the highest quotation on the Towers exchange. A
flat make of stone on the hanging-wall side of , No. 5
level has been proved for a distance of 136 ft. on the
underlay, and what is supposed to be a continuation
of this reef has been picked up in a crosscut- from
No. 6. No trial crushing has yet been taken from
it, and “fairly mineralised stone” is too indefinite a
description to trade on to any extent.
# * * *
Mammoth Copper (N.Q.) will start
another smelting campaign in October. Traction
engines will carry the matte to Mungana. Three of
the vehicles are in use and three more have been
purchased. The 160 ft. level is in 336 ft. south of
the shaft; the last sampling over a width of 29in.
gave 14.6 p.c. copper—further proof that in the
Mammoth the Glasgow-Australian co. has got hold
of a sound little mine.
There is really no need to be poor. A
Northern circular says that the Bowen River Coal
Co. (2000 shares of £1 each) “appears to have
struck a general junction of coal seams, and it is
only a question of time when this will be one of the
biggest coalfields of Australia.... Anthracite coal has
lately been found, and the seam has been named the
Bowen. Sinking is now being carried out to pick
up this seam, which is 17ft. wide, at a depth of
50ft.” And the shares in the wonder are worth
only 50s. Perhaps it’s all true; all the same, the
results of a few more borings would be very wel-
come.
If he can materialise various contemplated
additions and improvements to the plant, W. H.
Link, from Daylesford, now in charge of Sloan and
Scotchman’s, Stawell (Vic.), has hopes of making
the mine pay dividends. The one striking feature
of Sloan’s, so far, has been its consistent abstinence
from the dividend-yielding habit; so, if the new
hope is realised, the local populace will regard the
present manager as a link that was missing too long.
Mount Jasper (Tas.) is the show that
Frank Powell long since blasted with an arctic
breath. Nevertheless it is blooming again under the
nurturing care of the Millses, the Connors and the
Bryans; it would be easier to permanently blast
Home Rule than their faith in the Jasper. Its mis-
sion now is to furnish enough profits to put up a
plant for the Mount Stewart mine, as well as to de-
velop four lodes on a modest block of 681 acres.
Copper ore developed in the Jasper “old find” is put
at 20,000 tons ; but until a comprehensive sampling
is done it would be unwise to count on these reserves
yielding the capital the co. wants. The “new find”
is more quartzy than jaspery. It consists of a for-
mation 30ft. wide, carrying patches and veins of
rich ore; but so far the extent of the rich stuff
disclosed doesn’t justify shareholders in adding a
chauffeur to their household retinue. Why, too,
should it be necessary to talk of 12 to 20 p.c. ore
as “solid metal” ? This latest lot of hot air is
produced because of the sale of 90 tons for a profit
of £ll per ton, and because £9 per ton was netted
from 61 tons taken from the Heazlewood lode. Scraps
like these are pretty, but they don’t make a jnine.
The ore in the Tasmanian Copper Co.’s
mine (Tas.) is heavily charged with zinc in the sul-
phide zone. There is a contract with the Tasmanian
Metals Extraction 00., but that concern has taken
only small quantities, owing to the difficulty of
roasting the refractory stuff in the Merton furnaces.
In three months to the end of July, 3175 tons were
despatched for a return to the mining co. of £3BBO.
las. Metals Extraction is a cut above concentration
for export; its aim is to produce zinc oxide, which
is a step nearer spelter. The Bulletin's blessing
goes with it; for it is quite time Australia woke
up to the fact that it can produce its own zinc and
lead paints.

scheme; but we can’t recommend any investments.

T. H. Martyn. of the Malay Peninsula
Syndicate, got quickly off the mark in securing tin
leases in Malaya. He has secured one area, and is
sampling five others, and his long Australian experi-
ence will back up any business he does with the
North.

A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, September 22, 1913.
My Dear Arini, —
Social revels want a master of ceremony
or a perpetual A.D.C. in waiting to comb
out the tangles that knot them up in one or
two places. After a tame winter, October
is going to break out in a mad whirl of
gaiety that is likely to knock out the most
seasoned Society campaigners. Nearly every-
thing in the way of an extra that can hap-
pen will occur about the same minute —or
next day. The Australia and Sydney follow
the old'lmperial plan of striking this port
for race week. They’re careless enough to
pick on October 4, when a section of the
community wants to air its new clothes at
Randwick, as the day on which they will
loaf up the harbor.
The first week in the month of roses is so
full of Australian warships, race meetings,
private dances, matrons’ hop in the. Pad-
dington Town Hall, Mayoral reception in
the Town Barn, harbor illuminations, more
races, more dances, etc., that plain citizens
will have to climb up Venetian poles
to get a fair notion of what is actually
bitting them all over at the same time.
October 7 is earmarked for a dance at
Government House by way of how d’you do
to the new naval frills.
The silverware this town is going to hand
over to its namesake Sydney is to be, the
chief item of the civic welcome to our new
war boats. On that occasion Mayor Cocks
will be draped in rabbit-skin and entirely
surrounded by gold chains almost large
enough to hold the Sydney to her moorings.
He will be an affable island in a sea of
jewellery. This will give you some idea of
the majestic preparations that are now
raging round our municipal stronghold.
The conversazione has recently been
raging with great vigor in this important
centre of civilisation. It is a revival of a
favorite mid-Victorian dissipation, and is
associated with champagne shoulders, san-
dal shoes, ringlets and the rest of the outfit
of the times which were adorned by Aug-
ustus Snodgrass, also Winkle, Tupman and
the Infant Phenomenon. The person in-
vited to a conversazione is presumably
chosen for his or her conversational ability
to drown any musical item that fights for a
hearing. Mrs. Cecil Youl rounded up many
leisured stragglers from the Apple Island to
conversaz. in the King’s Hall the other
afternoon.
A hearty dust-laying shower fell on the
R.A. Society's roof on the evening of its
conversazione in those compressed air apart-
ments. President Lister Lister, G. W. Had-
field and E. Oxnard Smith were in charge.
On the walls the most attractive lady pres-
ent was Christine Silver, “Taking a call.”
You can almost see the footlights flicker on
Christine’s charming face, and she looked
a sure winner for a place in the National
Gallery. But the event didn’t come off—
possibly the picture ran stiff or something
of the sort—and the artist, George J. Coates,
is still prepared to part with this charmer
for 250 guineas. She is worth all the
money.
Another popular revival is the 1912-13
fashion of a wedding in which the brides
are sisters or chums, and the grooms do as
they are told, and fix up the best men in a
nicely-assorted pair. Two of the Cumming-
Watt sisters were married at the same
time and place the other evening. They
are daughters of squatter Watt, whose flocks
and herds browse on Moree pastures. Mr.
Archie Flood, of an early-settler clan in
this State, married Miss Millie, and Mr.
Charlie Weston was the groom who piloted
Miss Edie Watt down the flower-trimmed
aisle.
The Waddy cricket team is likely to be
surrounded by a following of 50 or 60
N.S.W. trippers, on the deep blue track to
Colombo.
The Alan Carroll Child Study Association
held its annual interview with its balance-
sheet the other evening. Its balance-sheet,
by the way, is officially recorded as only
worth £lB or so. The Players are going to
lend their aid to this slim financial credit.
They’ll try to fatten it up, by presenting
“The Liars” to a large and truthful audience
on the evening of September 25. The
Repertory Theatre (Grosvenor-street) will
be the place.
Mrs. Mayoress Cocks presented the Alex-
andra Hospital authorities with the gifts
dragged from small and willing competitors in
Farmer and Co.’s fashion journal. The
gifts were in kind. Secretary Clarence Moss
probably had a roomy dray tethered to the
nearest post, to rattle the proceeds off the
hospital babes before the eggs began to
chirp, or the flowers to moult. Mrs. Leslie,
of the journal just quoted, was responsible
for prodding the young competitors in the
right place.
The “What to do with our Girls” Exhi-
bition is still demonstrating the amazing
ingenuity of the Australian woman. This
is the third show of the kind the Girls’
Realm Guild has, in the person of organ-
ising sec. Grace Burrows, produced every
other year in our Town Hall. The occa-
sional man who wanders through the
municipal premises at the present moment
wears the chastened expression that natur-
ally characterises a member of the super-
fluous sex. Even when he puts on an
apron and fastens it up with a safety-pin,
he loses his presence of mind in any finan-
cial crisis, and chases dominant woman for
change of a ten-shilling note. Prof. David
tore the show open with a flow of eloquence
on the generally useful tendencies of the
feminine sex —notably as persons with
whom man could not hope to compete in
the domestic virtues. His own experiences
in washing up dishes and darning socks at
the South Pole added point to these remarks.
Down there, according to the heat and
burden of his remarks, mere ineffective man
has to boil his own whale as best he can,
and lay the cloth askew for dinner to the
utmost of his limited ability. The Cullen
lady murmured a firm codicil to the Prof.’s
statement, and heartily approved of the tact-
ful manner in which the Girls’ Guild
pressed the necessary cash into the palms
of needy sisters. Rabbi Cohen having
flung another bouquet at the Australian
girl, the company stamped round to see the
show.
Architect Harry Day is responsible for
the design of the model bungalow in the
Town Hall basement. This has been the
chief attraction in the Girls’ Exhibition.
In its kitchen, open like the rest of this
fibro-cement creation to the public gaze, star
pupils from the Tech. College, under Miss
Gibbes, prepare the evening meal. This is
eaten, in due course, by the hostess of each
event and her seven or eight guests. When
the lions begin to roar at the table, the
curtains of the dining-room are drawn.
This enables the guests to feed themselves
in private. The public is, therefore, unable
to locate the exact crisis at which soup is
stabbed with a fork, or an oyster is dis-
sected with a meat axe. The crowd wan-
dered round the other evening till it dis-
covered a chink at which a few inches of
partially-dined alderman was distinctly
visible. This thrilling spectacle was much
appreciated by about 40 oersons in turn.
The poultry section, with Miss Brace as
hon. fowl expert in charge, signified its
recognition of the opening of the show by
promptly demonstrating the correct way to
lay eggs. Three chicks obligingly hatched
themselves in a “Canberra” incubator about
the same moment. As they tottered out of
their shells, in the presence of a large num-
ber of spectators, they were so overcome'Ry
the excitement of their infantile demonstra-
tion, that they went off in a dead faint for
quite one second.
Miss Lamond, a dairy lady from the
South Coast, is helping to shape the destinies
of buttermilk in the cow section of the
Girls’ Exhibition. Governor Strickland
personally conducted part of his vice-regal
kindergarten on a walking tour round the
show. Daughter Mabel, who cultivates
chickens as a hobby, lost her heart to an
incubator. His Ex., on the other hand,
wasted the treasure of his bright young
affections on a tireless cooker. This friend
of woman is alleged to cook the dinner
while the lady of the house is hanging over
the back fence, or addressing her suburb on
the Real Reasons why they should vote for
Mr. XYZ at the coming elections. Should
she fail to return —till after the elections—
the dinner will probably go on cooking itself,
till a hungry husband and starving family
discover its whereabouts and dig it out,
nicely browned in its own gravy.
The Ladies’ Kennel Club, which is demon-
strating the Dog, plain, poodle and
fancy bullpup, in the Girls Exhibition,
was expected to parade on Saturday after-
noon. Instead of being dragged round the
premises, however, by heavy-weight canines,
the Kennel Club dames kept their darlinvs
on the chain. Two vast bulldogs, who might
have bounded gaily round the building with
a few bonnet strings as their only tow-
ropes, yawned wearily in the faces of ador-
ing women. Mrs. Stopford is president of
this menagerie, which is barking and get-
ting its hair brushed in the Cathedral-side
vestibule of the Town Hall.
Mcllwraith, McEaeharn’s new inter-
state liner Katoomba was dressed in bunt-
ing the other afternoon. About 1200 souls
and bodies had been bidden to the fray,
which consisted of musical items (accom-
panied by the screech of Darling Harbor
steam funnels) and light refreshments.
The Katoomba is something of a leviathan
as a coastal craft, and she carries every-
thing, including passengers, in an unusual
amount of breathing space. A girl-orchestra
is a daring innovation. Singer Mabel Bat-
chelor, fiddlers Kathleen Long and Patricia
of that ilk, with pianist Lilian Cassidy,
will endeavor to cheer the voyager who
feels the same color ,as Maximilian Robes-
pierre, with carefully-assorted selections
while the Katoomba is churning its way
round Australian bights.
Skipper Ned Moodie-Heddle, of the brand
new Katoomba, is a long and decorative
Scot from the Orkney Isles. He is a cousin
of Miss Young Australia Fotheringhame,
and related in the 43rd degree or something
of the kind to the late Traill, of Bulletin
note. Traills, Heddles and Fotheringhames
have been more or less connected ever since
the first fishing fleet lurched up against
those storm-beaten rocks. Skipper Ned’s
eldest brother is the Ileddle of Melsetter,
who is something of a landmark in that
bleak plot of earth.
The Actors' Day Matmee produced a
programme that rolled Her Majesty s cm-
tain up at 1.45 p.m. and nailed it down
about 6 p.m. Snacks of nearly everything,
including two lugubrious recitations, gave
a crowded house an awful lot for its money.
Lewis Waller clothed “The Portrait” with
all the misery it needed. Kay Souper, who
by chance followed later with a rather sim-
ilar form of melancholia, tapped a few
stifled giggles in the gallery. Hamlet, that
well-known Irving reminiscence, was repre-
sented in the third scene of the fourth act,
and Ophelia (Nellie Ferguson) strewed the
stage with brightness from a pinny full of
flowers. Sybil Arundale’s high kicks were
followed by Sybil’s over-worked smile, an
expansive ear-to-ear effect that won’t wash
off. Walter Bentley was the Bishop who
stole the buns in “Kodak” O’Ferrall’s comic
interlude. He looked so plump and rosy
that the little boy in the case (who is a
little girl off the stage) ought to have
howled for a slice of Bishop’s cheek. GernSe
fluttered in to the Hugh Wards’ box. Her
small, tired face was just visible under the
flopping verandah of her turned-down hat.
Later, this chilly sylph maintained a mas-
sive silence on the stage while Titheradge
sold on her behalf an actor’s autograph
photo programme for ten pounds ten shil-
lings. Percy’s cheery chatter would have
run that trifle up to some unknown robbery
of hard cash. As an auctioneer Mrs. Genge
is as helpful as a stuffed robin.
Sheffield, the ancient British lord who
recently whisked his coat tails round our
scenery, was the most inquisitive member of
the party. He hurled questions at the rate
of a quick-fire machine-gun, and often hit
the mark with some accuracy. He was
moderately stunned for half a minute at the
magnificent flow of water that splashed oyer
Leura Falls, while J. Bull, M.P., was gaping
up at it. Sheffield couldn’t understand how
it was that, when oil the adjacent creeks
were dry, tons of water were bolting over one
slab of rock. The old man asked every
question but the right one in this case. If
he’d demanded the exact amount of water in
the swimming baths up above the falls, and
how much Niagara they could make when the
Tourist Department gave the order, “Let
her go,” he’d have been on the right track.
As it was, he probably put the phenomenon
down to the eccentricity of the Australian
Labor party. The worst pun of the age
was made on this occasion. A wholly un-
authorised person got it off his mind in a
whisper. He said: “Waterfall is there, my
countrymen.” Probably he knew that it
wasn’t always there.
Merchant Walter Shakspeare. who mar-
ried a charming Miss Barnes, of this town,
seven or eight years ago, is moving on to
London. The Shakspeares have camped in
Ceylon for some years, where he made a pile
in rubber and tea. Now they’re going to
make their headquarters in the British rain-
fgU •
' Mrs. Sam Lazarus, of Suva, is staying on
the terraced heights of Kirribilli for a while,
pending the. possible advent of merchant
Sam fiom his tropic counting house.
The Orvieto’s purser this trip, Mr. Ingram,
is an amateur musician of some wrist power.
He slams out all the harmony he knows, by
ear, and has an amazing repertoire. The
aesthetic Ingram learnt how to brush his
hair in an English ’Varsity.
Mrs. Donald Brown, of this township, is
hurrying off to Colombo, in the Morea, on
October 4. She means to sample life on
an up-Ceylon plantation, with a daughter
who is married to a tea planter thereabouts,
Fleet Paymaster Treacy has been buzzing
like a whole swarm of bees between this
port and Melbourne, for some weeks. He
is up to his collar in preparations for our
massive welcome to the Australia’s naval

Mrs. Beatty, the fine, upstanding VO ung
matron who won the ladies’ tennis singles
championship the other day, is the wife nf
Fleet-Surgeon Beatty, R.N. She is a North
Sydney enthusiast who has been a famous
hand at the racquet game ever since she nut
up her back hair.
Guy Hastings, of the Bert Bailey Co has
added an inch or so to his waist belt since
he was first imported from Bull’s Archi-
pelago. He is getting so used to his billet
as Sandy, in ‘‘On Our Selection,” that he
now looks over the boiled collar of his soft
shirt (as worn in that piece, with loose
riding pants) as natural as life. In the
earlv days of the Hastings’ stride through
Australian drama he seemed to be feeling
with his chin for the high linen fence of the
London clerk. Sandy is about 90 per cent
more kangaroo this trip.
The “Old Cremorne” waltz and the
“Moonglide” ditto are two new excuses for
the three-step hop. In the first, Gordon
Davison has written a simple melody suit-
able for the schoolgirl’s technique. In the
second, Reginald Ben.von beats out an at-
tractive waltz tune, which changes its key
as often as a Society damsel changes her
hats. Pedro de Zulueta contributes an in-
offensive intermezzo called “Starlight” to the
one-step movement. Some new songs in
the same consignment include a melodious
trifle by Liza Lehmann, to carry the words
“Oh, bother, sang the thrush.” He only
bothers in three verses. Haydyn Wood and
Dorothy Forster respectively are guilty of
“May in My Garden” and “A Psalm of
Love.” Chappell and Co., Ltd., are the pub-
lishers.
Soprano Amy Murphy is beginning to
hurl her fine voice all over the adjacent
scenery with a reckless disregard to soft
pedals. At the Actors’ Matinde this singer
gave the Waltz Song from “The Chocolate
Soldier,” with the impact of a brass band.
The gallery chortled with delight, and
wanted some more, but the uproar was too
uproarious to tickle sensitive ear-drums.
From Paling’s come a two-step, “Going
Some,” by L. Hardwick ; also a “Dream of
Spring.” The latter, by Agnes Armytage,
is naively described as dance for pianoforte.
“Going Some” is not so breakneck as its
name suggests, and it sits down suddenly
in a massive chord at the terminus, like a
heavy train that arrives at the Central Sta-
tion sooner than it expected. Ella Wheeler
Wilcox, the favorite poetess of the best girl
in the Sunday-school, is responsible for the
skim-milk sentiment that trickles through
the words, “Whatever is—-is Best.” It is
a comfortable sentiment, but a very unfor-
tunate one. If it had been adopted a genera-
tion or two ago the mails between here and
Maoriland would still be carried per ketch.
William Shottoh mercifully introduces a few
sharps into the otherwise flat setting of
Ella’s upward flight in song —“And I know
when my soul speeds onward in its grand
Eternal quest, I shall say, as I look back
earthward, ‘Whatever is—is best.’ ” Poetry
of this buttered-toast species could only
emanate front a lady with a good digestion
and a handsome bank balance.
Another Australian contralto trembles on
the horizon. She is still in the raw, being
too young to have a vote. Her name is
Mary Murphy, and her voice is alleged to
be large enough to take the roof off any care-
lessly built premises. A complimentary
concert to introduce the girl, who is a pupil
of Miss Mary O’Keeffe, occurs on September
30. The Concordia Hall, where this warble
will make the rafters ring, is already booked
to the extent of 500 seats. Little Mrs.
George Earp is in charge of events. A
fund to give the Voice an extensive educa-
tion is to be hatched next month.
Musician Arundel Orchard, who waves his
baton over Liedertafel voices, has been
blazing a small, neat trail for “Ring of the
Nibelung.” His lecturettes, in the Kings
Hall, are intended to plough up the average
intelligence so that it can be planted with
Wagner from 6 p.m. to midnight for there-
abouts) during the coming opera season.
Stout citizens can cultivate waists by giving
up the evening meal for solid helpings from
the “Ring.” A few cheese straws —guaran-
teed not to crackle —might be stealthily con-
sumed during the “Twilight of the Gods.
Tne Wentworth Hotel opened its new
acreage of tea garden with an extensive
w’istaria-trimmed dinner the other night.
Mrs. “Soup” Maclurcan. who started as
Mrs. Landlady with a roll call of about oU
boarders, is now in command of a concern
which has floated itself into a limited com-
pany. About 800 persons can assimilate an
evening meal in the new garden annexe.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

As Australia’s foremost Black and White Artist, Norman Lindsay and his work are well known
and highly appreciated, but no one ever dreamt of accusing the artist of being an author. Neverthe-
less the fact remains, and when we say that the work of the author is on a par with that ot ihe

things, and the illustrations alone are well worth the money. That so much genuine humor can be
got. out. of the adventure of the luckless curate speaks volumes for the ability of the artist-author
who has so suddenly leapt into the literary limelight.
“THE GOLDEN KANGAROO. '*
By AMBROSE PRATT.
(Author of “ Three Years with Thunderbolt.” “ The Outlaws of Weddln Range,” etc., etc.)
Price, 1/-; Post Free, 1/1.
The Golden Kangaroo was reported to be one of the best paying propositions the Australian
mining world had ever known. Its directors were pillars of probity who would never drearn ot
issuing false re >orts or of salting samples. But the hero of this tale had his doubts and started in
to expose the fakers. He abducts the chairman’s daughter, with whom he goes through many
strange adventures ;he works in the mine until he unravels the plot to rig the market; brings tne
designing directors to book; saves the shareholders; and marries the aforesaid daughter.
Altogether this is a fascinating story well worthy of the attention of readers who enjoy thrilling
situations described in tense, graphic language.

To Hear Ourselves
as Others Hear Us
The Modern Blarney Stone isn’t a stone at all, and you don t have
to imperil your neck to kiss it.
But it has the magic qualities which tradition ascribes to the
famous stone of Castle Blarney.
The Modern Blarney Stone is a cylinder which fits any Edison
Cylinder Phonograph, and upon which you can record your own
voice in the privacy of your home as easily as you speak into a
telephone. Then by a simple adjustment you cause the phono-
graph to repeat to you what you have spoken or sung to it.
Thus you have the opportunity to study your own voice and
manner of speech, correct the faults you discover, and ultimately
acquire the gifts of tongue, which, according to the old Irish legend,
can be achieved by kissing the Blarney Stone.
Write for this great
Booklet it’s FREE!
Our new booklet, “To Hear Ourselves as Others Hear Us,” or “The
Modern Blarney Stone,” suggests various advantages which result from
the study of your own voice on the Edison Phonograph. A perusal of its
pages is commended to those who sing, whether professionally or merely for
the edification of themselves and their friends; to teachers of music, com-
posers, lawyers, clergymen, actors and actresses, lecturers, political students
and speakers, business executives, salesmen and saleswomen, young people
generally, and to all hostesses.
Send your name and address to-day for a free copy of this grand little
booklet, and we will forward it to you by return mail.

in London during August, Messrs. A. Wulfing & Co.
were awarded the Grand Prix for their well-known
tonic-food, Sanatogen. It is a significant fact that
an international jury of leading medical men should
have singled out this preparation for the highest
possible award, and the makers are congratulated
on their success. Sanatogen has a world wide repu-
tation, and thoroughly deserves the good opinion
held of it by the medical profession.*

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, September 22, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The British visitors dined with G.-G.
Denman, then made their first big public
bow at State Parliament House, on Monday
evening of last week. A herd of guests
dodged the All Australia Exhibition in one
part of the great barn, and arrived in full
regalia in Queen’s Hall and the adjacent
corridors. A party there gets as scattered
as an open-air garden rally. Rita Hope
was bravely playing Chopin in one corner,
while a brass band was blowing the latest
ragtime in the corridor. A full-powered
concert, with Hal Elvins accompanying,
managed to get some sort of a hearing in
another location. Premier Watt, with his
lady dressed in magpie black and white, re-
ceived the visitors. State Governor Fuller
being absent, John Madden put on his
Lieutenant-Governor warpaint and per-
formed in time to the Anthem. Son Guy
raked out his coat with the blue lapels,
shook the moths out of it, and did a turn as
Gentleman Help. The Barttelot dame and
her husband accompanied the Emmotts, who
have been given shakedowns at Federal
Government House. The moving pictures
have helped to familiarise Melbourne with
his bald patch, long, rather wavering legs
and bland official smile. His dame dazzled
in diamonds showered over a dress like a
salad mayonnaise. Mrs. Greenwood wore
fine black Australian opals on a greeny-
yellow dress; and Mrs. Sherwell won hyster-
ical applause from keen women critics
with some intricate pink brocade. Little
Mrs. Stead hovered about and emitted little
shrieks of greeting, most of the visiting wives
being personal friends. Bishop Riley, of
Westralia, was just too sweet for anything
in a purple coat over the loveliest black silk
legs buckled with diamond garters. Ivy
Deakin-Brookes was in yellow, and accom-
panied her husband, who has just been
elected Head Man of Commerce, or some-
thing of the kind. The Forrest dame, wear-
ing a great quantity of lace, helped her Big-
john to buzz about and look important. The
British folk were rather shy, and, the gather-
ing being numerous and scattered, introduc-
tions were difficult. Australian guests
would suddenly come upon a lonely couple
of foreigners sitting forlornly on one of the
corridor lounges and eye them dubiously and
rather defiantly. The social ice was thick
in all directions. A careful selection of
gold-laced naval men wandered about; but
a careful selection of Antarctic explorers
would have been of more use. Most guests
had a furtive air; everywhere you saw local
people considering the frozen newcomers and
begging to be informed which of them
really mattered. It was like an uncata-
logued day at the Zoo. A programme of
welcome was handed round like a tract or
a political pamphlet. It was burdened with
a long and florid poem about the bonds of
Empire, and was signed “E. B. Loughran.”
The intention was good, but it didn’t break
any of the ice. The new Overseas. Club
spread itself out thin and got lost in the
mazes of the crowd. People drifted away
thankfully at 10 o’clock, cold and unsatis-
fied, in spite of the supper rally.
The Gaud Mayoral couple opened the
Town Hall doors as wide as possible, on
Saturday evening, to welcome the everlast-
ing British Parliamentary push. The motor-
crawl to the doors began on the far side of
Princes Bridge, and kept on till long past
nine o’clock. The huge herd of Melbourne’s
diamond-studded Best were being shepherded
towards the Gaud Mayoral gloves when the
lights blinked to a mere glimmer and an
inflamed map of the world (done in colored
lights) became visible. There were also
colored prints of Westminster and Federal
Parliament House, some flowers and a bust
of George V. Very cheap and tasty. In
the middle of the red-carpeted hall a foun-
tain played in variegated colors all the even-
ing. G.-G. Denman and his dinner party
sauntered in presently and headed for the
dais. Mrs. Hennessy did the hostess act
in a dream of gold and silver brocade fitting
everywhere without a wrinkle and eked out
with graceful bodice embellishments of
black chiffon. The Emmott dame (whom
everyone pronounces “a dear,” as she wears
a comfortable smile and hasn’t a trace of
the snob about her) manipulated an ivory
brocade creation encrusted with beaded
Japanese embroidery and rose floral touch-
ings. Dame Madden looked tired but hand-
some in pearl grey and turquoise blue, with
all the family diamonds and misty tulle
hair-ties, while daughter Lesly bore up in
royal purple upholstering, outlined in dia-
mond trimmings. John looked expansively
important, and a brigade of blue-trimmed
Helps backed him up. The star visitor is
little Mrs. Greenwood, whose fair prettiness
was upholstered in white satin and floating
veilings of grey-beaded chiffon. The Pirie
daughter looked demure in white satin hid-
den in a mist of white chiffon. The
Barttelot dame was willowy and discreet in
expensive black. Mrs. Frank Madden, also
clothed in black, relieved with a posy of
real pink roses, had a couple of daughters in
tow. Mrs. Arthur Sherwell and the
Carlile clan were well gemmed, and Mrs.
.Andrew Fisher looked motherly and placid
in grey satin, lace and black ninon. The
Norton Grimwade dame staggered most be-
holders in white silk embroidered net over
black, with colored touchings, and a giant
black osprey held down with diamonds. A
programme of music was drowned success-
fully with conversation. At feeding time
the Fatted Few solemnly paired off, and
proceeded upstairs to some exclusive
refreshments, while the crowd stayed below
and struggled as furiously as possible for
mere victuals.
A rather chilly and depressing party was
hurried through in the gardens of Federal
Parliament House on Saturday afternoon.
Needless to say, it was engineered in honor
of the Confound those British! they
keep on turning up everywhere! The
solemn spree was sandwiched between lunch
and a cadet review, with a big dinner func-
tion and a reception to follow. Senator
Givens and his wife did the welcoming, and
Mrs. Andrew Fisher, clad warmly in tweeds,
looked the most sensible woman present.
The bleak wind drove the feminines in
flimsy silks and satins to cover. The
Emmott dame put sable and brocaded velvet
over her thin blue frock. A band played
vigorously, and tea cheered the chatterers
before they moved off to take their places
on the steps for the march past.
Presently the flight of grey stone steps was
flecked with brilliant raiment. Specially
selected, scarlet-clad soldiers, on superb
horses, were stationed on a tan square, and
uniforms blazed everywhere. G.-G. Den-
man, riding at the head of the young army,
was a blinding sight. He seemed to have
everything on but his wife’s diamonds and
the family cruet-stand. The Treasury win-
dows were hung with rugs and scarves "and
filled with eager faces, as were the windows
all down Collins-street. After the dull round
of food, talk and speeches, the parade must
flave come as a relief.
The Gaud Mayor gives a dance in the
Town Hall, on Wednesday, October 1, with
the G.-G. pair as guests of honor. (The
Denman lady is to return before then.)
The affair will probably be a sort of glori-
ous thanksgiving revel, as it is understood
among the corporation paunches that Hen-
nessy will be re-elected for a fresh term.
Among the public, who are not unconcerned
with the dust problem, the .Hennessy re-
election is approved of. There seems a
vague belief that he is laying a little more
dust, or spreading a little less sand on
windv days than his predecessors did.
Mrs. Hennessy heroically led the prudent
army of lymph-receivers at the Town Hall
in the first days of the small-pox scare ; but
she has been suffering severely for it ever
since. She reappeared in pubiic last week,
taking up hostess duties again with a sigh
for the entertainment of the British.
Mrs. Albert Austin is lending her garden
and praying for fine weather for Saturday,
27th. (Meanwhile the squattocracy are fer-
vently thanking Heaven for the inches of
rain that splashed on parched Northern
Victoria.) Qn Saturday, all Toorak, led by
St. John’s Guild, is to extract money from
itself by means of a fete. The convalescent
Gaud Mayoress is gathering strength to
open the show. The members of St. John’s
Guild sew for the poor, week by week; but
Toorak has not enough poor to go round,
so the surplus garments are going to the
Streets and Lanes Mission. Charity is
really a boom this year. Maybe it will
sober down into a habit.
Mrs. Peter, of Glenloth station, whilst
motoring towards town on a shopping raid
from her far-north Victorian station, was
overtaken by rain, and finally held up alto-
gether by a melted road, which clung like a
long-lost creditor to the wheels of the car.
She had to get out of the stationary machine
and walk miles to the nearest means of
transport—a bullock-waggon, or something
of the kind. When at last she arrived
home,, muddy and tired, she was met by a
beaming husband chanting thanksgivings for
the burst-up of the drought. At any
rate, the Glenloth dance happened on the
last fine evening. The squattocracy for
miles round motored to the homestead and
danced and sat out on the twenty-foot
verandahs. A careful chauffeur who was
told off to light up placed two paper globes
in front of each sitting-out nook. Later on
he complained to Missus that he had to
“light all them lanterns again,”, the wind or
someone having blown them out. Since the
big revel a whole crop of engagements has
sprouted.
Commander Stivey is to give a great party
aboard the White Star sea-giant Ceramic, at
Port Melbourne, on Friday, 26th. Each in-
vited guest has received an immense slab of
pasteboard with a detachable slab for the
admiral in charge of the gangway.
Tommy Cochrane, the Incomparable, is
billed to play a part in the Repertory
Theatre . production of “Major Barbara,”
and Society is convulsed with horror be-
cause it is reported that the adored youth
will have to sacrifice his beautiful sideboard
whiskers. This may seem a poor, trivial
matter to make a fuss about. But perhaps
you haven’t seen those whiskers?
Dave Armstrong, a popular old collegian
of “Scotch,” has taken Miss Je9n Arm-
strong, of Sandringham, to wife. The bride
had just returned from a foreign trip, and
the pair were quietly wedded at Scots’
Church last week. Sol Bloom, the Univer-
sity youth with political 'aspirations, was
best man, and, in that capacity, helped to
get the pair safely off to Bananaland. When
they return to Melbourne and earth gener-
ally, the bridegroom will continue selling
real estate, as before the wedding.
The hand of sympathy has gone out to
Argus editor Cunningham,'who has suffered
two bereavements. His sister, Mrs. Snow-
den, died suddenly in Tasmania, and his
mother passed away, three days later, at the
house of her son Charles.
Chas. L. Forrest, squatter and ex-M.L.A.
for Corangamite, at his death left a will
bequeathing (so it was thought) £lOO,OOO
to charities. His nephew has contested the
will, and it has been declared void for un-
certainty—to the great gain of the nephew
aforesaid.
Fan Dango’s husband built a beautiful
house for his captive star, at Berwick ; and
when the dainty little lady tired of the
country, the place (red roofs, windmills and
all) was sold to Andrew Chirnside. The
Chirnsides have had an anxious time ever
since their housewarming. Mrs. Andrew has
had doctors and nurses from town in at-
tendance at Berwick for weeks. The latest
report tells of three of Melbourne’s leading
medicoes. petrolling up the drive in mad
haste, with a batch of relieving nurses fol-
lowing closely. It is hoped that the united
skill of all these people will put the poor
lady on the road to health again.
Berwick, a smart golfing resort which lies
just beyond the suburban radius, found that
additions to the club-house were and
sent out a call for help. So it came to pass
that councillor Scott Sharp and his wife,
and one of the Pearson squatting clan gave
a tea party and a dance. Melbourne motors
whirled the road dust into the air, as the
Best People fled to the revel. Chirnsides,
Millers, Harpers and Agar Wynnes put
their best wheels foremost and thoroughly
oiled the atmosphere, and the neighbors
hurried down from their picturesque hilltops
to join in the chatter and gyrations. Kath-
leen McPherson Knox has a house on a hill,
and the Oscar Armytages have another with
a view of ever so many miles of picked coun-
try. Two of the woolly Pearson clan are
settled loftilv in the vicinity, and the Robert
Harpers’ only daughter, Mrs. Taylor, is a
neighbor. Then Mrs. Webster, of the St.
Germains Patterson family, still owns a
corner of the countryside. All these hill-
owners rolled down loyally to help the good
cause. For the dance that followed, the
guests were heavily disguised with grease,
paint and rouge till some of them lost all
resemblance to human beings. Grandmother’s
oldest silks got an airing on several smart
young dames. 'T'he financial results were
highly satisfactory, and the club-house
threatens to grow as fast as a rumor; also,
the membership list is swelling visibly.
President Mrs. aßeckett had perforce to
stay away. When the revelry was at its
height—or away up—she was being tenderly
nursed in a Melbourne hospital and fed with
beef tea. She had been taking her politics
too seriously.
The captain of the newest inter-State
steamer, the Katoomba, lately gave a marine
party. Young Mcllwraith, son of one of
the firm, had travelled out with his wife
from England on the boat, and helped the
skipper to welcome the guests. Maggie
Stirling sang on the gleaming deck, and a
brass band blared gaily where the wind
whistled shrilly round a corner. (The wise
woman always ties her hat on firmly for a
ship function.)
The “Hoffmann” matinde cast last week
included none of the hard-worked principal
voices, except Gladys Ancrum’s serviceable
soprano. Hedmont, tall and plump, sang
the tenor hero part nicely in a small way,
and acted quite well enough. The tall
Ancrum lady waved her long arms as the
alluring Giulietta, and made a fine picture
at times. But when Edith Clegg and she
did the barcarolle duet at the beginning of
the Venetian act, it was hardly recognisable
as the rich blend of contralto tones that
little Rose Beyron and Edna Thornton in-
troduced to Melbourne on the opening
Quinlan night last year. Clara Butt and
her Rumford were in one of the boxes. The
barcarolle duet is one of their concert num-
bers, and they had come to hear it. They
must have wondered as to exactly how much
difference there is between their own con-
tralto-baritone blend and the two soprano
voices on the stage. A musical draper
might describe it as the difference between
mercerised cotton and silk velvet.
Quinlan’s version of “Walkure” is worth
the money, just to see the blue god’s cloak
Parker trails amongst the war-maidens’
mountains. The garment is a magnificent
thing, both as regards size and color. It is
the green-blue of a tropic sea, and Parker-
Wotan sweeps it round him so that it looks
like a swirl of cloud. He even veils the
rather awkward subsidence of a too-squat
Brunnhilde, when she goes off to sleep on
her rockv bed with the fires around her.
The cloak and the Brunnhilde red hair veil
the pronounced curves of the lady until the
shield and eagle-feathered casque are in
place. One woman, dazzled by the blue
robe, remarked plaintively in the half dark-
ness that she was “dying to see how it was
made.” The lights failing to go up, she
remarked crossly, “I can’t see a bit! Why
don’t they light the stage better?” “Be-
cause it is midnight in the mountains!”
growled her husband alongside. Then the
disturbed females all around hunched their
powdered shoulders contemptuously and
gave a splendid imitation of a bleak day in
a frozen meat depbt.

The Craftworkers, who started this year
of grace in practical business style are
making steady headway. People seem to
like the co-operative idea as applied to the
arts, and the rush of business has induced a
better show than ever. Things are set out
for inspection in the big Centreway window
and secretary Isabel Gullett is kept hard at
work.
Miss Thea Proctor had a correct artistic
crowd round her fans on opening day. The
absent Denman lady has rather a taste in
that direction; but the Carmichael dame
was a fan expert. She had a wonderful
collection of old examples—some painted and
others embroidered. (She loved anything
from the needle.) When Hardy Wilson
brought part of his dainty collection to Mel-
bourne, the vice-regal enthusiast was
frankly envious. Wilson has a special col-
lection of “sticks” —the ivory and inlaid
mother-of-pearl frames, the making of
which was an early Florentine art in itself.
Miss Proctor says she finds great pleasure
in fan-painting because any fantasy is quite
in place. Pity is the delicate, flirting things
are quite out of fashion in this age of sporty,
athletic women.
The Victorian artists bohemed politely
enough at the East Melbourne galleries on
Thursday evening. Music and two-stepping
prevailed until 9.30, when a joyous rush was
made to the spaghetti banquet. Great bowls
of flavored macaroni, hot from the stove of
an Italian chef, were carved into eatable
lengths by most of the company. Sculptor
Cadorin was an interesting exception. He
juggled deftly with the slippery yards of
nourishment, and never choked once.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Matrons’ Ball in the Walkerville Hall wag a
success. Anyhow, no one lodged a protest to any
one of the seven ladies who were responsible for it,
which is good enough evidence for me. Mrs. Lav-
ington Bonython was protected from the elements
by an artistic black creation, and Mrs. C. M. Muir-
head —who was a Miss Butler till a few months ago
—wore her wedding dress. Her mother (who was
one of the seven above-mentioned) was in cream,
with drapings of apricot ninon and an attenuated
train. Mrs. Graham Stewart, another of the host-
esses, adorned a black ninon frock with a dash of
coronation blue. Amongst an absolute welter of
youth and beauty were to be seen Mrs. Walter Law
Smith, Miss Coles, Miss Creswell, Miss Muirhead, a
couple of fresh-complexioned Butler girls, Miss Paul-
ine Schomburgk and Miss Ringwood.
Mary Grant Bruce has been boomed by London
Daily Mail as “the distinguished Australian author-
ess.” The Mail has published some of her articles,
and she has managed an interview with Northcliffe.
She talks of a return to her native Gippsland in
the early part of next year.
On Friday evening Dr. and Mrs. Charles Todd
gave a children’s fancy-dress dance. The Bonython
trio were a Turk and a Turkish lady and a be-
spangled fairy. The small Robert Rymills were
also of Asia. A slim Poulton child was a miniature
Gende, and Mrs. A. E. O’Leary’s daughterlet was a
dashing beauty of the Nell Gwynn period. Mrs.
Todd was helped in the business of feeding the
guests, quelling riots, etc., by Mrs. Lavington
Bonython, Miss Laxmoore, Miss Davis and her sister,
Miss Hawker, Miss Jean Wigg, Miss Vincent and the
Misses Young.
The Dudley Haywards, who have been globe-
trotting, are expected back some time in November.
Mrs. J. Harvey will be back next rfionth.
A goodish crowd of sporting people went to
Cheltenham on Saturday, which was all right for
the Port Adelaide Racing Club, but considerably less
so for the people who had to use the trains. Usually
we think our train-service on race-days is the limit,
in the wav of rottenness, and can be no worse. But
it can, and on Saturday it was. Also it did. Mrs.
Jervois was there, also Mrs. A. Bowman, Mrs. J. H.
Pile and her daughter, Miss Lewis, the Leslie Bick-
fords and W. F. Driscolls and hosts of men. Fight-
ing Prince gave the few who backed him a £22
dividend; he attracted only £64 to the totalisator.
Lady Bosanquet gave away the prizes at the
Queen’s School Sports on Friday, in a well-tailored
garment of amethyst serge. On the lawn were
but suppose I cut out the usual old list for once
in a way.
Mrs. Darnley Naylor, who died on Sunday morn-
ing at the Grand Central Hotel, had been ill, more
or less, ever since she went to England last. She
took a kawi interest in her husband’s work, and
was an earnest philanthropist.
The School for Mothers held its annual meeting
on Monday. It is rather on the lines of a kinder-
garten for the instruction of grandparents in the
art of sucking eggs. However, it is an extremely
useful institution. Lady Bosanquet was there to
see that the proceedings were carried out correctly.
The strolling politicians arrived here this week.
Their advance agency work had Been well so
all was in readiness for them. They were re-
ceived” by the Mayor, and likewise at a “small anu
early” at Government House on Wednesday evening.
Also, they were motored hastily through the hills,
from which trip they returned in a more or Jess
damp condition. If there had been time they would
have been fed and motored even more. We are never
backward with these delights where affluent visitor
are concerned.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.

HOW TO HAVE A BEAUTIFUL
COMPLEXION.
One of our lady readers who found that
her skin was becoming harsh and dry from
the use of powders, rouge and creams sends
us the formula below. She states that the
simple, harmless and inexpensive lotion made
therefrom softened her skin, and so greatly
enhanced the beauty of her complexion that
she has entirely done away with all other
preparations which she had been accustomed
to use. This lotion can easily be prepared
at home or by any good pharmacien. It calls
for two ounces of Hose Water, one drachm
Tincture of Benzoin, and two ounces
Flowers of Oxzoin. Mix together and apply
night and morning with the hands, or use
a soft cloth or sponge. Always shake well
before using. Our obliging correspondent
asks that her name be withheld for personal
reasons, but nevertheless gives us permis-
sion to publish her letter for the benefit of
other readers. She adds that this formula
was given her by a woman 05 years old,
whose youthful complexion and almost
total absence of wrinkles were a source of
wonder and admiration to all who knew
her.*

TRAFELLERS ’ TALES.
TRAVELLER : “Strange breed them, Mister!”
DROVER: “It was this way, boss. Our men
got speared. Short-handed. Had to bore
holes through trees, poke the tails through,
and tie ’em in a knot. One night they
rushed!"
“Dux” : I’ve heard of racehorses that could
“catch swallows,” but they’re only hearse-tuggers
alongside Henare’s br. c. Crimson Dash. The other
morning they tried Crimson Dash over four furlongs
straight on the beach. Henare held the clock (a
full brother to grandfather’s), and, when he waved
the blanket, little Tiki dug the hooks into the colt,
and the rest was just plain streak. As he passed
us I gasped, “Whatcher make it, Henare?” Henare
scratched his head and surveyed the clock doubt-
fully. “Py cripes,” he said, “tat te goot garrop.
Run te four furlong before te clock had te time to
start.” I have a faint suspicion that Henare forgot
to wind the ticker; but I’m saving up to have a
punt on Crimson Dash, anyhow.
“Uuarel” : There’s a salt-bitten old skipper
in charge of one of the small craft that trade out of
Hobart to the ports as far as 50 miles south, and he
has been known to lose his urbanity once only. That
was last summer, when he was piloting a cargo of
tourists to view the historic Port Arthur penal settle-
ment ruins. Tne boat was about two hours behind
time and one superior bore had pestered the old man
as to when he was likely to make the port. For the
185th time the pest had been answered good-humor-
edly, but on the 186th occasion the sea serpent
turned. “Aw, I say, Captain,” said the stranger,
“dash it all, y’ know, we ought to have been there
hours ago.” “Sir,” was the reply, “you ought
to have been there years ago.”'
* * * *
“Lagani Namo”: Superstition? Well,
this is the depth of ft in Papua. Every pourri-
pourri man knows that he himself is a fraud; but
he’s just as credulous as the other natives, and be-
lieves that the other pourri-pourri men have superna-
tural powers. I happened into a village on a Sun-
day afternoon some years ago, and found it thronged
with excited natives, a couple of thousand or so of
them. They sat about a sandy circus in the middle
of a cocoanut grove solemnly stuffing themselves
with betel nuts while two coppered brothers were
writhing in semi-conscious agony in a cleared space.
Relatives of the victims (for that is what they
were) sat by holding them down during fits, the
women wiping green froth away as it exuded from
their lips. A group of devilish-looking pourri-
pourri men were handy. One of these, his body
smeared with charcoaly oil and colored pigments, his
face encircled in long, black cassowary feathers, neck-
lets of red shell charms on his breast, dozens of hoops
of plaited cane about his waist, biceps and calves and
ankles, with garters of grass and fibre around them,
was leaping here and there, and shrieking accusa-
tions and vituperations. With a final fierce panto-
mime he illustrated what he would do to those
responsible for the plight of the “boys” writhing
at his feet when his charms would have discovered
them. As soon as he concluded his performance
another Mephistopheles leapt into the theatre scream-
ing some more, and blowing red betel pulp all
about him. He cursed everyone in general, and
some in particular. This sort of thing went on for
a couple of hours, during which a dozen or more
sorcerers leaped. Suddenly three youths were
dragged into the arena, the sorcerers sprang about
them grunting like pigs, spitting threats and ex-
pectorating scarlet juice over them. The youths
simply gazed fixedly straight before them. “Speak !
you sons of pigs,” commanded the sorcerers, and
one of the youths opened his lips to begin, but the
pourri-pourri men shouted him down. These sorcerers
had every villainy imaginable depicted on their
wrinkled faces. They looked like fiends vomited
from Tophet for the occasion. One of the boys
died —poisoned, of course, by some of the pourri-
pourri men themselves. Subsequently several of them
were arraigned and a host of witnesses summoned
by the Crown, but witnesses know very well that
unless they tender evidence favorable to the sor-
cerers they too are almost certain to be having a fit
on the sand when they go back to their villages.
And after the brief description I have given here
of a fit on the sand you can understand the reluct-
ance the inhabitants feel to undergoing one.
* * * *
“W..T.H,”: So prolific have opossums be-
come in the southern provinces of Maoriland that
numbers of rabbiters have given bunny the go-by.
In some cases cheques of from £35 to £4O a month
are being made from trapping the marsupial, and it
has been computed by the Otago
Acclimatisation Society that from
December to May last over 14,000
’possum skins, valued at £4200,
were exported from Dunedin. This
points to the early extinction of
the little furry citizen or institu-
tion, and powerful friends are mak-
ing efforts to block the mercenary
exterminators. A lady at Burnt
Flat, Catlins (Otago), has taken a
practical way to save the species
in her district. Makjng a pathetic
appeal to a number of youtntul
trappers, she got them to pass on
to her the joeys found in the female
marsupials' pouches. For the ac-
commodation of the babies she had
a run constructed, much on the
pattern of a fowlyard, with the dif-
ference that the nests were placed
just under a roof, and a ladder was
hung from the carefully wire-nettpd
yard to give access to the roost-
ing places. The guests are fed on pap, such as is
given to chickens, and green stuffs of all kind. The
experiment is progressing valorously.

“The Immature Male”: Me for “Inebri-
ated” when we open the Multi-Synonymous Word
Competition. In the number, aptness and variety
of its colloquial equivalents I consider it comman-
deers the pastry. For instance:—
Loaded, primed, beered, on the bust, blued,
potted, shot, sozzled, soused, inked, tanked, flooded’
lull, tiddley, umpty-do, blind, blue-blind, joyed, wet,
staggered, ripe, rolling, paralytic, stung, jim-jammed!
on, tangled, outed, snake-juiced, half-seas over,
moist, off the waggon, dizzy, ding-donged,. gone a
million, soaked, sprung, ale-d, damped, three sheets
in the wind, syruped, juiced, bunkered, guzzled,
gone, afloat, rocky, jagged, irrigated, under the ini
Alienee, gargled, flummixed, squiffy, slopped, ele-
phant-trunked, boozed, doped, hipped, watered,
brimmed, chloroformed, tight, merry, a-wash, or!
the loose, drunk, screwed, shickered, canned, groggy,
corned, razzled, sewed up, lushy, muzzy, slewed,
drunk as Chloe, drunk as a fiddler, drunk as a
lord, drunk as an owl, putrid, dead to the world
and plain blithered. And I have heard a Spaniard
say, “Drunk as the Lord’s sepulchre.”

“To Babarantiko”: I am fairly well
acquainted with both the N. S. Wales and Islands
variety of centipede, but I never saw it “suckling”
its young, as mentioned recently. On the other
hand, I can say positively that my old friend,
Mother Scorpion, has that attractive maternal
habit. Anyhow, the small, speckled Islands variety
has. This crawler is not so poisonous as some of
the family. First time he tasted me the burning
sensation around the locality impoverished descrip-
tion, and bankrupted my stock of metaphorical
retaliation. Unpleasant numbness followed the pain,
and for some days afterwards the skin and flesh
were curiously hard and senseless. Still, those
were comparatively mild symptoms. It was a com-
mon, barnyard fowl that squared the deal between
me and the scorpion push. 1 had occasion to use a
stack of some hundreds of rough palm-leaf mats
(tinai) for copra drying, and these were literally
swarming with the spider of the Zodiac. And the
way that chook threaded the procession down her
throat was an unholy revelation. If she wasn’t
zinc-lined, then hen must be impervious to scor-
pion.

WILLIAM JACKSON,
who was carelessly rode
over, and killed returning
from the races,
21 May, 1831,
aged 21 years.
Oh, Henry you did me kill
And would not pay my Drs. Bill
Your drunkedness has caused my fall
But consider you must come. When God doth call.
The other moulders peacefully in God's Acre at
Jamberoo (N.S.W.) :
He was a neighbor good and true
To many known in Jamberoo.
Kiama now his loss bewails,
And all his friends at Riverdale.
His sons their father's name revere,
And had this stone erected here
His honored grave for to respect
And as a tribute of respect.
Died in 1*849.

“Mint” : Dave and Bill were room-mates.
Bill was superstitious. If he spilt the salt, walked
under a ladder, or had 13 beers running in the same
bar, he knew he was threatened with disaster and
death. All this was mental stimulus for Dave. One
Wednesday morning he remarked that he (Dave)
had dreamt that Bill was to die on the following
Sunday, and advised him to be careful, as dreams
very often came true. On Saturday they came home
together. Dave immediately set about making pre-
parations for the afternoon’s outing by making for
the bathroom. Before he went he artfully reminded
Bill of his fate, and exhorted him to see a parson.
When he came back, Bill was gone. Ten seconds
after he realised that his week’s pay had also gone.
Bill came back on Monday night, and, in answer to
a demand for an explanation, said: “Dave, ole man,
we’re cobbers. You tole me I was goin’ to die, an’
I knew you’d like to see me die happy. Two quid
wasn’t enough to make me ’appy, so o’ course I
had to take yours.” Dave has decided not to work
on Bill’s superstitions any more. There is no money
in it, and even less fun.
* * * *
“Moses” : A bird in the hand is still worth
two in the bush. Steinwedel was a machinery trav-
eller. The turkey he won in a raffle at Digeniont
he gave to a pal, as Purplestraw had promised to
meet him at the train with a pair of turkeys—
payment for fixing up his binder at the brink of
harvest time. Purplestraw turned up all right.
“Sorry,” he said, “I couldn't get them turkeys for
yer. But here’s the next-best thing,” and he pushed
a bag of rabbits through the window as the train
blew out of the station. Next day 10 infuriated
fettlers reported themselves as injured through being
struck by flying rabbits.
“Bygum”: “Gilgunnia’s” statement (B.
21/8/’l3) that the Mitakoodis use the too-ta for
poisoning fish is O.K. ; but the too-ta is only one of
many plants similarly used, and is not by any
means the most effective. Up on the Mitchell and
around Cloncurry (Q.) Brother Binghi uses either
the yakooro or the jinbul when he wants a fish
lunch. As the yakooro, or fresh water mangrove,
grows around the waterholes and lagoons, it is very
handy. The bark is cut up, hammered on a stone
and thrown into the water. The fish eat it readily—
and then up goes their number! If the fish are
UNNECESSARY LABOR.
“ Ho, there, Bill!"
“ Wot yer want? ”
“ Nuthirt."
“Then wotcher wanter make a bloke turn
round for? ”
eaten at once there is no unpleasant taste, but if
kept for a day the tang of the vakooro permeates the
flesh. The jinbul tree grows on all the Gulf rivers.
The branches are laid in waterholes and sicken the
fish, which rise to the surface and are easily ob-
tained. Strange to say, the roots of the jinbul will
destroy the fish in the brackish and salt water near
the mouths of the rivers, while the leaves and
branches have no effect there. The inside bark is
also beaten to pulp and used for poultices in case
of snake-bite. A handy tree is the jinbul to have in
your vegetable garden.
