ONE COW, ONE CREED.
[For The Bulletin.]
One Meth Singh, Indian hawker, of Terang (Vic.),
had to be sworn on the cow—the only oath he held
binding.-— Press item.
Once, before a P.M. cheery, came a Hindoo dark and
bleary, with intent to give his witness in a
curious colored case;
And the P.M. hesitated, for he duly estimated what
a power of subtle perjury there lay behind that
face.
“Ahmed Ragbag,” fin'lly said he, “Are you list’ning
—are you ready to give truthful testimony in
this inter-alien row ?
Will you swear upon the Book,
That no statement false or crook,
You will utter?”
Came a mutter:
“I will swear it on the Cow!”
Not bv bell and book and candle —such to him were
fashions Vandal f.vith the Aryan from the
Deccan banish falsehood from his breast;
Sounding brass and tinkling cymbal to his wily wits
and nimble, are the wretched old devices that
our Courts of Law infest;
But by Her he holdeth holy, be she known as Poll
or Poley, ’fore the Ileav’ns that loom above us
doth he make a solemn vow.
To the Fact to clip and cleave,
And in no wise to deceive;
Now and ever.
Swerving never —
He will swear it on the Cow.
And the dairy whites around him cried: “This Hin-
doo man, confound him ! Doth he hug the very
symbol that ourselves we count most dear ?
Is there aught we reckon higher in the days when
we aspire, than the skin of Poll or Poley, where-
upon he sweareth here?
We are near to thee, O brother! We were suckled
by one mother, and our blood it beats to thee-
ward, as our thumping pulses trow ;
Let us live as kinsmen both,
Let us use a common oath ;
Our Shekinah
Shall be Dinah,
And our pledge shall be—-‘The Cow!’”
So the White Man and the Hindoo make the moo-
cow’s varied skin do, in that district as a
symbol of the goddess they adore;
And in mutual bovine tether do the.v sweetly dwell
together—Christ or Boodh the outward banner,
Doll or Dinah at the core;
Howsoe’er we chirp and chatter of this Ahmed's
alien patter, of his coat that shineth soapless, and
the guile upon his brow,
In our bosoms we must feel,
At our altars doth he kneel;
To things Royal
He is loyal,
And he sweareth on the Cow !
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.

“Stohr Ruddell”: Ask a Murrumbidgee
whaler, and he will tell you Narrandera (N.S.W.)
is the champion place of Australia for either beer
or tucker. It is the only country town of any size
through which Matilda may be humped without
calling forth cutting remarks about work from un-
thinking school children. It holds the balance be-
tween Wagga and Hay on the whaler’s main route,
and is the first hurdle the shearer has to negotiate
when, cheque-proud, he takes train for Sydney or
Melbourne. I learnt the secret of Narrandera’s
glory last shearing season from three whalers with
whom 1 camped in a hut at Howlong, outside Carra-
thool. I had given voice to a rumor that rouse-
abouts were wanted at Groongal, seven miles off.
They smiled. “That’s no good to me,” said one.
“They can’t catch me with them jokes. Workin’
five or six weeks, and blowin’ it in Narrandera in a
couple of days. I’ll get in at the cut out, an' catch
Wyvern, Tog., Bringagee, Groongal, Kerarbury and
Benerembah. I'll touch the babblin’ brook here for
a page of scran to-day (it was Friday), do Groongal
on Monday, and work the other sheds down to
Yanco. A man can always fluke a farg there, and
the Johns don’t know how to take him in Narrandera.
That’s where some blokes settle themselves. They
haven’t got enough savee to get out while shearin s
on. They hang around the town, an’ the Johns and
pubs get ’em snouted. The game’s got to be
worked.” I proved the truth of this later in Nar-
randera, when my Howlong friend stood cheerfully
on a pub verandah in the midst of a dozen “cheque-
men.” Admiring and envying his genius for free
drinks, I asked him how long he could last. “If a
man doesn’t do his block, and make himself a nark,”
he replied, “he can put in here till Christmas.
The ’s always a Yanco mob in, but Wagga’s better
after harvest.” From that I gathered that it was
only necessary to keep comparatively sober, to ques-
tion no other man’s bona fides, and to agree that the
man with the money was all that he claimed to be,
and Narrandera would ask no questions. Wherefore
Narrandera is nominated for the best town stakes.
“Pick”: To call a man “a cow” is the
average bushy’s dead-end of argument. The other
day I listened to a lurid dispute between Bill Shearer
and Dan Bullock}', in which the sulphurous vocabulary
of each was drawn upon to the utmost. Bill com-
manded the better stock, and denounced Dan as a
thief, a liar, a Chinaman, and every other degraded
thing, in or out of the dictionary. Dan stood it all
unmoved; but when the sheep-barber wound up his
reproaches by declaring that his opponent was “a
cow,” the latter shot out of his apparel. “You called
me a cow, did yer ?” he shouted. “Put up yer
dooks.” “Well,” said the tomahawker, “yer
mightn’t be a cow, but yer everything else.” Which
Dan accepted as all the apology that the case called
for ; and they went in to “wet it.”

“Pang” : My struggle for a crust takes me
round the tobacco plantations up in the north of
Ma’s territory. Hi Bung is one of the yellow mul-
titude growing the weed, and Hi and his white
missus had been having a few words. The air was
still hazy when I arrived. Hi was down at the
shed, so Mrs. Hi and I had quite a friendly chat.
She told me of the falling-out. Very interesting, of
course; but I wanted to get to business. So I asked
her when her husband would be back. “Me ’us-
band!” said Mrs. Hi. “Me ’usband! He ain’t me
’usband! It’s bad enough to be livin’ with the old
Chow without bein’ married to him!”

“Sess” : One of the Maoriland racing rules
stipulates that: ‘‘No race shall exceed 20 minutes in
duration, and after the expiration
of that time from the start the
judge shall leave the box, and no
horse which shall subsequently
pass the post shall be placed."
One might deduce from this that
the Maoriland steeds are deadly
slow beasts; or the jocks are
addicted to waiting for one an-
other. That, however, is not the
reason. The rule was begotten
of the Autumn Steeplechase, run
at Kllerslie (Auckland) in April,
1884. Of the four horses that
started, the winner, Chandler, rid-
den by Jack Rae, was the only
one to get round without a mis-
hap. A little bay gelding called
Billy-go-by-’em, ridden by .1.
Russell, when running second (the
other pair had died, or something)
stuck up at a fence on the hill,
and a stubborn contest arose be-
tween horse and rider as to which
should prevail. Half an hour
passed, and Billy was still argu-
ing the point. More time slipped
by, and eventually the stew-
ards, in desperation, ordered the
next event to be run. This was a
flat race, and necessitated the re-
moval of the hurdle in the
straight. The sight of the flying
colors coming behind him set
Billy going again. He reached
the straight an hour behind time.
The removed hurdle was hurriedly
re-erected so that Billy-go-bv-’em
could complete the course pro-
perly and enable his owner to
draw second money. A protest
was lodged against this business,
but the stewards threw out the
objection.
“Wandi”: Jones’s horse had rubbed the
bridle off and levanted, what time Jones was busy
in Smith’s licensed tabernacle. Jones’s farm was
three miles out. Nobody had a horse handy that he
wanted to lend, nor was a vehicle to be had at any-
thing like a reasonable rate. The night was wet, and
Jones, who wished to get home urgently for business
reasons, had rheumatism in both legs. So he ambled
up to the doctor’s house, where he wasn’t known,
and besought the medico to come at once to Jones’s
farm. He couldn’t say (in reply to pertinent ques-
tions) what was wrong, except that it was a very
serious case. The doctor’s motor was brought out,
and, jumping into it, they drove furiously through
the miserable drizzle and the miles of slush and bog.
On arrival Jones stepped gratefully down, and
inquired: “What’s your fee, doctor?” “Thirty
bob,” said the medical man in surprise. Mr. Jones
handed up the amount. “Those blamed motor fel-
lows,” he remarked, by way of explanation, “wanted
50 bob to drive me home. Thanks very much,
doctor. Good night.”
“One Dollar”: I was recently batching
with a friend in Gippsland (Vic.), and one Sunday
afternoon decided to attend a service to be held in
the local hall at three o’clock. Arriving 10 minutes
before the business was due to commence, I found
no one about; but, after I had waited a while, a
man arrived who turned out to be “the preacher.”
Accompanying him was an old man, with a dog
following closely. I had often spoken to the old
man before, and knew he owned two dogs, “Spot”
and “Dodger.” The dogs were much attached to
him, and this was the first time I had seen him with
Spot alone. Being therefore curious, I asked where
Dodger was. The old man did not heed my question,
although I repeated it several times. Each time he
would begin a conversation with “the preacher.” I
thought he must be deaf in the ear turned to me,
although 1 had not noticed it before. It seemed that
“the preacher” was interested in my inquiry, too.
Meantime the congregation had rolled up, and as it
was now about time to commence, “the preacher”
left us to open the door of the hall. I was just about
to ask after Dodger yet again, when the old man,
pointing to the hall, said to me in a voice full of re-
proach, “You shouldn’t have kep’ on arstin me that
question in front of him! He’s the dog collector,
and thought 1 had only one dog! ”
“Dry Track” : Re “Flinderino’s” yarn of
the man who carried the door five miles to the
nearest scholar, in order to get a message that was
written on it. There was an old publican, near
Queanbeyan (N.S.W.), who, when electors’ rights
were used some years ago, feared he might mislay
his, as many others had done; so he pasted the
document on the back of his bedroom door. When
election day came round, old Tom found he couldn’t
remove his right without destroying it; but, not to
be beaten, he took both door and right to the
polling booth, some couple of hundred yards down
the street, and recorded his vote.
“Silas Snell” : The barber’s shop at Ewe
neck was next the pub at which I
staying, and on the first afternoon of mv sniJ!!!
I drifted in to be shaved. There were three'
tomers in the saloon, which was of unlined corn
gated iron, and suggested a melting-pot. Haddon
the ambidextrous weed-shifter, was operating
Kelly. Rawbone and Code were discussing religion
and politics on the only chairs, so with customary
modesty I seated myself on the gin-case in a far
corner, and plunged into the Age of the day before
the week previous to yesterday. Rawbone and
Code became loud, then vehement, then vitupera-
tive. Coole struck the first blow; Rawbone re-
sponded with the greatest good will, and the battle
raged round Kelly and the operative. “Gentlemen!
Gentlemen!” said Haddon, in patient expostulation
but he went on shaving his patient. Coole, tottering
from a sledge-hammer bash, crashed into Kelly, and
went down with the lathered non-combatant. •‘Gen-
tlemen! Gentlemen!” complained the barber, as he
rescued Kelly and restored him to the operating
chair. “Do he careful.” Haddon resumed the
shaving of Kelly, and Code, being on end again
rushed Rawbone, and dinted the corrugated iron of
the north wail with his enemy’s head. The next
dash carried the pair right over me, and they
cannoned into Haddon, and buffered him into a
corner, where he remained a moment, with his razor
held aloft, expostulating mildly, “Gentlemen!
Gentlemen!” The fight detached itself from Had-
don. and raged in another corner among the shampoo
basins and ware, so Haddon got at the imperturb-
able Kelly again. The shaving went on in com-
parative peace, while Rawbone tried to drown Coole
by pushing his head into a basin with two inches
of water in it, and kneeling on his neck. But
Coole, by jerking his heel, secured a breakaway,
and the fight roared round the shop once more'
“Gentlemen! Gentlemen!” said Haddon, as he held
Kelly up by the nose and rasped his lip. I smartly
side-stepped the fight, and it tumbled into a small
table littered with bottles of oil and perfume, scat-
tering the stock all over the shop. “Gentlemen!
Gentlemen!” expostulated the presiding mechanic in
all mildness, chasing through the stubble up Kelly’s
right jaw. Here the locked combatants rebounded
from the south wall, crashed into the business
operations, and bore Haddon and his client to the
ground, where the whole four mixed and blended
with reaped hair and detached whiskers and satpsuds
for a space of 80 seconds. Haddon was first up.
He rescued amiable little Edward Kelly from the
tumult, and restored him to his chair. “ Gentlemen!
Gentlemen!” said Haddon, with a trace of weari-
ness. “Do he careful, please. Do you want me to
cut Mr. Kelly?” But the war was drifting in my

THISTLEDOWN.
[For The Bulletin.]
Was it thistledown, thistledown,
Fluff from some old goblin’s beard,
Wrinkled, apple-cheeked and weird,
Who in fury tore his beard,
Making thistledown?
Was it thistledown, thistledown,
O’er the tree-tops floating,
Tiny sail of pixie ship,
Breath that left an angel’s lip
Speeds the ether boating?
Was it thistledown, thistledown,
Or a blossom uncontent
(Tired of being knit to stem
In some forest diadem)
On adventure bent ?
Was it thistledown. .. .thistledown,
Feather frail my heart to strike?
Was it Love that passed my way,
Blown across a golden day .
Ah, they are so much alike,
Love and thistledown !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.

Chunder Loo,
Of Akim Foo,
is a poor man,
It is true.
Up the plank
You see him roll
With his basket
Full of coal.
Little worldly
Wealth has he;
But he toils on
Happily,
Singing prayers
In Allah’s praise;
“COBRA” brighte
All his days.

i value of Formamint as a preventive is
appreciated in this house.”

carry Wulfing’s Formamint with me as a

How to Avoid Infectious Diseases.
Do you realise that most of the dangerous infectious
diseases, such as Diphtheria. Consumption, Influenza, Scarlet
Fever, Measles and the like, are caused by germs which gain
entrance to the body through the mouth and throat ?
The minute living organisms which produce such diseases
grow and develop like the seeds of plants, and the mouth and
throat form an ideal hot-house for cultivating them.
The scientist, of course, can grow them outside the body,
artificially. For example, a test-tube culture of the germs
which cause Consumption is depicted here ; and also a bottle
of Wulfing’s Formamint —the means which Science has found
to destroy such germs in the mouth and throat, and so protect
us against the diseases which they breed.
TT* WULFJNG ’5 • .
rormamint
THE GERM-KILLING THROAT TABLET
These harmless and palatable tablets contain the most
powerful destroyer of disease germs known to Science. They
dissolve in the saliva, and the disinfectant is thus carried to
every little crevice, chink, or cranny in the mouth, gums,
tonsils and throat. Thus all germ growths are sought out and
destroyed, their spread is checked, their power to do harm is
entirely broken.
As a preventive of infectious disease, Formamint Tablets
should be used daily by everyone, in health or sickness, and
they are particularly recommended for school children, who
are so exposed to infection, especially at this time of the year.
Beware, however, of harmful imitations ; there is only one,
genuine Formamint, viz. Wulfing’s, and all Chemists sell it at
2S. 6d. per bottle. The manufacturers, A. Wulfing & Co.,
17, Bond Street, Sydney, N.S.W., will gladly send you a Free
Sample and an interesting Booklet, if you write to them,
mentioning this paper, and enclose two penny stamps towards
postage.
“Gyko” • Here’s the whole strength of the
wreck of the Federal liner Devon at Wel-
lington (M.L.) heads recently, as con-
vincingly set out. by the captain, the first
officer (on the bridge), the second officer
(up aloft), the man at the wheel and the
look-out man :
They had passed the last headland visible in day-
light ' (Turakirae) at a suitable offing, and pre-
sently in the darkness they picked up the beam of
Pencarrow light. Their general direction at the
time was west, and the next caper was to pick up
Somes Island light and turn north into the mouth
of the port. Somes Island has three light sectors—
green, white and red. The business is to get into
the white and then steer straight ahead. They did
it. When the white beam was opened up they laid a
course on-it and steered straight ahead for 12 minutes.
Then a typical Maoriland rain gust swooped up
astern, swept over them, and drove up harbor, blot-
ting out the Somes Island light. They steamed on
by compass for five minutes, and when they got the
rain out of their eyes managed to pick up a little
red fixed light. This happened to be a fad light of the
harbormaster’s placed on Falcon shoal (near the en-
trance) and cursed from time immemorial by master
mariners. In their simplicity, the steerers of the
Devon imagined that they had got out of the white
Somes sector into the red sector, and the helm was
put over. The crash came next minute. The har-
bormaster’s fad light had practically engineered the
wreck. But Maoriland wasn’t going to have its
harbormasters and navigation lights knocked about
like that, so the Court solemnly found that the
Devon had never been inside the Somes Island white
sector at all, which was tantamount to saying that
the five men who were the only witnesses on the
situation were plain, unblushing land agents, or
even commercial travellers.
“Dacapede Bay” : The bad habits of these
Westralian State-owned steamers on the
Nor’-West run are likely to provide some
stupid Tory paper with empty arguments
against State ownership, whereas the blame
rests on bad administration —the same sort
of bad administration which often lands Pri-
vate Enterprise in the Bankruptcy Court.
The water shipped on a recent northern run
from Fremantle was bad, and tea made from
it was undrinkable. When you got enough
to wash with it was too filthy to use, and
when it wasn’t too filthy for use you couldn’t
get it. During the voyage a shower fell, and
the steward caught it as it ran off the deck ;
so, clean or not clean, we had one drink of
good tea. We were days behind time, by
reason of bad coal —rubbish sold to the Gov-
ernment at top price. We were 26 hours
from Fremantle to Geraldton, and the usual
time is 19 hours. This steamer was once a
Bussian boat, and was chased by the Japs,
hut had too much foot for them, so I reckon
the brown dwarfs must have been pursuing
on rafts. Full ahead in smooth water it can
do nine knots. And, apart from the ships,
political captains and party ships’ companies
are no good unless they’re as good as the
average crew that isn’t political.
“Simon” : There will be no special exhibi-
tion of Australian-made goods in Sydney
shop-windows this year, but there will be.
’tis said,, a special uncorking of patriot zeal
next year,. And then I will, as usual,
make a '(scoffing round of business houses
which display our own goods for advertising
purposes during five days, and turn them
down religiously the other 360. I claim to
be a manufacturer of decent local goods,
and I will take my oath that any number
of firms which flaunt the “All Australian”
label on such occasions would almost as
soon file their schedule as give a local manu-
facturer a chance where things were about
equal. The reason is this: They want ex-
clusive goods, and they also want to sell at
their usual 50 per cent, profit. Whatever
else they are, local goods are hardly exclu-
sive ; and, besides, there is the danger of
other firms selecting the same lines and
selling at their usual SO per cent. on. The
danger, of course, is that the public will
tumble to the difference in prices. This, is
called “getting caught.” Any clothing, shirt,
tie or hat maker will tell you the same.
Still, things are imnroving, and to-day the
drummer of Australian goods is occasion-
ally sent on his way rejoicing by hearing a
customer demand the Australian article.

The man who is stricken by bis conscience
and refunds the money is often painfully
long-winded. He loves to explain on paper
how his better feelings were gradually awak-
ened, partly by the ministrations of the Rev.
Mr. Foozle, partly by reading the twentieth
verse of the fourth chapter of Ruth, and
partly by marriage and a natural desire to
hand down an unblotted reputation to his
daughter Keziah, now seven years of age and
already in the Second Reader. Then he
darkly signs himself “Repenter” or “Balm of
Gilead,” or something of the sort, and en-
closes 2d. That is the unbusiness-like peni-
tent. The business-like one whirled down on
the N.S.W. railways the other day. He
enclosed 305., with the short remark : “What
IOU.” And Johnson wanted 30s. badly just
then.
A Bulletin correspondent says tha.t a
Foreign Office official told him the following :
Joseph J. Neave, Quaker, who died in Syd-
ney recently achieved, in the course of his
strenuously altruistic career, at least one
triumph that may almost be placed in the
miracle class. Twenty-five years ago he
was a small storekeeper at Queanbeyan
(N.S.W.). It was borne in on him that he
had a mission to relieve the sufferings of the
Russian Stundists, who at that time were
being persecuted vigorously by the adherents
of the Orthodox Church. However, he had
hardly sufficient money to take him to Syd-
ney, much less to Russia. A relative died
unexpectedly and left him £2OO. He went
at once to London. At a large Quaker meet-
ing there he rose up and explained his pur-
pose, which was to go to Russia and tackle
the job of saving the Stundists with his own
hands. Those present were so impressed
bv his earnestness, or something, that they
offered him a considerable sum of money.
He said, “No. The Lord had commissioned
him for this service, and He had made all
the requisite arrangements,” or words to
that effect. The Foreign Office got wind of
his projected adventure, and offered him a
letter of introduction to the British Ambas-
sador in St. Petersburg. He refused, on the
ground that such a missive would be a
“Sword of the Flesh,” whereas the only
weapon he meant to use was of Spiritual
make. Arrived in Russia, he went straieht
to see Pobiedonosteff. who in those days and
for many years afterwards, was the official
head in all ecclesiastical matters. Inciden-
tally. be was one of the most enthusiastic of
the persecutors. Pobiedonosteff happened to
arrive at liis home just as Neave was being
ejected, with contumely, by a porter. He
asked, in English, what was the matter.
Neave replied in the same tongue (the fact
being that he knew no other) with a series of
Quaker salutations and greetings. The
Quaker phraseology has its counterpart, more
or less, in the ordinary colloquial language of
Russia. Pobiedonosteff. who had never be-
fore encountered an Englishman of this type,
became interested, and asked Neave inside.
Ultimately he took him to the Czar. The
Czar was so impressed by the N. S. Wales
trader’s presentment of the case for Stundism
that he did an almost unprecedented thing.
He issued a rescript, which gave. Neave
authority to investigate the whole situation,
cross-examine officials, etc., inspect those
prisons where the Stundists were confined,
and get details as to the treatment of all
religious confinees. Amongst other things,
the rescript called upon all Russians to aid
Neave in his investigations, to the utmost of
their ability. Neave’s report was made
direct to the Czar. The effect of it was to
produce a complete reform in the attitude of
the Government and the Church towards the
Stundists. As soon as he realised that all
was well, or, at any rate, as well as it
humanly could be, this astonishing man
returned unobtrusively to Australia, and,
without any public mention of his triumph
settled down to the old life. It is a queer
commentary on the disproportionateness of
things that one who, like Neave. carried
through a venture that made many thousands
happy should have died in comparative
obscurity.
Concerning some early land-hunters :
Mr. G. 11. F. Cox, great-grandson of that Captain
William Cox who built the first road over the Blue
Mountains (N.S.W.), has written a history of Mudgee
from the day on which his grandfather camped at
Menah (or Munna), in 1821. William Lawson, one
of the three who had crossed the Mountains in 1813,
was commandant at Bathurst, and in his spare time
made excursions into new territory. In an expedition
towards Liverpool Plains he crossed the country now
known as Mudgee, but failed to reach Liverpool
Plains. On his return to Bathurst, he informed his
friend George Cox, then managing Hereford for his
father, Wm. Cox, of the splendid pastoral country
through which he had passed, and advised him to
secure some of it. To this suggestion George Cox
lent a copious ear. After consulting his father, he
set out, early in 1821, accompanied by
Richard Lewis, who had been Captain Cox’s
overseer in the road-making, and William Lee, the
founder of the Bathurst Lee family, who had worked
with Lewis on the road. Taking with them two
“servants,” they travelled as far as Tabragar, and
found the country all that could be desired, except
for a scarcity of permanent water. All three-;
land, water and herbage—were, however, found in
what is' now known as Mudgee. Returning to
Bathurst, George Cox sent for his brother Henry,
who was managing at Mulgoa for his father. The
pair, with Richard Lewis and 500 head of cattle,
two stockmen and two laborers, set off for the
promised land. They followed the track originally
blazed by Lawson until they reached Menah, where
they decided to settle. In th§ night the cattle
stampeded, and a davlight search found them at
Burrundulla Swamp, which years after became the
homestead. The huge gum-tree close to the river
bank, where the brothers first camped, is still stand-
ing. The Cox family will allow no axe to touch it
William Lawson followed the Coxes, and, as in dutv
bound, to him, as discoverer, King gave first pick
of the country. He chose the other side of the river,
and there the Lawsons still hold country. The his-
tory has been tvnewritten and bound. Only six conies
have been issued, and these are for the Cox family
and friends. But the booklet should be published, as
it is a valuable contribution to the history of Dinner
settlement west of the Blue Mountains. p neer
All things considered, it is an extraor-
dinary fact that Frank Madden has had the
longest innings of any Speaker of the Vic-
Assembly, save the original Francis Mur-
phy. who kept company with the Mace
from 1850 to 1871. None of the following
10 Speakers lasted longer than seven years.
Then came Frank Madden in 1904, and he
is still disguising his natural non-dignit.v
under the Speaker’s wig. For nine long
years the Vic. Assembly has been content
to regard Frank as its First Commoner.
Probably the best index to the character
of a nation is to be found in the sort of
advertisements it publishes. When A.S.W.
was a lot less decent than it now is, it
allowed the great dailies to print the ap-
peals of obviously quack doctors, villain-
ously quack medicines guaranteed to cure
anything from impecuniosity to large feet,
establishments devoted to pre-natal murder
and other iniquities. This type of ad. still
gets through occasionally, but only occa-
sionally. The country has grown more
decent, and the daily paper has had to do
likewise. The writer picked up a Yankee
sporting publication the other day, and
having glanced through its columns, he lit
a very bad cigar to disinfect the atmosphere.
Imagine a community depraved enough to
allow manufacturers to advertise marked
cards, loaded dice; and bogus money. The
United States /does, and marked cards are
obtainable at one'dollar a pack, impossibility
of detection guaranteed, and money re-
turned if found unsatisfactory—strictly
honest dealing in the tools of robbing and
swindling. “Crap Dice” are $2 a pair,
and again money is returned if the goods
are proved unsatisfactory. Hand-colored
“stunning female beauty cards,” 25 cents
for packet of five ; “Four Nifty Photos 10
cents”; “Twenty Confessions of Twenty
Divorced Women —a thriller from cover to
cover” ; “Classy Den Pictures. $1 for 15 —
no two alike” : “Sneezing Powder. Great-
est joke out. Blow a little in the air and
everybody must sneeze. 25 cents a bottle”:
“B 4 & After! A Maiden’s Dream. 12 r<ch
art gems, all 10 cents”; and last but not
least this :
The Rose Door, a brand new book. The first one
of the kind ever published. It gives a vivid account
of the actual lives of four fallen girls, the cause of
their downfall and whv they could never reform. A
complete history of life at the Greatest Resort on
earth. 200 pages, cloth bound, illustrated.

Where fragrant airs their whispers blend

What time their weary wand’rings end
In some proud city, towered and free
Her billet-doux!
I hear thee say, my cynic friend,
And words of cautious counsel spend;
But, if thou doubt she lives for ine
Who thus could write: “I love but tneer -
I blame thee not —thou hast not kenned
Her billet-doux 1

POLITICAL
POINTS
THE RISING TIDE.
The throwing out of Meehan, member for
the Darling (N.S.W.), has become so nearly
an everyday function that his final vanish-
ment at the end of the session will be a
relief. (He has lost the party nomination,
so presumably he will return to the West
to hold converse with the bears and kan-
garoos. ) Meantime, it would be a kindly
act to suspend him for the rest of the ses-
sion and pair him with Wood. No one could
seriously object to the suspension of Wood.
Poor old unready China has no luck. Two
*or three Japanese got killed by parties that
the Crockery Republic couldn’t control; they
got in front of an insurrection, and waved a
Jap flag, and in effect asked the insurgents
to shoot at the tails of their coats, and the
insurgents shot accordingly. Japan de-
manded about half the globe as compensation
and China accepted its proposals without a
kick. Then Japan decided that it had been
too moderate, and it has apparently re-opened
the question, and wants more compensation.
And presumably China!, which hasn’t had a
streak of good fortune since it cut off its
pigtail, will have to cave in once more.
Even the kindly old S. M. Herald has its
embittered moments, and it came near to
hoisting the flag of bloodshed and disem-
bowelment and insurrection in its leader ot
September IS, re Victoria’s claim to the
country between the Murrumbidgee and the
present border-line. It got so far on its
foaming career as to state that: Should
she [Victoria] succeed in tearing territory
from New South Wales—a possibility which
we absolutely deny —she [Victoria | must
see that her own fate would be decided almost
at a moment’s notice.” So apparently
Melbourne is going to be burned in short
order. The problem, after all, is a very
simple one. The decision of the British
Government was that the north-eastern
boundary of Victoria was to a beeline
drawn from Cape Howe to the nearest
headwaters of the Murray 1 liver, and after
that the water was to be the boundary. In
the then general ignorance of geography
the line was drawn from Cape Howe to
the Indi River, which falls into the Mur-
ray. It is now known that a line drawn
to the Murrumbidgee, which a[so falls into
the Murray, would have been much shorter,
and that line would have given Riverina to
Victoria. Apparently the legal right is with
Victoria, but New South Wales has the
right of long possession. So there is
material for an interesting scrap.
HIS BEST GIRL.
Mr. Matthews: Why was the fleet taken into the
danger zone, when there were so many clean ports
that it could be taken to?
The Prime Minister: I don’t know why they are
being taken into the danger zone. I have no doubt
the men have been vaccinated, and are willing to
face anything, even small-pox.
Item, from the Federal Parliament.
THE OTHER: “Hussy!"
Commonwealth Statistician Knibbs _is
going to haye another try at ascertaining
how the money goes. Two years back he
issued specially-prepared account books to
householders who 1 were willing to keep them
posted, but not enough householders were
willing to make the data of much value.
Now, Cost of Living being still a very
healthy and active bogey, he is asking for
volunteers to keep their domestic accounts
for a month instead of the former year. In
particular, he wants returns from persons
with very small incomes. The information
will, of course, be treated as strictly confi-
dential. Knibbs’s address is the Bureau of
Statistics, Melbourne.
From the Vic. State Gallery :
Even the press gang didn’t expect Prendergast’s
retirement. In the early part of his career he and
Watt played a game of in-and-out. “Pren” entered
Parliament in 1894, for North Melbourne, but was
ousted by Watt in 1897. Then in turn he ousted
Watt, who thereafter was out of politics for several
years, unable to squeeze in anywhere, albeit he had
done well enough in the House to be given a seat
in the McLean Cabinet. Prendergast succeeded
Bromley as Labor leader in 1904,' Somehow, nobody
could think of him as a Premier. If Anstey had
stayed in State politics he would have taken the
lead now, and might soon have arrived at the Pre-
miership. But he wouldn’t have had much back-
ing, for th 6 Laborites in the Victorian Parliament
are a weak lot. Elmslie, the new leader, has no
distinction, and is not equal to Prendergast. Watt
whacks ’the lot of them in every debate. Next to
Elmslie is Robert Solly—able, but too quietly philo-
sophic. Next must be reckoned John -Lemmon. Good
qualities again, but he never seems to get beyond
the juvenile-lead stage. These men droop beside
Anstey, Tudor, Russell and even Findley, all of
them in the National House, as the best men should
be.
A Flying Item :
If Senator Millen doesn’t restrain his rash haste
he may yet accomplish something towards starting
Australia’s aviation corps. The present position is
as follows. Early this year the Fisher Government
ordered a Bristol' biplane. It was stored in Sydney
pending the arrival of the imported fliers. The first
thing the experts were called upon to do was to
choose a site. They selected Point Cook, adjacent
to Port Phillip Bay. Then Minister Millen happened
—and profound inaction. The site, of 750 acres,
may be presently acquired, at the stipulated price
of £BOOO, if nothing untoward intervenes, but there
is no certainty that something won’t intervene. Also
a “hangar” must be built. A hangar is a common
wood-and-tin shed, which a half-dozen carpenters
could build in a week. (This is mentioned merely
to dispel the idea that it is an enterprise ranking in
order of vastness with the construction of a cathe-
dral.) Meanwhile the biplane has been humped to
Melbourne and unpacked, and found to be in s a
hopeless state of health. The canvas wings are rotten
and other parts are in such a condition that no
sane aviator would care to risk his life in them or
on them. It would probably be cheaper as well as
safer —the biplane cost £lOOO, and the canvas alone
will cost £lO0 —to get a new ’plane. Alternatively,
the country might get a new Minister.
This is the time when the aspiring politi-
cian gets all the trouble that is coining to
him. First he stands for the nomination.
Then, if he has luck, he stands for the elec-
tion. Then he doesn’t sit for the constitu-
ency, but he feels very much exhausted about
the feet. . .
A sense of shame, or something akin to it,
has at last induced a block of M estralian
Parliamentarians to withdraw their opposi-
tion to a railway to run inland from Fspei-
ance Bay. on the south coast. The original
proposition, so bitterly fought against, was
to join Esperance with Kalgoorlie. 11ns
would have meant decentralisation, because
some goldfields’ trade would be diverted
from Fremantle and Perth. The converts
still insist that the goldfields are a pigeon
that belongs to Fremantle and 1 ertn ex-
clusively, wherefore they have _ stipulated
that the line shall go only 60 miles inland
from the bay. So long as it stops there
the goldfields’ pigeon will still be then-
pigeon. It’s a wretched stipulation; still,
60 miles is a stage, and the goldfields can
talk about the rest afterwards.
It was during a debate on the Federal
electoral laws, at the Women’s Fashionable
League’s conference at Ballarat, that one
rabid “Liberal,” Mrs. Ottery, complained of
the blind and deaf being allowed to vote.
“If it were not for the blind and deaf vot-
ing,” remarked the lady (Age 19/9/lo),
“Mr McKay would have been returned for
Ballarat.” This statement was vigorously
applauded, after which several other irra-
tional leaguers (whose statements were also
vigorously applauded) voiced strong con-
demnation of the Labor party for abolishing
the postal vote, and thus “depriving old
people and invalids of the power to exercise
the franchise” !
Michael Burke, one time an alderman of
the City of Sydney, has been elected again
to that eminent municipal synod, repre-
senting Pyrmont ward, vice Andy Kelly,
deceased. In its haste to impart the good
news, a special edition of the Government
Gazette was issued four days after the
election, and three days after everyone inter-
ested had heard about it. This was not
done to give Michael some extra swank, but
because some fool of an Act ordered it to be
done. What the billygoat of a law would
have done if nobody had brought out the
special Gazette the ass of a Statute doesu t
say.
Ex-Premier Verran (S.A.), lately relieved
of the Labor leadership, has been letting Ins
tongue run away again. He was inveighing
against the wickedness of the Government in
cutting off the mining town of Wallaroo
from the agricultural part of the district in
order to give the Liberals a chance of getting
in a member or two. His cue was to slang
the Ministry, but he switched off on to the
farmers. Providence had been too good to
them ; nine fat years had swelled their heads,
he said. Two good years followed by two
bad years to keep them humble was. his
prescriotion. The Labor paper unwisely
printed the speech, and then there was the
Devil to pay, for the farming vote controls
two-thirds of the Assembly, and if it were
all turned against Labor the Liberals would
have a gaudy time. In the struggle to make
capital out of the ex-leader’s words, it hasn’t
mattered a bit, of course, that poor blunder-
ing old Verran roared just what the very
best Tories whisper—that what is wanted is
a bad year or two to bring the wage-earners
to their senses.

All goods traffic leaves the Adelaide rail-
way station yard this week for the new
goods-sheds at Mile End, just outside the
western boundary of the city. The passenger
trains that still run from all points into the
bottle-necked North Terrace station will now
be able to sort themselves out better; but
they will all still have to go out the same
way as they came in, and the problem of
making a circuit becomes a more difficult
and costly one with every year that passes.

INCURABLE LIBERAL.
FINANCE.
[For The Bulletin.]
They would find that he had not been extravagant.
He felt sorry for the man who was to follow him
“We have in reality 48 millions in business
undertakings, producing revenue, whilst the balance
of 24 millions, though not in active reproduction,
produces revenue indirectly just the same! The
resources of the State are so unlimited and the
outlook so good that we will have no trouble in
finding money to finance things.’’ — N.S.W. Treasurer
Cann, according to S. M. lleisald.
A certain Minister named Cann —
A plain and unpretentious* man —
Was put in charge
Of spendings large—
And that was when the fun began!
The constable he soon outran,
Egged on by others of the clan;
And in a year,
Or so, ’twas clear
He’d beat all records by a span!
AVith high aplomb and great elan
He scattered sovereigns by the cran,
Or basket-full —
He had the “pull,”
For there was none to bar or ban!
He paid no heed to “if” and “an,”
But, like a modern Kubla Khan,
Went straight ahead
With loans, and said —
“The whole wide world shall come and scan
The noble works we mean to plan
Yea! from Be-ersheba to Dan
Our name and fame
Shall brightly flame —
At finance we are in the van!”
He crowed like any wild Houdan
Before it frizzles in the pan !....
’Twixt you and me,
I think that he
Is marching on to his Sedan —
He’ll find out by the first of Jan.

ALMOST BLIND.
SPOTS BEFORE THE EYES CAUSED
BY LIVER DISORDERS.
Mr. Tom Holt, whose remarkable state-
ment is published below, is another example
of what Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills have
done for one who has suffered from that
most common trouble, “A Liver.” Mr. Holt
is an old resident and property holder of
Bondi Junction, and now living at 55 Stan-
ley Street, Sydney, N.S.W., and his state-
ment is a voluntary one and purely an ex-
MR. TOM HOLT
(from a photo.).
pression of gratitude
for the benefit he has
derived from taking
Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills. He* tried
doctors and hospital
treatment to no avail,
and actually states
that these pills saved
his life. He has been
taking them now for
ten years, and still
continues to recom-
mend them.
“W ithout Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root
Pills I don’t know
what would become of me. For a number
of years I have suffered with a sluggish
liver, which produced constipation to such
an extent that sometimes I have gone for
days without a movement of the bowels.
Spots appeared floating before my eyes, and
I almost went blind, and got so bad that 1
went to the hospital as an outdoor patient.
They treated me for my eyes, but with no
result —in fact, I became worse. Someone
told me my blindness was due to , liver
trouble, and recommended Dr. Morse’s In-
dian Root Pills, which I began using with
the most gratifying results. In a short
time my sifirht was as good as ever, and the
spots which had been floating in the air
were gone, to my great relief. I was in
such a condition that work was impossible,
because I was sleepy and drowsy all the
time, with no energy to do anything. This
was ten years ago, and I have continued
the use of the pills ever since, taking two
or three as I need them. I believe they
saved my life, and I consider them a most
valuable medicine. From time to time
have been persuaded to try other remedies,
but I never got the benefit I received from
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills. In fact, the
other medicines are of no use. lam an old
resident of Bondi Junction, and now living
at 55 Stanley Street, Sydney, _am well
known, and have much pleasure in making
this statement, which is voluntarily given,
and will always be ready to personally con-
firm same.
(Signed)
“TOM HOLT.”

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Nigel Barker, champion N.S.W. amateur
sprinter of five years ago, is the latest to attempt
to “come back.” The attraction is the promised,
though undecided, visit of the five Yankee sprint
champions early next year. Barker had a few trials
with the Great Public Schools cracks when they
were training last week, and left them all behind;
so it the Yankee visit does eventuate, the ex-Sydney
University sprinter should give a good account of
himself. He still has four Australian records to his
name: 200yds., 19 9-10 sec. ; 220yds., 21 4-ssec. ;
300yds., 31sec. ; and 440yds., 48isec. The event he
has his eye on this time is the 440yds.
“0.K.”: Bonnie Ruby Roberts and boy
Walter Lindrum, properly boomed and staged, might
be worth a good many hundred pounds gate-money
in England this coming winter. Considered as a
draw for the public in general, rather than for
billiard cranks and experts in particular, the maiden
arid the lad would be more effective than any pair
of adult male champions. Their fresh young skill
in the game is more instructive to ordinary amateurs
than the finest play of the mere men champions.
But the chief attraction of the show is in its sex
rivalry and human interest. The women who went
to see Ruby and Walter play beautiful billiards at
Alcock’s Rooms (Melb.) the other week were as taken
by the personality of the performers as the men
were staggered by the precocity of little Walter’s
talent. The meagre boy made it a sufficiently good
race by conceding a start of 2000 in 7000 to the
buxom girl, who finished within two or three hun-
dred points of him. If they were touring in Eng-
land the managerial policy, of course, would be to
give Miss Roberts a rather longer start and spread
tales of how her young friend always gnashed his
teeth —in private—when he got beaten. In Australia
women are invited to come gratis to the Ruby-
Waiter matches. In the Cold Country, where 20
times as many women are familiar with the rudi-
ments of billiards, they would be expected to bring
the men along to buy seats for them. Moreover, an
enterprising manager would probably secure the par-
ticular patronage of the Pankhurst League, and
reserve a section of the seating accommodation for
suffragettes.
* * * *
The sport of road racing aboard cycles
had a little incident brought into it in Victoria the
other Saturday, when one Blake just managed to
beat a certain J. A. Cochrane at the finish of the
Northern Suburbs C.C. Bracelet. The discovery that
all was lost, and that the bangle could never be his,
so worked on Cochrane’s feelings that he stepped
up to the judge and passed him a couple of dennv-
heysers or woodeners. Another was presented to an
official who interposed ; and finally the defeated glad-
iator begged to be allowed to fight the entire com-
mittee. A section of the crowd of 1000 and odd
citizens who witnessed the affray reckoned it was
creditable, inasmuch as it proved, anyhow, that the
go was on the dead level. Not so the committee.
Having retired to the privacy of an armor-plated
boiler or something, they passed the warrior ouU for
two vears.
* * * *
“Weston the Walker,” famous as a pedes-
trian in the 70's of the last century, turns out to
be still alive. It was he who set the fashion for the
idiotic test of endurance known as “1000 miles in
1000 hours,” which meant doing on a track (some-
times even in a hall) a walk of as far from Sydney
to Adelaide in a succession of jerks lasting six
weeks. One mile had to be done in each and every
hour; and generally it was worked this way: A
mile from 1.45 to 2, another from 2 to 2.15, then
rest till 3.4 s—and so on. Weston turns up now with
a walk of 1400 miles, from New York to Minne-
apolis (U.S.A.), done in June and July. Its onlv
interest, supposing it was all genuine, lies in the fact
that Weston is now nearly 75. He says—or the
Yankee press says for him—that he promised at 50
to show himself “as fit as ever” a quarter of a centurv
later. Make it “as futile,” and The Bulletin will
come in with a burst of applause, and wave all its
bad debts round its head.
The “Sporting . Commission” of the
French Automobile Club is busy just now drafting
the. conditions for the next Grand Prix. The question
which obfuscates the Commission is whether it
shall or shall not include a tyre test. Some cars
devour tyres twice as fast as others, and the reason
why should be a thing worth knowing. There is
some reason for it, and a careful examination under
nroper test conditions would probably disclose what
it is, to the everlasting joy of the man who pays
for the machine’s footwear.
* * * *
Hockey, which has alwavs been a favorite
game in Maoriland, is evidently on a good footing, as
it is now talking of international matches. It is
proposed to invite an English team to leave the Cold
Country in April next and play 12 matches during
a six-week's’ tour of the Kiwi Islands.. N.S.W.
hockey is now sufficiently developed to consider the
question of playing Maoriland, and although the
Cornstalks would probably get beaten during the
first raid, the experience would be beneficial, as well
as interesting. Maoriland is a good place for a
holiday.
Rosehill (Sydney) race-meeting on Satur-
day was a preliminary canter for a host of prads
engaged in the big events of the coming Spring
Meeting at Randwick a fortnight hence. It was an
hilarious time for Maorilanders, as four of the six
events were won by gallant steeds from over-sea,
such as Soltano, which downed a well-bred field in
the Soring Stakes (w.f.a.), and Beau Soult, which
gathered the Rosehill Guineas from an army of
brethren in brutedom. Both these animals are con-
cerned in the A.J.C. Derby and other big deeds of
the season. And they seem worth watching.
There returned to his native-land lately,
after 12 years’ absence, mostly in the United States,
an Australian of some size. Rupert Jeffkins, to wit.
Jeff, was born in Maitland (N.S.W.), and took to
motor-racing early, having taken part in the Bou-
logne-sur-mer road-race of ’O4, when 20 miles an
hour was thought wonderful. Since then he has
put up temporary records on the Dayton, Indian-
opolis, San Jose and other tracks, and is one of the
only 18 drivers licensed by the United States Auto-
mobile Association to take part in motor-racing con-
tests. Jeffkins lately ran a car round the slightly-
banked cinder track on Richmond (Vic) racecourse
at a 70-mile gait, and threatens to hold a motor-
racing carnival there at Cup-time. There is sport
and money in motor-racing beyond doubt, but the
Richmond course is only a saucer. A proper sneed-
way is wanted, at least a mile to the lap, and it
would be necessary to go at least 15 miles out, say,
to Mentone racecourse or the country behind, tft get
it.
On Saturday last there was held around
London an Aerial “Derby,” for the Daily Mail
Cup. It was won by a foreigner named Hamel, on
a French monoplane, with Barnwell second, and
Hawker, the Australian who attempted to fly round
Britain, third. Sydney Pickles, the other Australian
airman, did not compete, it being understood that
he and Hawker are in the same stable. After the
Derby was over, however, Pickles gave a display,
and a lady aviator named Mrs. Stocks induced a
passenger to give her his seat. That passenger’s
luck was in, for, owing to the engine misfiring, the
machine dived, got out of hand and fell, so severely
injuring both Pickles and Mrs. Stocks that at time
of writing their lives are in serious jeopardy. This
holding of Aerial Derbies, Skybourne Cups and
Cloud Doncasters around the environs of a crowded
city is likely to lead to some excitement. It is all
right as long as the machines don’t run off the
course, but if they do and come down crash in a
busy street, it ought to make for some excitement.
Some time ago a Yankee millionairess was holding
festival in her conservatory when an aeroplane
crashed through the roof and scattered things. No-
body was killed, not even the aeronaut, who climbed
out of the wreckage and explained it was a good
job he hit the conservatory, as the framework of the
roof broke the force of the fall. He remarked, as
he left, that thev- could keep the ruins of the ’plane
as it was no further use to him, being in bits, and
he had also taken such a sudden dislike to flying
that had it been uninjured he never wanted to see
the durned thing again. Then he fled.
Sid Burns suffers from staggers, and takes
four helpings of count prior to succumbing to
Arthur Evernden at Sydney Stadium.
Summer baseball burst on Sydney with a
bang on Saturday, when, at the Sports Ground,
Petersham and Leichhardt put up a drawn game
with 12 runs each, before a surprising crowd of
paying public. The new game appears certain to
catch on. Still, trouble is ahead. There is friction
over the question of control between the Baseball
Association and the £5OOO Syndicate which is run-
ning'the summer branch. If the Syndicate doesn’t give
way there will be wholesale disqualification, fol-
lowed by a struggle which will practically kill both
summer and winter branches of the game for a
few years. Anyhow, the outlook for cricket is
considerably clouded for the moment.
* * * *
Victorian baseballers struck an awful
whirlwind in Adelaide on Saturday when the gentle
Groweaters pounded their guests by 23 to six in a
seven-innings game. The time of devastation was
the South Australian sixth innings, when the Vic-
torian pitchers were smitten for 15 runs, and Hope-
fell dead and Possibility gave up the ghost.
* * * *
Sydney Grammar School maintained its
monopoly of the Great Public Schools athletic pre-
miership last week by scooping up the honor for the
18th successive year. It’s a wonderful record.
* * * *
The Soccer struggle for the State Cham-
pionship of N.S.W. still drags on. On Saturday
Annandale and Sydney played off their drawn game ;
and a great game it was —hard, fast and scrupu-
lously clean. But it ended in nothing, so to speak,
as both sides scored three goals, and though extra
play was ordered, neither side managed to add to
the stature of its score. So they will meet again
next Saturday, amidst tremendous excitement. New-
town, which had already qualified for the final, re-
played its match with Granville, and by dint of
roughness, unfair play, and generally disreputable
tactics managed to bog out on top with three goals
to two.
Treasurer Green, of the N.S.W. Cricket
Association is quite a different type of chancellor to
his friend Cann, who looks after the State exchequer.
Green decided to knock out the grant to assist
country clubs during the coming season and justified
it this way: “We have just enough money to carry
through the season and finance the S’African tour
next year. If we have a good inter-State year it
will be all right and we may have a few pounds to
spare—but I don't think we can count on a tiptop
inter-State year.” So rigid economy is the order of
the day.
* * * *
N.S.W. bowlers came to a shocking end
at Brisbane last week when Queensland trampled
them underfoot by 113 points to 70. The second
Test went the same way, except that the scores were
only 91 and 88. The final was a hurricane effort
by both sides, and the Ma Staters pulled themselves
together for one supreme effort to make it safe to
return home. But Queensland’s response was just
as strenuous, and the result was a drawn game
with 94 points all. In the three matches Queensland
won with an aggregate of 298 to 252—the sort of
thing Ma is not accustomed to.
* * * *
It was also on Saturday that President
Gardner of the Newcastle Bowlers fell on Sydney
with eight drinks—or rather rinks. President
Spence opposed him with a similar force, so there
were 16 drinks in all. In spite of small-pox, vac-
cination and other things he managed to repel the
invasion by 187 points to 184.
There was considerable hoofball on the
Metropolitan Cricket Ground, Melbourne,- on Satur-
day, and very little anywhere else. From noon till
3 p.m. teams of sprightly nippers held the ground,
and then the representatives of St. Kilda and Fitz-
roy stepped smartly out, before the largest crowd
ever seen on the ground (54,747), and for the next
two hours afforded a really joyous spectacle. The
Maroons, with their extra weight, wanted to make
the game a series of close, congested rushes, but
the lighter Saints weren’t taking any. Their business
was to keep the play open, and they kept it so.
Their passing was superb, the forwards manipulating
the ball with wonderful precision and speed. At
the first change they had acquired four goals two
behinds to one goal two behinds. Then for a while
’Roy got its own way; its big followers charged
through crushes, striving to win by main force,
and during the quarter several men were temporarily
slain, and remained dead for a while pending re-
covery. At the interval, ’Roy had five goals six
behinds to St. lvilda’s five goals four behinds. The
’Roy men’s third quarter is notorious. It is then
they mostly put on a lead that is neve\' overtaken,
and they set about it at once, getting a pair of
behinds —but that was the end of it. The Saints had
strict orders to open the play at all risks, and
did so, so that at lemon-time they had added two
goals four behinds, giving them a two-goal lead. It
was up to then a beautiful but stubborn, hard-
fought game, and neither side could afford to make
a mistake. In the last term the Seasiders kept up
the same open, systematic play, and put on an addi-
tional three goals two behinds. The Maroons, how-
ever, . made a large mistake. They lost their heads
for a while, the big men rushing in with needless
violence, charging and slinging players who were
not in possession of the ball, while now and again
a clenched fist could be seen landing heavily. With
a strict umpire these capers only brought them dis-
aster and the hoots of the crowd, so that when the
bell rang, with Saints 10 goals 10 behinds to ’Roy’s
six goals nine behinds, and the stronger and fairer
side had won, most of the spectator departed well
satisfied. ’Roy has the right to a deciding game,
which will occur next Saturday; but unless the
Maroons change their method of play they have
little hope of winning, though a wet day will give
them an advantage. The “gate” was the biggest
on record—£2lo4 —but the League has a dim sus-
picion it ought to have been a few pounds more.
That body regularly issues some hundreds of compli-
mentary tickets —to club officials, etc. —and during
the week certain evil-disposed persons forged a num-
ber of shamefully accurate imitations of these, and
sold them about the city. The fraud was discov-
ered, but not before some had been used ; while it is
strongly suspected that the “gates” of the two pre-
vious Saturdays suffered largely from the same
unsuspected cause. And the impecunious League
has quite enough claimants on its “gates,” as
things are.
* * * *
Reginald Baker, commonly known as
“Snowy” of that ilk, is an ascetic. He has never
been heard to swear, and he never tasted champagne
till he came under the evil influence of the writer of
this paragraph. Then he not only tasted the cham-
pagne, but paid for it. But with all his aloofness
of appetite the physical condition in which some of
his boxers enter the ring has placed a heavy strain on
his tongue, and he has felt that only language could
express his feelings on the subject. So he has deter-
mined to supervise the training operations, and satisfy
himself that all bruisers are fit and properly well to
fight to a finish in a 20-round scuffle before he allows
them to enter the ring. He will pay unexpected
visits at all hours, and woe betide the bruiser who is
found playing fan-tan when he should be skipping or
knocking the corners of Sheol off his boxing partners.
The new order of things has already commenced, and
the first man who was visited happened,* for the good
of his soul, to be training—not drinking. About the
second, third and fourth man, and so on up to the
seventeenth man, there is no information to hand.
But the eighteenth man is solemnly warned that
Baker has an eye on him, and he had better
straighten up.
Arthur Evernden, the English welter-
weight who some time ago defeated the popular
Frank Picato, demonstrated at Sydney on Saturday
night that he is still a man with a punch. His
opponent was that Hebraic youth who fights under
the name of Sid Burns. What his real cognomen is,
writer knoweth not. It may be Moses Solomonson.
Properly trained, he is a fighter whose vicious box-
ing is in the first grade, who has a good command
over his feet and a better over his fists, and who is
able to hit harder than most. Armed with these
qualifications, Burns seemed booked for a long and
profitable stay in Australia. But on Saturday night
the glory passed away, and the £l2OO he had a
chance to win during the next few months is now
dangling before the long upper lip of Evernden.
Which may seem hard luck for Burns, but he cer-
tainly conduced to it by entering the ring against
so hard a fighter as Evernden when out of proper
condition. It seems that when they met in Eng-
land Burns out-boxed his rugged adversary, and
scored a decision on points. Perhaps that fact
caused him to hold Evernden too cheanly. The
long white road from Jerusalem to Jericho and
Sodom is strewn with the bones of men who held
the opponent -too cheaply. It is mighty bad policy
to neglect the training—men who can fight out of
condition, as Jeremiah Jerome did when he met
Bradley at a moment’s notice, are few, few indeed.
It must be admitted that at first Burns easily out-
boxed Evernden and scored whatever points were
going. Could he have kept going till the finish he
would probably have won on points ; but his lack
of fitness caused him to fall a victim to the ruthless
battering in the 10th round. He could not avoid
Evernden. That boring Gentile crowded right on to
him, butted through his guard, and smote with a
ton of smite. Evernden fought quite a different
battle from that he put up against the nlncky
Picato. He used an unpicturesque but effective
With his shoulders pushed forward and
his palms resting on his face on either side, his jaw
was completely covered and his bingey out of reach.
Swaying from side to side he would waddle round
after Burns, draw his lead, and then one fist or the
other would shoot out, and the whole of the weight
of the body would be hurled in after it, driving it
home. He didn’t hit —he lunged and put his weight
behind it, and when he made impact the effect was
terrific. Other times he would get in close and
uppercut with a vicious jerk. But, as he had to
swing the fist to do this Burns was able to see what
was coming and avoid it. It was not till the fifth,
when his condition began to fail, that Burns com-
menced to find trouble. He was smiling at the
attacks of Evernden when that doughty warrior
handed him a right which sent him down for seven
seconds and rattled him badly. Up again, he
showed much more caution, but was knocked down
again with a right hook just as the gong went.
Burns looked very groggy as he came out, and
Evernden stood in to finish him But Burns boxed
him off, and towards the end he hurled Evernden
backwards, followed him up and gave him one to go
on with as the bell clanged. The eighth found
Burns in trouble again, being down twice for eight.
But when he got up from the first knock-down he
all but outed Evernden with a right swing to the
side of the head. It was while following up that he
received the wallop on the jaw that sent him down
for the second time. In the ninth it was very
patent his strength was giving out. His seconds
vainlv bellowed to him to hang on. He continued
to mix it, but was shockingly slow in the breaks-
away. He was obviously done. He came out in the
10th to take the inevitable. It came quickly, and
he was down for nine seconds. Then he rose once
more, and staggered' into the fray, hoping against
hope. Another right sent him down again, and he
rolled on to bis back, his heels shot up in the air,
and the police interference and the towel ear™
simultaneously—so £l2OO vanished like a beautifVi
dream. It was nearly five minutes before Burn,
was able to leave the ring. AH for the want of,
little more training. d
As all interested parties know, Saturday
night will present what is confidently J
pected to be about the most stubborn fight ever seen
in the Sydney Stadium; and one of the most im
portant—a fight which will be followed with interest
all over the Anglo-Stoush world. Moran and Wells
meet. As a record crowd is expected, special
arrangements have been made to cope with the rush
The doors will be open at 6.30 p.m., and over 100
ushers will be on duty to handle the people. As
the real battle does not start till 8.30, the patrons
will be entertained with a preliminary bout of 15
two-minute rounds between Bob King and Hector
Melville —both good fighters who have been sparrin°-
with Moran and Mehegan respectively. The rounds
have been shortened to enable them to make the
pace fast. As for the big scrap itself, both men are
absolutely confident. Moran is, like Wells, just
about the perfect build for a boxer. He is a bit
more rugged than Wells but not quite so robustious,
and will scale very little over 9st., while Wells will
be close up to lOst. Both men have trained very
hard indeed, and there will be no lack of condition.
Wells announces that he will fight a totally different
battle from that he put up against Mehegan—and it
is to be devoutly hoped he will, for that was not an
attractive display. He concludes his remarks with
a promise to knock Moran out. Moran doesn’t care
a hang what Wells’s weight may be, neither does
he mind a rap what sort of scrap Wells fights—it’s
all one to him. He carries the punch, he believes,
and if the punch is there and can be delivered, what
does it matter what attitude the receiver is in when
he gets it ?
e* * * *
It seems that in addition to an injured
nose Pat Bradley sustained a dislocation of one of his
thumbs the night he met the whirling Prophet
Jeremiah, and although the nose has healed, the
thumb, at time of writing, makes serious prepara-
tion for his postponed fight with Smith quite im-
possible. Later on it is understood that Bradley
will be put up against Evernden —and won’t that be
a dream! Jerome’s broken hand has put him out
of the hunt for some considerable time, but he will
be matched with Bradley again after the Basher
has met Smith and Evernden. The return scrap
between Wells and Mehegan will rage in Melbourne
oi Cup eve. In fact, Cup week in Melbourne is going
to be also Stoush week, with three championship
fights: Mehegan-Wells, Evernden-Summers, and Bill
Lang with the winner of the Doran-Bell contest.
Incidentally, writer saw Bell at his training-camp last
week, and is firmly convinced that it is only lack of
moral courage that prevents him being among the
world’s boss heavyweights. He is unusually skilful,
and a mighty hitter before the gods; but in the (
ring, somehow, he seems to straggle about the back- *
bone.

“Jean Sibi,” from the Federal press gal-
lery :
As an admirer of oratory, plain or colored, I
beg to draw attention to the lack of effective speakers
in the House of Reps. I doubt if there are more
than three or four' rnefi on either side who have the
real gift of speech concealed about them. Hughes
is usually fine and trenchant,, but s be needs to be
roused from the comfortable serenity that, is grow-
ing upon him. Glynn has a Hibernian fluency that
is pleasant in moderation, and Bruce Smith has a
measured delivery, to which he is the most atten-
tive listener. To my mind, the trouble with Bruce
is not the method but the matter of his orations.
In a manner of speaking, he gives you the bones
without the bovril. Roberts, I think, is the most
forceful in the House —an incisive talker who argues
with an impression of profound suffering. Kelly is
a distant echo of Oxford, with insufficient urbanity
to make his mistakes charming. Iceberg Irvine is
good, and is occasionally guilty of a faint note of
passion which his countenance declines to corroborate.
Anstey is the most unusual. He begins like an
actor and ends like a demagogue, and embellishes
his speeches with fantasies of cynicism, awful nasal
infections, and most disrespectful humor. Bar
O Malley, who buries hoary platitudes in gorgeous
vocabulary, none of the others are sufficiently odd
and surprising to call for mention. The remarks
of persons like Cook and Webster are usually de-
scribed as “Interruption,” and there is a horde of
others, like West, Bennett, Fenton and McWilliams
who are merely victims of the indeterminate sen-
tence. Of course, I except old Jim Page’, who is
really in a large class by himself When Jim talks
he recalls a rogue elephant in a field of .daffodils,
and a bon mot from him resembles a crash of dyna-
mite or a falling building.
The Hastings and Macleay electorate has
supplied three Speakers to the N. S. Wales
Assembly—Edmund Barton. J. H. Young
and H. D. Morton.

•‘A Message from Mars” has been most successfully
filmed for picture-play purposes. The coming and
going of the brown man with the gospel of other-
dom’’ are highly supernatural effects on the screen,
and the picture supplies details which are missing
from the stage performance. lor instance, when the
firebell rings in the second act a fire engine dashes
out of the station and tears along to Horace's domi-
cile.
The Williamson Limited combine opens the
S’African branch of its business next New Years
Day. On January 1 it takes possession of Her
Majesty’s, Johannesburg, and the other Wheeler
theatres thereabouts, and thenceforth every show ex-
ported to Australia by the combine will come via the
Cape. Meynell, now in England, is to fix up matters
in S’Afriea before he returns to Melbourne.
From London: They have odd ideas here about the
impossibility of acting American plays as written.
No less a person than Stephen Phillips was engaged
to Anglicise “Everywoman,” and “Within the Law
has been the victim of a foolish attempt at adapta-
tion —foolish because the whole atmosphere is Ameri-
can, and the policemen become caricatures when they
are stated to come from Scotland Yard. Also, Aggie
loses much of her joyousness when her U.S.A. slang
is amputated. The final act is not in the police sta-
tion, but in Mary’s flat. Joe confesses to the murder
from sentimental motives, and not under Third Degree
pressure, and, altogether, the thing is a muddle
which betrays its repudiated parentage at every turn.
“Ike Pagliacci”: Mention Italy to an Australian,
and he thinks (1) of steak and oysters, (2) of organs
and monkeys. The sentimental miss with a know-
ledge of Marion Crawford may get visions of guitars.
As a matter of fact, Italy knows as little of steak-
and-de-oyt;’ as Australia knows of the delights of
macaroni done up Dago fashion. In a recent month
spent among my brother Dagoes, I didn’t see a
single organman. Later I discovered him in great
numbers in Germany, although the monkey was miss-
ing in nine cases out of 10. The musicians are par-
ticularly thick in Bremen. As regards the guitar in
the land of my relations, I fear it has been replaced
for serenading purposes by the gramophone, the
local lover having less faith in his own top note
than in Caruso’s.
“Melb”: Although the Williamson theatre will
start building shortly, there is talk of yet another.
Tallis, Meynell and Ward have a difficulty in placing
their shows here. Allen Doone says he would have
acted much more in Melbourne if he had been able
to get a house. Buckler and Miss Paget, like all
others, object to the Bijou, being entirely upstairs.
Surely the gallery steps at this “shop” are a record.
On the top landing fat women are dismayed by a
wag having written “Half-way Up.” Corson Clarke
and his company are driven to the Majestic picture
place.
Oscar Hammerstein, undeterred by the dropping of
a quarter of a million in giving London a gorgeous
opera house which it didn’t want, is now busy put-
ting up another in New York. He (or his press
agent) says he has discovered a second Caruso
among the workmen on the job. He (or his sti-
pendiary public leg-puller aforesaid) was watching
the plasterers, when one of them started singing a
selection from “II Trovatore.” Plasteruso was
promptly taken from the mortar and Pent to
musical academy. Meanwhile the London (w P a
House that wasn’t wanted is doing pretty well ■ •
variety theatre. There is apparently no end *i. a
variety theatres that London wants.
“Harrison O.”: Barrie’s baronetcy has evidentlv
brought him no luck, since the curtain had to 1
rung down on the first-night performance of his new
play, “Adored One.” This sudden slump is mor
astonishing in view of the fact that a few montl'
ago, when three one-act plays by Shaw, Pinero and
Barrie were put on at the Duke of York's Theatre
Barrie’s “Rosalind” was the only one to plea-
cither critics or public. The most likely
tions of the failure are that the dramatist attempted
to carry his love for quaintness too far, or allowed
his pawky Scawtch humor to become plain fudge
A little of each has happened before; more of both
would quite likely prove fatal. Anyway, though
London audience is prepared to stand "a lot from
the creator of “Peter Pan,” its patience gave out
half-way through “Adored One.” And it shroke at
the gifted author with the shriek of mockery.
“Lancet”: For the London Medical Congress this
year 80 American doctors came by the Bremen from
New York. On the third night of the voyage a
grand masquerade ball was interrupted by Dr
lvovacs, of Brooklyn, announcing that one of his
professional brethren had developed acute appendi-
citis. As an immediate operation was necessary, the
Pall must be stopped, perfect quiet being essential
Great excitement. The patient was carried into the
dining-saloon for the operation, and hundreds of
passengers assembled as closely as they were allowed
Many distinguished medical men stood around. Tin-
operation was performed by Dr. Seaman, of Phila
delphia, and Dr. Albee, of Vermont. They were
dressed in the usual long white gowns and india-
rubber gloves. Instruments were arranged and dipped
in antiseptic solution. Chloroform was apparent.lv
administered. Dr. Seaman quickly made the inci-
sion. Out came a large bunch of grapes. Next, a
pineapple. The patient then sat up, and called for
a glass of beer.
Circus panics, proceeding from the misconduct of
caged animals, are more wildly unreasonable than
the theatre panics due to somebody’s cry of “Fire!'’
At Ghent, last week, a party of performing bears
started to maul their “tamer ” and a young woman
who had entered the cage with him. Whereupon
the audience rushed for the exits, as though the
bears were looking for meat outside the cage. Their
quarrel was with the tamer and the woman who had
ventured into their family circle. It was a purely
domestic outbreak.
The varied life-story of the Little King William-
street house known till now as Adelaide Tivoli: Built
as White’s Rooms —-White was a tailor with a musical
son—-nearly 60 years ago, it was the city’s chief
ball-room, concert-hall and banqueting-room. Ex-
plorer McDouall Stuart, among others, was “break-
fasted” there, after the custom of those days. It
then became Garner’s Rooms, under Arthur Garner,
at one time of the Williamson and Musgrove firm,
and, being a solidly-built place, it stood even his
weight. Then it was the Bijou, under Tommy Hud-
son. All this century it has been the Tivoli —with
a dress-circle, etc. —under Rickards. Now that
Mclntosh has taken that name to a far bigger
house, it becomes the Star, with a continuous-picture
show. Naturallv the old proprietary did not care
to have a variety show running in the old house.
but it is understood that, when the present lease
runs out, two years from now, the Brennan-Fuller
circuit has the right to take it.
