Business, Bobbery, etc.
Kaylee : Commend me to a Rockhampton (Q.)
carnival crowd for meanness. Jefferson Tait, with
a travelling melodrama show, opened recently on a
threatening night, and, in terror that the tent would
blow down and smother the hero and heroine, choke
the villain and asphyxiate the cheeild, to say no-
thing of blotting out the audience, he paid back the
money. But much audience climbed from the bob seats
into the two bobs, and from the good seats into
the very best seats, and collected dividends at the
door so unblushingly that, when Tait had paid out
all the cash he had taken, there was still £7 worth
of people clamoring inside. So he paid out the
from past profits—after which the weather
naturally change ( d for the better, and the only cloud
in sight was the one on Jefferson Tait’s brow.
* * * *
“C.E.”: The Melbourne Electric Supply Co. deals
with the suburbs, the Corporation Supply with the
city. The co. has lately ordered, from the Allge-
meine Elektricitats, of Berlin, two additional sets
of machinery, each of 5000 kilowatts capacity. The
present machines can supply 9000 kilowatts. Thus
the output can be doubled. The new machines will
be the largest at present in Australia, except those in
Sydney; but the electrification of Melbourne railways
will demand larger than any. The M.E.S. Co.’s new
machines will run into £30,000. Lane, the secre-
tary, first tried to get the job done by English
makers, but found that, under the soporific influence
of Freetrade, they couldn’t do it in the time fixed.
So he went to up-to-date, Protectionist Germany,
consulted the two great rivals, Siemens and the
Elektricitats, and placed an order at a Drice so much
lower that the difference will more than compensate
for the extra duty to be paid. The contract time is
seven months.
“Kidman’s Man’: San Francisco advices show
maintained interest in Australian meat. The largest
importer, Booth, of Monterey, declares that, as soon
as the duty of cents the lb. is removed, he will
swamp the U.S. with flesh from Sydney. It was
confidently expected that the duty would be off in
September, and that large markets would then imme-
diately open even in Chicago and New York.
Booth’s manager, Marshall, says on organisation is
perfected, with a chain of distributive centres, from
Pacific to Atlantic. Already he imports five times
as much as all other concerns combined. The San
Francisco community was clamorous for the meat, but
the importer had run against the jobbers. This led
Booth to strike out independently through the Retail
Butchers’ Associations; one of which has about 400
members in San Francisco, while another, with head-
headquarters at Oakland, has 225. Thus early he
has wrought up from a small sample shipment to
steady and continuous consignments of 200 tons per
steamer, and the next one was taking 300 tons. The
fight has been hard, with the opposition trying to
foist off as Australian the carcases of old cows
stags and “dogs,” as the old, used-up cattle are
termed. The Panama Canal will, however, be rather
unfavorable, since it will open up handier communi-
cation between Argentina and San Francisco. As
yet this competition does not trouble us, as it does
not pay to send meat over the Andes from Buenos
Ayres to Chili, for shipment to San Francisco, while
the voyage round the Horn is out of the question.
Nevertheless, the Australian meat future is boundless >
Apart from America, there is the incalculable outlook
m China, Japan, Java and the Archipelago, Malaya
and even India. All those millions will go on meat-
eating more and more. They always do. Meat,
however unhealthy, is in tropical regions a standard
of Respectability. So is wheat as against rice Our
vast Northern Territory—what millions isn’t it going
to be worth before many years are gone, if we don’t
lose it by stagnation and State Frights'
* * * *
“F.H.R.”: Under present conditions in N S
Wales a person can purchase a heap of tailings on
any old goldfield, subject it to cyanide treatment,
and waltz away with the proceeds, leaving behind
him as deadly a poison as can be found in any dis-
pensary. To children, who are naturally fond of
romping in the sands, the dust is, to put it mildly
dangerous; and to the roving cow and her brother
the salts that arise in the form of a white coating
are fatal if licked after rain. The least the Mines
Department should do is to insist on a fly-proof fence
around every such abomination.
* * * *
“Bloore”: By the beard of Admiral Patey, I am
gneviously stricken! I was looking for something
to amuse “Bloore,” junior, the other day, and spotted
the Australian fleet, neatly made up in a box, all
for 15 pence. Before spending my tobacco" monev,
however, I had a look at the cover of the box, arid
hang me if it didn’t bear this inscription— “ Made
in Japan” !
* * * *
“Feng-Shui” (from a big Chinese city): The
convenience of cheap coolie labor is the most expen-
sive inconvenience in the world. My coolies have
been digging a trench. One down below slowly and
painfully thrusts a sort of hoe into the soil, and
withdraws it. A friend carefully and slowly scrapes
off the small pieces of clay. He passes it to a
companion standing by, who, with the deliberation
of a sculptor, moulds it into a ball. This third
man, with anguished effort, delivers it into the
custody of a colleague standing above, who in time
transfers it to a fifth. This last, as if it were
precious porcelain, lays it reverently on the ground
The five require a kangani to watch over them or
they will not work at all. Every now and then’the
chain is broken by No. 2, who goes off for a chew
of betelnut, or by No. 4, who is merely bored, and
the entire process stops. Six able-bodied Asiatics =.*
a very sick or a (worst adjective you know) lazy
Australian with a trowel. And wandering around is
a mad Australian who has flogged himself to try to
get the “cheap” laborers to change their ways and
can t manage it. I’m the mad Australian. If I met
one of those people who want to “develop” our
North with this kind of loafer, I think I should kill
him.
JANE, AS A BUSINESS PROPOSITION.
[For The Bulletin.]
A pleasant girl is nature’s choicest rose,
All poets dwell upon this modest fact;
The tender petals ’neath the damsel’s nose
Bloom in a manner which, to be exact,
Though often nutty, fragrant, red or fruity,
Require new terms to classify their beauty.
I cannot see why language old and trite
Should be employed when cataloguing Jane;
Her lips are red, her hair is black as night,
But such description fills my soul with pain.
Roses and night and stars I here abolish —
Her lips are radishes, her hair’s stove polish.
Her eyes are like two spots of round, kind ink
Dropt on white china, and all fringed about
With down of young black Orpingtons that blink
Against the incubator lamp and doubt
Its mother brightness. Her white forehead arching
Is like a broad, white collar curved with starching.
Her ankles twinkle when she moves around
Like necks of pleasant bottles upside down,
Her waist, with webs of silk or satin bound,
Curves like the tramway in some street in town,
Where, two main arteries of traffic merging,
The cars are towards the city’s heart converging.
Her smile is something very sweet and fine,
Like some clear lamp just lighted in the night
When that the happy hour has come to dine,
And all the table cutlery is bright;
Her heart, when sentiments of joy dilate her,
Glows like a warm electric radiator.
Such is the simple catalogue of Jane,
In business terms that folk may understand;
No waste of verbiage here, but crisp and plain
The language love has ready at command.
Could roses, stars or moons make it completer
To help you recognise her when you meet her?

this, WITHOUT EXPENSE, is
to write to me describing your invention, enclosing
k small sketch where necessary. Nicety of drawing
is not essential; all that is necessary "at first is to

for further procedure.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them —•
the strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What
a relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly, understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural

BRISBANE.
IT WORKED WONDERS.
“While ploughing I wash seized with influenza,”
writes Mr. M. J. McAvady, Ohura, N.Z. “I went
to bed and was so ill next day that I called in the
doctor. He prescribed for me, but his medicine did
no good. A friend recommended me to try Cham-
berlain’s Cough Remedy, and I did. It worked
wonders, and I was able to resume my ploughing in
a few days.”*
Government Notices.
KALGOORLIE TO PORT AUGUSTA
RAILWAY.
Applications for Position of Supervising
Engineer at Kalgoorlie.
APPLICATIONS are invited from persons
competent to fill the position of Super-
vising Engineer at the Kalgoorlie end of
the Kalgoorlie to Port Augusta Railway.
Applicants must have a thorough know-
ledge of Railway Construction in all its
branches, and be capable of handling large
bodies of men. The successful applicant
will be immediately responsible to the
Engineer-in-Chief for Commonwealth Rail-
ways. The salary will be at the rate of
xi 50 per annum.
Applications, accompanied by copies of
testimonials, and addressed to the Secre-
tary, Department of Home Affairs, will be
received until noon on MONDAY, 6th
OCTOBER. 1913.
JOSEPH COOK,
Minister of State for Home Affairs.

IN
BANANA LAND
About Railway Matters.
This is the tale of “Don Q.”: —
The Queensland Railway Deiiartment has accepted
delivery of one of the McKeen auto-carriages, and
it is now running upon a South Brisbane suburban
branch. So it seems as though the State is to be
saddled with the five white elephants, after all.
As stated in The Bulletin (28/8/’13), the Minis-
ter for Railways announced in the Assembly that
the autocars would not be accepted, the objection
being that they exceeded the “axle load’’ by I6cwt.
Axle-load, in railway lingo, indicates the load that
each axle bears, and as these carriages have four
axles, the total overweight would be 3 tons 4cwt.
per car.
Commissioner Evans • might be asked to explain
how this matter of over-weight was rectified. Evans
is very patient, and very kind indeed to the foreign
contractor.
At one time we were told in the press that the
whole deal was off, and that the Department planned
to build its own autocars (with steam motors) in
its Ipswich workshops. That was the only glimpse
of sanity displayed throughout the whole transac-
tion. Men here threw up their hats with glee when
they read the news, for it meant about £25,000 of
work for Australians instead of foreigners. But
apparently the co. talked Evans over. It would be
interesting reading to have tabled in Parliament a
copy of the McKeen contract, and the whole of the
subsequent correspondence.
That these vehicles will be very costly seems to
be the belief of local railway men. Evans nearly
dropped when he was told the amount of the fuel
bill for the Toowoomba trip. Petrol at Is. 9d. per
gallon may be all right for a light moto. - , but when
you have a 30-ton monster scoffing it up in bucket-
fuls, Barnes’ little surplus of £6000 will soon go
up in vapor.
These American cars are supposed to be 200-hp.
Granted that the h.p. of all petrol engines is purely
nominal and largely imaginable, there seems to be
something unduly wrong about the compilation.
Take the Brisbane to Laidley test run of the car
with a 15-ton trailer behind. It failed by 20min.
to keep mail-train time. Now, our Australian-made
B17 mail engines develop 700-h.p., and handle on
this run a load of 200 tons (it takes 3\-h.p. steam
to handle one ton of load on this route), but the
Yankee alleged 200-h.p. couldn’t handle 15 tons of
load, which means {motor) to each ton
load, and failure at that.
Mechanics in the Ipswich workshops tell me
that the imported cars do not develop anything
near 200-h.p., but Evans has the importing craze
badly, despite the fact that moulders have been
fired from the works and others are under notice
to quit.
These imported cars seat only 70 passengers, and
cost with duty £6374. Our biggest mail train
engines cost us £4250, develop 700-hp., and handle
200-ton loads up the Toowoomba Ranges. The
wretched auto-toys, with their alleged 200-h.p.,
apparently can’t satisfactorily manage a paltry 15-
ton load.
They have their own way of doing things
up North. A young man, found wandering
in a bush-woman’s bedroom while her hus-
band was away, told the Court that he really
didn’t remember anything about it, as he
was soaked in bush-rum at the time. “Then
go down on your knees to this lady and
apologise, or go up for three months.” com-
manded the bush Solomon. After a moment
spent in weighing the relative disadvantages
of going down and up, the rum-soaker de-
cided, and down he went.
The Denham liquor legislation continues
to break its peck. One section says that no
female may b6'served with drink in any bar
or room set apart for drinking purposes.
The other day a Croydon publican .served a
woman in a passage leading through his
hotel, and was summoned for a breach of
the Act. And the Act came the cropper to
which it is getting quite accustomed when
the case was dismissed on the ground that
the passage was not a room within the
meaning of the statute.
“Rangan” : Bush fires were once the daily
terror and often the total ruination of west-
ern landholders. Now they are catalogued
simply as a nuisance. The reason is the
general use of the fire plough, which is now
as common in the West as harvesters in the
THE RASH ACT.
GREGG: “ Here , here , don't you know that's a
dashed dangerous way to commit suicide?”
Riverina. Various runs are now so literally
honeycombed with fire-plough tracks that
when an owner sees the black smoke-cloud,
instead of going baresark, he calmly lights
his pipe and sends a messenger to see which
block has been burnt.
Queensland had many more smiles for
Governor Maegregor at his recent “silver
jubilee” than it had; when he began his vice-
regalling 25 years ago. The North’s own
John Douglas had been appointed High
Commissioner and Administrator of Papua,
and was performing the duties with tact
and ability. It was assumed that the Brit-
ish Government had taken him into
the diplomatic service, and that his future
would consist of a round of Governorships
and other high official appointments. But
Maegregor arrived to supersede him without
apology or explanation, and John was again
a simple Queensland politician. It ap-
peared later that the word “special” in the
terms of his appointment—he was “Special
High Commissioner”—excluded him, accord-
ing to diplomatic usage, from having any
claim to future Imperial emplovment.
Queensland, however, knew little and cared
less about diplomatic regulations, and as it
liked John it sulked considerably and threw
scowls at the Scotchman who had arrived at
Port Moresby to take his place.
The Commissioner for Railways hints in
his annual report at coming increases in
fares and freights, to make good the in-
creased costs. The interest bills begin to
come in, and generally things in Queensland
are not as brisk as they were. Hence the
time is coming to repeat the fatuous old
policy of making the people pay up in times
of slump for what they were excused from
paying in times of affluence.
“Petramos”: Queensland lately got an
immigrant who at one time was a good deal
talked about. His name is Carl Prinz.
With the exception of Jonah few men who
have caused animals any discomfort have
had a rougher experience. He had an act
with 17 lions and Mrs. Lions. At Ports-
mouth (Eng.) one of the family ate portion
of its trainer in full view of the audience.
When the part of Prinz that was not eaten
came out of hospital it found that the lions
had contracted a sickness (paralysis, maybe
—there is nothing worse than 17 paralysed
lions i which had put the whole menagerie
out of the game for good. Prinz philo-
sophically went to work as a stable-hand
until he had prepared some monkeys for a
music-hall debut. Now he has acquired a
farm and will settle down to train pigs to
become good sausages.
That black songster,
the crow, is to have a
price put on his head,
and if the Govern-
ment won’t stand the
cost the local authori-
ties say they must.
The offence of which
he has been found
guilty is spreading the
prickly pear, and that
is a capital crime in
Queensland nowadays.
Unfortunately, nowa-
days is just 50 years
too late. Still, it is
better than no-time.
Jim Kenniff, sen-
tenced to death in
1902, along with his
brother Patrick, for
the murder of Dahlke,
a station-manager, and
George Doyle, a
policeman, is due for
release in November,
1914. Patrick was
hanged, but Jim’s sen-
tence was commuted
to “the term of his
natural life,” and then
—on account of the
crowning of George
V.—to mere “life,”
which, with remissions
for good conduct,
means 12 years. The
conviction of the Ken-
niffs raised a storm of
r >rotest at the time.
Evidence as to the
actual murder was
only circumstantial,
and the most vital
part of it —the part
that hanged Patrick
by the neck until he
was dead, was v'.en
by an aboriginal.
About the most out-
spoken and strenuous
of those who consid-
ered the Kenniffs’
guilt had not been
nroved was Judge
Real.
The foolish habit of whirling on roller
skates for scores of hours has caught hold of
Central Queensland. A while back, one
William Black, of Mount Morgan, snatched
the laurels from William Eckard, by spin-
ning round the same monotonous circle for
80hr. lOmin. But somebody cast a sprinkle
of doubt on the performance, and while this
was being argued, one Aeilson, of Charters
Towers, whizzed for 80hr. 30min. Where-
upon Black decided to carve his name at a
dazzling height, and did the monotonous thing
for lOOhr. Meantime, as likely as not,
Black’s missus was home chopping wood, or
Eckard’s old man was battling single-handed
against a maddening invasion of prickly
pear. In a country where there is so much
wood to chop and so much prickly pear to
fight the Blacks and the Eckhards ought to
be given the frozen eye, and the Neilsons
should be taken by the ear and their noses
turned to the grindstone.
The Denham Government has again
shown what a good Australian it is by plac-
ing an order for the Bremer bridge in Ger-
many. Its explanation is that the foreigner’s
tender was 40 per cent below the local
nrice. How much better off Queensland will
be through sending £6O out of the countrv
instead of spending £lOO in it Denham
hasn’t so far explained.
Whatever Shall We Do?
[For The Bulletin.]
Queensland Assembly has now four political par-
ties. We predict trouble from this abandoning of
the sound old two-party system.— Brisbane daily.
Whatever shall we do
With parties more than two?
How sweetly did existence run
When rival clans were blent in one !
When every man would meekly wait,
Without a notion in his pate,
To do ot once what he was told
When Bills were pushed or place was sold,
And ne’er a one was ever known
To broach opinions of his own,
But promptly ran the floor across
To duck and vote and please the Boss.
With parties more than two
Whatever shall we do?
Whatever shall we do
With parties more than two?
’Twas Lib., or Lab. a while ago,
With each his little row to hoe ;
And, though they differed in their ways.
And each one had his little craze,
Yet on one point they both agreed:
“There is no room for any creed
Or color here but Ours and Us,
So damn this Independent Fuss !
Would you upset this lovely rout
Of parties two, with turn about?
And give the man of honest mind
A chance to leave the ranks behind,
And drop the canting party cries,
And tell the truth that in him lies? —-•
A thing he couldn’t do
With parties only two!”
Whatever shall we do
With parties more than two?
D’ye mean to say you’d let a man
Abjure the precious party plan?
Of sitting dumb and voting blind,
And whistling conscience down the wind,
And start a system wild instead
Where men could vote with Blue or Red,
Regardless if the party fall
So long as Right and Country call?
Why, such a game of fast and loose
Might simply play the very deuce
With Us who run the party race,
And spoil our hold on Perks and Place! —-
’Tis plain ’twould never do
With parties more than two!
QUEENSLAND.
F. B.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September 20 191 a
My Dear Thargomindah,—

had a special tent of their own. but I dS
know whether there was a special brand of
refreshment mside it. On the same day a
the Exhibition, the young blood of th,
Gregory Terrace Christian Brothers’
sported before an equally large body °o°
female relative. y 01
Thousands of people stood ankle deep in
mud what time their hats were total £
ruined by watery raindrops, and their in
adequate umbrellas trembled in the thunder
and waited for Wizard Stone and one Jones
to aviate above the racecourse enclosure
this afternoon. When the bedraggled mul-
titude was quite sure that there would he
no aviation, and also that it (the multi-
tude) had caught a severe chill, it departed
with all its legs moving at once. Arriving
at the city, it found another damp stream
of equally disconsolate people returning
from the Exhibition, where there had been
no repeat performance of the Carnival of
Nations, in aid of the hospitals. The un-
fortunate children were dressed for their
parts, refreshments and the band were on
tj l6 ground, and all arrangements were con-
cluded except the arrangement to hold off
the rain.
On Tuesday night the Orvieto disguised
'tself in some dozens of flags, a whole cargo
ot Chinese lanterns, a ton of palms, a few
extras in the shape of a fountain (suspected
of being another version of the ship’s hose)
a rockery (with a strong resemblance to the
geological specimens used on the nearbv
breakwater), and attracted to its decks sev-
eral hundreds of Brisbane’s social moths
All this because for the first time it had got
past Pinkenba and burst up the river as
far as the New Farm wharf. Girl moths
in pretty dresses floated on the boat deck,
and chaperon moths and married moths and
other species who were unable to obtain
flitting partners folded their wings on the
promenade deck. The Brisbane concert
band made music, and the cook composed a
supper in seven cantos and about 400
stanzas.
Same night the defenders of the country
hereabouts gave a party at the United Ser-
vices Institution. His Ex. and Lady Mac-
gregor looked in for a little while, and were
received by Major Boyd and Captain Had-
dock. Colonel Lee wasn’t there. Ditto Dr.
Hopkins, who sports a Lt.-Colonelcy in some
regiment or other, and who is usually a
faithful ally to these shivoos. He has been
to England in search of health, and from
all reports seems to have found it. But as
he will not be back for about a fortnight he
was unable to attend the party except by
wireless telepathy.
Dr. Elkington, the popular medico who
presides over the Health Department here,
is to be transferred to the Federal service.
It is unfortunate for this State, where he
has been untiring in his work. Recently
he went on a tour of the islands in Torres
Strait, and vaccinated all the natives he
could lay hands on in order to provide a
barrier for the mainland, should Asiatic
small-pox ever be inclined to make*' south.
The Queensland quarantine station, too, has
been made very up to date under his rule,
If the Federal Department hasn’t got a bar-
gain I’ll —I’ll take to wearing elastic-sided
boots. No woman can lay longer odds than
that.
The McCormack drifted in this week, with
one of the Taits and pilot McSweeney in
charge. John found a packed house wait-
ing for him on the first night, and at each
concert since the packing has had to be
done by expert stevedores. Not content
with mulcting the public by means of his
voice, the McCormack went to the races this
afternoon, and won largely. A horse with
an emerald green name, another named Don
Jose, and a third sculled by a saddle-mariner
named Maeterlinck, all assisted John to get
right with the landlord. On Monday the
last concert will be given, and on the same
night Lewis Waller will commence to break
the Butterfly on the Wheel.
There have only been two hops this week,
and thev were quite tame affairs. One was
a twirl given by the Ladies’ Rowing Club,
in the Commercial Club’s shed, and the
other, which happened to-night, was the
distraction of the Young Wimmin’s Liberal
Club. E. Id. Macartney was the only mem-
ber of Parliament to hand in his presence
and patronage.
The split skirt, which is causing so much
embarrassment to our Cousin Sam, is mak-
ing a few attempts to undermine our peace
of mind. Block trotters were cheered a few
mornings ago to see a young but austere
policeman standing by a damsel with pink
cheeks and trembling fingers, who was pin-
ning up a most split skirt with safety pins.
No one knows if the split was the result of
an accident, or of too much perusal of
musical comedy fashions. And whether the
pinning was a command from the Law, or
whether the Law was merely present as a
sympathetic onlooker, are matters likewise
unknown.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

lnquirer: The problem you set is toe
vast to be handed efficiently in any cor-
respondence column. Each University has about
half a dozen different varieties of robe. An Oxford
D.C.L. wears one garment like a pink Inverness
cape, a white tie such as you may see attached to
the windpipe of any local barrister, and a species of
bashed bowler hat. An M.A. wears a black Inver-
ness with dashes of red about the cape (or hood)
part of it, and a mortar-board head covering. A
Cambridge LL.D. painfully suggests, as regards his
appearance, the D.C.L. of the sister University. The
local Universities have faithfully followed the ex-
ample of the British ones in the matter of cut,
garishness, etc., so far as their garments of dis-
tinction are concerned. ...Syd. C.: Commonplace

Changed the fantastic ball

Wetton: According to the final results of the census
of the Maori population, taken during March, 1911,
and forwarded to the Native Minister, in February,
1912, there had been an increase in five years of
2113 persons. The first census was taken in 1874,
when the Maoris totalled 45,470; in 1878, 43,595;
in 1881, 44,097; in 1886, 41,969; in 1891, 41,993;
in 1896, 39,854 ; in 1901, 43,143 ; in 1906, 47,731 ;
in 1911, 49,844. The Under-Secretary in his memor-
andum to the Minister said that “the increase shown
by the present (1911) census may be taken as a
fair index of the position.”

“G.R.N.”: The reference (B. 11/9/T3)
to Governor Bourke’s Church Act of the
’3o’s, fixing the screws of the old-time Aus-
tralian parsons, brings to mind another epoch
in church history in N.S.W. which goes to
show the resourcefulness of the aforesaid
pilots. In 1863 the calls made by the Church
were so urgent ,and many that they threat-
ened to embarrass the State finances, so
State aid had to be dropped. Bishop Barker
at once called a council, and it was decided
to thereafter pass round the plate in church.
The Bishop issued a circular to all the par-
ishes suggesting this method of raising the
funds, but did not insist on it. The late
Dean Cowper has left it on record that the
parishioners of St. Philip’s raised strong
objections, the chief being that it would make
the service so much longer. The Dean says :
“I undertook to shorten my part of the
service, and the Latin proverb solvitur am-
buJando, which is applicable to many other
anparent difficulties, received illustration.
The trial was made, the difficulties van-
ished.” Prior to this no plate went round.
Those were the “good old days.”

Sydney
The Inky Way.
The linotype again! A London paper has just
apologised for saying that at a certain co-operative
society s picnic “25,000 rums and coffees were dis-
tributed. ’ It added: “Obviously ‘rums’ should have
been buns,’ and we deeply regret the unfortunate
misprint Now if that paper had told the whole
S °l td truth, it would probably have called it “the
maddening misprint.’’ Suppose an editor happens
to state, in the “Personal” column that the lost
soul of Smith got a jug of cold water last Thursday,
when it didn’t! Or that it was given a block of ice
to put on its tail, when it wasn’t! Is that a libel!
It hardly seems like one, though it must certainly
be an aggravation if Smith (deceased) gets a chance
to read the paper before it blazes up. And surely it
isn’t up to any paper to apologise because it said in
error that a large number of people got something
warm and comforting for the stomach instead of the
rock-bound and imperishable bun.
“Penman”: While the proposed Evening Mirror
must be set up in opposition to Melb. Herald if the
business is handled with ability, what a gruesome
collection there is in the graveyard! Among
dailies the defunct ones include an Echo, Evening
News (2), Mail, Standard, and World. And of others
the Ant, Arena, Bulletin, Champion, Criterion,
Dispatch (killed by the concealed viciosity of a
contributed poem, “To Agnes,” with awful letters
beginning the lines), Eagle, Daily News (very old,
bought by the Argus), Farmers’ Journal (from the
Age office), Examiner, Daily Gazette (very old),
Humbug (Marcus Clarke’s), Federal Australian,
Irishman, Illustrated News, a former Life, Mag-
pie, Patriot, a former Sporting and Dramatic,
Sketcher, Sportsman, Telegraph, Town and
Country, Touchstone, Town Talk, Weekly Argus
and Age, Victorian, Yeoman. But so far as the
defunct dailies are concerned, reflect on the growth
of Melbourne. There is now more space for two
than there was available for one when most of the
previous attempts were made.
W. N. Willis, one-time of Sydney, is discredited
with another book by the Athen/Kum (London).
The title is “Why Girls Go Wrong,” and the
Athenaeum gives the contents short, not to say
hangman’s, shrift. “We doubt whether this badlv-
written book, with its gaudily sensational cover, will
do any good. Some of the author’s remarks are
amazing, if not fatuous.”
■* * * *
The death of John Miller Sweet removes a well-
known N. S. Wales journalist. He was connected, as
owner, for many years with the Newcastle Herald,
and afterwards acted for a long period as Sydney
representative of that journal, which he founded
after serving his apprenticeship with the Maitland
Mercury. His widow was a daughter of James
Fletcher, so long M.L.A. for Newcastle.
“Marloo”: The morning paper had arrived at the
Dago oyster shop. Andru, the boss, who is unable
to read da Englese, stepped to the front door and
ejaculated, “You read da war news, Snaky!” In
answer, a lean, hungry-looking youth entered, very
willingly, for the raw wind had bitten into him.
Snaky unfolded the sheets and slowly droned out
the required information. Andru was greatly pleased.
“By Gar, Snaky, dat da boshta news,” he said. “Reada
agen.” Glancing at the column, Snaky delighted
Andru’s ears with this: “Eleven hundred Bulgars
surprised 700 Greeks near Adrianople. The Greeks
fought bravely, killing 600 Bulgars. The remainder
fled. The Greek losses were slight.” Andru’s eyes
sparkled, while he jumped round with excitement
“By jinga, Snaky, you read da gra-and news. I ver
pie-eased. I shout you da feed. What you like?”
Snakv made it stewed oysters. When I
was " handing over my dues at the counter
Andru pointed to the place where Snaky had found
“the gra-and news.” He requested me to read it
again. I was about to oblige, but, after a look at the
paragraph, remarked that I had forgotten my glasses.
Snaky was a fraud. He had basely concocted “da
boshta news,” and the item Andru had pointed to was
a prattling paragraph about the Hague Conference.
* * * *
“Gouger”: Have you ever had the mortification,
brainy brothers of the casual pen, to behold the
stolen product of your very soul in the columns ot
some sneaking piratical paper? It is not the un-
authorised printing that hurts, brothers; it is the
loss of beer and cheese. A North Queensland weekly
of some tonnage is a confirmed paragraph thief.
Again and again had it lifted the fruits of my
own genius, and I had borne it; but at last I
wrote and said that, as the paper honored itself
bv printing matter of mine which a Red Rag had
already paid for, it might perhaps like some for its
very own. And then I made the fatal error of
sordidly mentioning money The answer was silence
deep as the caverns of death, persistent as the mur-
mur of the sea. Truly the road of literary genius is
beset bv flat beer and sharp burglars, and the vul-
tures, waiting by the ink-smeared track, pick the
hapless brains of genius without giving a bob in re-
turn ! # * *
“Patrick Melon” : W T e had had a bad week in the
office of the Kangaroo Advocate. The reporter had
been paid last publishing day, and hadn t been pre-
sentable since. The boy had pied a colurnn. The
comp was sulky. I had the influenza. We were
short of copy. There hadn’t been even a police-
court case, and we were all hunting round for news
At the last moment the long-legged reporter rushed
in with “The Mayor’s missus has got twins! Run
off a par,” I said. He did, and did it well, finishing
off bv congratulating Alderman and Mrs. Fitzmug-
e-ins on the new arrivals. There was no time for
proofs “Read it in the stick,” I told the comp
Biit iust then the Mayor came in and took the staff
out to drink the kids’ healths, which we did—twice
for each of them. 1 believe the par. wm afterwards
read in the stick. Anyway, at nine o clock next
morning the Mayor rushed into the office without
Ws hat or coat. Holding up the paper he de-
manded in blazing language what we meant by con-
gratulating him on the new animals.
IN LONDON.
[For The Bulletin.]
There’s rain upon the murky streets and black clouds
in the sky,
Where busy crowds of London folk go hurrying
ceaseless by ;
I know there is not any face my eyes may chappe to
see,
lo send an answering glance to mine, or think one
thought of me.
There winds the traffic lumbering on beneath the
great St. Paul’s,
While o er my heart the London smoke with sickening
odor falls;
And thoughts of Sydney blind my eyes, and Sydney’s
wattle days,
W hile London folk go silent by upon their London
ways.
I see a city fair, and far, far south beside the sea,
Whose tepid waters lap the stones beside the busy
quay,
Where flocks of gulls lean out to catch the sunlight
o’er the bay,
Dear southern sun and Sydney town, twelve thousand
miles away.
I have a dream of harbor boats, like fairy craft they
glide,
Slow-moving toward the sheltered cove or out upon
the tide—
The red sun casting radiant glow upon the city dome,
And faint upon the western sky the dear hills of
my home.
there s rain upon the murky streets and black clouds
in the sky,
And endless crowds of London folk for ever hurrying
by;
I turn my face toward the South, away from
England’s May,
Por all my heart’s in Sydney town, twelve thousand
miles away.
M. E.

Father’s Birthday.
[For The Bulletin.]
empty suburban street was drows-
I ing happily in the morning sunlight
when a small girl in a state of fran-
tic excitement burst from the hall of a semi-
detached villa and rushed to the kerb. Ar-
rived there, she commenced to leap up and
down and clap an accompaniment, to a shrill
chant of her own : “My—father's—a—
scoundrel!” she screamed, over and over
ggain, with the greatest enthusiasm, despite
the fact that she had no visible audience.
Presently the deeper voice of an invisible
small boy disorganised her solo with a mock-
ing imitation in different words. “Mother's
—cu— lying — old — haggige /” howled the
enemy. Whereupon, Father’s rebellious
daughter gave a shrill scream of rage, aban-
doned her performance, and fled inside, pre-
sumably to assail Mother’s detractor. As
she disappeared an iron pot crashed through
the front window' and landed upside down
on the pocket edition lawn. Then the front
door slammed to at the push of an unseen
hand, aud the single-fronted villa resumed
its air of bricky reticence. The upturned
pot and the broken window alone remained
qs evidences of inward strife,
i Alice, the maid at Mrs. Tribbens’ board-
ing-house opposite, having observed the
alarms and excursions from the dining-room
window’, w r ent heavily upstairs in an indig-
nant frame of mind. Meeting Miss Gummer
on the landing, she let off a series of short,
passionate sentences: “Them Strabswoods ’as
been at it again. Mis’ Gummer. Figbtiu’
spmethink ’orrible —throwin’ pots through th’
front winder, an’ callin’ each other such
names! I know beeos them kids ’as been
yellin’ them iu th' street. Them kids makes
s'peshul ’ditions of themselves w'hen any-
thing goin’ w'roug. It’s their father’s birth-
day ter-morrer, an’ they was goin’ ter make
no end of a fuss. Now they’re fightin’, if
yer please ! Th’ p’leece orter stop it, I say !
Makes th’ street look low!” Without wait-
ing for an answer she continued morosely
on her way upstairs to spread the disgrace-
ful tidings and thoroughly shock the wliqle
house. That house had for one brief and
memorable period been the home of the pot-
throwers opposite.
The Tribbens household, which collectively
regarded the Strabswoods’ presence in the
street as an insult, received the news
eagerly, and, immediately after tea, manned
the side balcony and verandah in order to
fake observations of the enemy’s lines.
The Strabswoods, according to their usual
custom, must have effected a grand recon-
ciliation shortly after the unfortunate pot
episode, for that missile had vanished from
the lawn, and the house W’as decorously still.
But the smashed window was not so easily
effaced. Shortly after the Tribbens boarders
had paraded, the front door of the Strabs-
woods mansion was thrown open, and the
family of four, all dressed in their best,
emerged laden with toy flags, four Chinese
lanterns and a quantity of string.
Strabswood and his wife—a couple of the
“superior” type—glanced across at the
crow’ded house-front, made some withering
but inaudible remark, and then set to work
in the most affectionate manner to decorate
the one-eyed cottage, assisted by the children.
The first thing Strabswood did w r as to tack
a small cotton Union Jack on the frame of
the damaged window, thus demonstrating in
one hammering his loyalty to King and
Throne, and also his willingness to let by-
gone pots be bygones. Then the flags and
lanterns were festooned along the verandah,
and the family clung together 1 the pot-
dinted lawn and rapturously applauded its
own work. The children were now seething
with excitement —the small boy especially
being close up to bursting point. At last he
could contain his emotions no longer, but
dashed out the gate and buck-jumped down
the street till he was opposite the pyramid
of silent, staring lodgers.
“Father’s going to give me ten shillings to-
morrow !” he screamed. “He says I can
spend it just as I like, but I’m not to ask
any vulgar Australian boys to share it! He’s
going to make a speech too, my father is!
You see if he doesn’t!” Then the .delirious
courier rushed back, to be playfully rebuked
for his exuberance. Until he was ordered to
join the family inside, the younger Strabs-
wood continued to leap, and run, and cheer.
As a preliminary exhibition of gratitude for
ten shillings and a speech, it represented a
rare an unt of cheaply-purchased energy.
The Tri jens household resolved not to miss
the fun ,ion on any account.
Next evening, at seven o’clock, the house
of Strai swood, with its five lighted lanterns
glowing in a dancing line, presented a mod-
erately gorgeous spectacle. The son and heir,
in an Eton suit and broad white collar, was
evidently under orders not to leave the pre-
cincts of the house. He k£pt jumping on
and off the gate like a restless monkey, but
never once ventured outside. An eager knot
°f 15 boys hovered before the gate, as if
awaiting some promised boon. Presently
the Strabswood boy leaped on the gate and
roade frantic signs. The mob broke out into
shrill whistles and cheers, and only desisted
when Strabswood himself, in full evening
dress,, stood bowing and nervously twisting
U P his clipped moustache on the narrow
verandah. 11 is high, pallid forehead shone
m the superfluous lantern glow, and the sup-
posed diamond in his shirt-front sparkled
feebly. He looked at his immature audience
m a fatuously serious way and announced
with fervor, “First of all. char boys, we will
sin " ‘God Save the King’!”
At these solemn words, tlie piano within
became emphatically loyal, and the Anthem
shriek* mu m w . lld brays aud straggling
and thpn i cbeers naturally followed,

Young Strabswood gleefully received the
veasure before the greedy and expectant
ZL°V h i cheerers: but he immediately

tainment by wheeling round and excitedly
snouting, “I m not going to spend it! I’m

This splendid impromptu was received by
the Home Government with applause, anil
by the (Jutlanders with stunned silence.
u . . last a small boy in front spoke out:
Ain t you goin’ ter buy nothin’ with it?”
(< „U’ I m not!” (Gasps of dismay.)
( Well, am t there no buns nor lollies?”
‘No! passionately shouted the heir.
The dazed mob took counsel. Then a
small, quavering voice said : “Keep yer
rotten munuey ! We don’t want it!”
The others took up the cry and repeated it
with insulting variations.
“Keep yer stinkin’ munney!”
“Oo wants yer rotten munney?”
'|Buy beer fer yer ole man with it!”
“Yer ole man’s a dirty pro-Boer!”
“Go an’ wash yer dirty neck!”
The angry chorus enraged Strabswood,
and he turned aud ordered them to disperse
instantly. They paid no attention to his
turious command, but continued to scream
insults and challenges until the whole street
had come out to see what the riot was about.
By that time, Strabswood had been reduced
to a state bordering on imbecility. In ths
midst of the noise, he could be heard bab-
bling of legal proceedings and sucli-like
futilities. Mrs. Strabswood, with her arm
wound round her daughter’s waist, was
standing at bay on the verandah, while hep
husband, closely attended by his son, raged
uselessly on the lawn. He had realised too
late that a shilling’s worth of stale buus
and lollies would have saved all the madden-'
ing disgrace, and that enthusiasm and pop?
ular. applause must be paid for in some
fashion. “Clear out of this !” he screeched,
for the fiftieth time, and struck over the
fence at the yelling pack he had just ad-
dressed so affectionately. “Go on, now!
Clear home, all of you !”
“Pro-Boer!” they hooted. “Yah! you
pro-Boer!”
“Get out, you ragamuffins, or I’ll call a
policeman!”
“Oh, yoif raiser!” chanted a furious fat
boy. “Never give us nothin’ fer cheerin'
yer!”
"Yon don't love yer King!” jeered
another.
“If yer loved yer King yer’d give us
somethin’ ter eat!”
“I don’t bleeve yer 38 at all !”
(“CLEAR OUT. WILL YOU!”)
“Chuck a rock at th’ dirty pro-Boer!”
Strabswood gave a hurried direction to
his son, and that youth fled inside and re-
turned at top speed with a walking stick.
A shout of rage went up to the sky, and a
clod whizzed over the fence and smote
Strabswood on the shirt-front. Another
knocked one of the lanterns sideways and
set it blazing. Strabswood threw open the
gate, and, followed desperately by his son,
charged the enemy, who turned and flew.
While the walking-stick was swiping at the
legs of one enemy, the younger Strabswood
rashly tried to assassinate the lumbering
fat bov by kicking him with his lightly-shod
feet. The fat boy, desperate with terror,
fell on his assailant and gave him a really
severe rolling-on. ere rising and stumbling
heavily into the gathering gloom. The small
boy whose legs had been rapped by Strabs-
wood ran howling in front of him, while
Strabswood picked up his blubbering son
and conveyed him back to the house of re-
joicing, where the smoking ruins of the
lantern swung forlornly on the charred
string.
There was a much-needed interval of
perhaps 15 minutes, before the tribe of boys
again invaded the street. They returned
confidently to the attack behind a small,
fussy and intensely indignant man, whose
marked resemblance to the boy who had
been rapped showed that he was his parent.
Still more ominous, he was, as the attendant
boys took care to inform the street, the
Strabswoods’ landlord coming to inquire:
(1) Why his son had been assaulted? (2)
Why his property had been damaged? (3)
When the rent was to be paid?
Strabswood was found in the garden, and,
being in the mood for publicity, his land-
lord bailed him up there, and, having plied
him with his three humiliating questions,
sternly refused him a hearing when he made
an attempt to answer them. The small
bodyguard, packed along the picket fence,
grew hilarious as the interview proceeded,
and hooted madly when the furiops tenant,
having refused to pay up there and then,
was loudly directed to quit on the morrow,
or take the legal consequences.
“Three groans for th’ dirty pro-Boer!”
howled the landlord’s son. as his triumphant
father walked off. They were given with
a will, and an extra one added by way of a
bonus. Then the stout boy, who was some-
thing of a depraved genius, shrieked: “Give
’im Gord Save th’ King!” It was duly
? j ron _ an d given with extraordinary vim.

shrieks, hoots and cat-calls were introduced
to give variety. At last, the distracted
loyalist rushed tumultuously from his illu-

KODAK.
Loans and Stones.
A DRAMA IN TWO THUDS.
[For The Bulletin.]
The Treasurer (Mr. Cann), who returned yester-
day from a visit to the South Coast, stated that
there are from 1,000,000 to 1,300,000 tons of stone
in sight at the Government quarries at Kiama. “It
is quite true,” Mr. Cann continued, “that as far as
the old quarry at Kiama is concerned no stone is
being quarried there now. The reason is that better
stone is being obtained from the workings on the
opposite side of the road which are part of the same
quarry. The 1,000,000 or 1,300,000 tons I have
mentioned will last for a day or two yet, apart
from the new workings which the Government has
opened.
The Treasurer (Mr. Cann) stated yesterday that
before members dispersed for the election campaign
he would have to put through a special loan bill
approximating £3,000,000 for railway construction.
This, he said would be apart from the loan expendi-
ture outlined last week in supply.—S. M. Herald.
Tiiup The First,
j time: Between this and the Next.
Place: Anywhere handy.
Persons: Cann, a Treasurer; Jugg, a Sur-
veyor.
Chorus (con spirito):
How beamingly and beautifully blue
Is the glad September sea;
How astonishingly shiny is the sun along
the briny—
Whatever can the reason be?
Antiphonal Chorus :
Oh. the reason’s very, very plain to see!
The illimitable azure shows its_ man
In a manner quite benumbing—tis in honor
of the coming
To Kiama of the optimistic Cann !
Chorus (inquiringly): Cann?
Antiphonal Chorus (decisively): Cann!
He’s the man —
He’s the smilingest from Beersheeba to Dan.
While Kiama still is haunted
By a Ghost, he stands undaunted —
See! he cometh with a comrade and a Plan !
(Enter Cann and Jugg.)
Jugg :
The quarry should be hereabouts, my
lord !
Cann :
Should be? I like it not that “should
be.” Ah!
The Plan says (consults Plan) Emm! —
the Plan says this is IT;
This cliff that faces East, and Nor’ by
East
Three points; and to the South-West a
new road —
Well, there’s the road! (points)
Jugg (eagerly): Yes! that’s the road, my
lord ;
We must be off it —Yes! I see the hole—
The blue-stone hole, my lord; I see it
now.
Cann (consulting plan):
“A million tons of loans.” Good! Very
good—
A noble find —a million tons of loans.
Jugg :
Pardon, my lord, a million tons of stones:
Cann :
Eh? Yes —of course, of course! Careless
of me!
Stones — loans — stones —loans —the words
run in my brain
Like some poor poet’s rhyme. I should
say stones ;
But, tell me, Jugg, who cast up the ac-
compt V
Who estimated, measured, reckoned out
This noble sum —a million tons of (er)
stones?
Jugg :
An’t please your lordship, our Department
did ;
Our Public Works Department sent one
here,
Who came, and saw, and said, “A million
tons”
And wrote it in his book and went his
way.
Anon the great Mac Griffin sent for him,
His prescience to try out —and said to
him :
“Thou’rt a base and pessimistic fellow!
Here thou sett’st down but one poor mil-
lion tons —
Darest look me in the eye and let it go?
Write, fellow, thirteen hundred thousand
tons /”
And sq he wrote, my lord.
Cann :
T see—l see ;
Then call it thirteen hundred thousand
tons.
If Arthur, our great Knight of Estimate.
The star of Fourpercent. from Slump to
Boom,
Reckons it up to that, why—make it so !
Is that the whole? (Gazing at it.)
Jugg :
It is the hole, my lord,
And, as you see., a right assuring hole
Whereout have come great expectations
And still containing
Cann :
Say two million tons!
And now away, to so acquaint the public—
But what’s this other hole that stands out
yonder?
Another quarry? or some black man’s
cave ?
Jugg :
It is the old Kiama quarry, my lord —
Haunted, they say; a vast and fearful
hole!
No stone comes from it now—the Ghost
that haunts it
Is crippled—bedridden—and cannot walk,
But howls and moans inside as one in
pain ;
Old loans in torment utter just such
groans.
Cann :
Horrible! O ye gods and little fishes!
A Ghost that cannot walk —Oh, horrible!
Let us away—l shudder —Come ! Away !
(Exeunt.)
Chorus (trippingly):
Blue is the quarry, blue is the sea;
Blue is the sky o’er you and me;
Blue is the outlook, South and North,
Blue was the Plan that Cann drew forth ;
Blue is the hole that gapes so wide ;
Blue is the Ghost that howls inside ;
Blewed is the Cash that the State ran
through,
But still remaineth the IOU —
Nothing is lasting, nothing true.
But the firmament —and the azure hue —-
The deep, deep blue of the IOU!
Antiphonal Chorus (with conviction):
Yet there endureth for a span
The faith of the Optimistic Cann!
Thud the Second.
Time: To pay up.
Place: Of perks.
Persons: Several ephemeral; all irrespon-
sible.
(Enter the MacGriffin —Trumpets and red
fire.)
The Mac Griffin :
To _ owe, or not to owe —What a sugges-
tion —
Not to owe! Not to live were called in
question !
For while I live I’ll owe, as some one
said it —
An Altruist, I swell my neighbor’s credit —
Raise loans by stealth, and blush to find
folk knowin’—
Oh, my percented soul, my Uncle Cohen !
Will he, too, turn a deaf ear to my sorrow’?
By Jove! I’ll try another scheme to-
morrow ;
I must have fifty millions. How to get
them?
The brokers deprecate advances —let them !
I think I see a way out of my worries—
Bricks? Timber? Joinery? I have it —
Quarries !
Come, come! they can’t refuse them as
security,
They’ll give us a half-Nelson on Futurity!
By Lombardy! it is a glorious plan—-
I’ll talk it over now with Brother Cann.
Ah ! here he comes —Good day !
(Enter Cann.)
Cann :
All hail, Mac Griffin !
Mac Griffin :
Well met; three minutes chat, and then to
tiffin.
About those quarries, Cann?
Cann :
Well, what about them?
Mac Griffin :
I’m selling ’em. We’ll try to do without
them.
Cann :
Well, I am glad to hear you talking like
that! I’ve just come back from Kiama
—you know —and Jugg, of the Public
Works Department, told me you esti-
mated 1,300,000 tons of blue stone
Mac Griffin :
What? Only 1,300,000 tons? Nonsense!
Jugg’s all wrong. I said thirteen mil-
lion tons —in fact, if I were to have
another look and re-estimate, I believe
I should find about 130,000,000 tons!
Those blue-stone quarries are dreadfullv
deceptive. Say 130,000,000 tons at 10s.
per ton —that’s £65.000,000. See?
Cann :
Certainly. I'll see anything you say.
Mac Griffin (joyfully):
Good! Well, how do we stand?
Cann :
Debt, £107,000.000; loan account over-
drawn, £2,700,000; loans authorised.
£3.000.000; Norton-Griffiths proposal,
£3,000,000; special loan bill.
£3,000,000
Mac Griffin (impatiently):
Oh, come to the total! Never mind little
niggling details.
Cann :
Well — ei um —our total commitments
would be £116,000,000 up to the end of
the year.
Mac Griffin :
Pooh ! We must have a few millions, at
once; otherwise, we shall not be able
to carry on up to the Elections.
Cann :
I cabled yesterday for £3,000,000 at any
price under 5 per cent., to go to the ad-
vance account
Mac Griffin :
Three millions! Ha, ha!
Cann :
I ought to have a reply by now, but
Mac Griffin :
There’s a wire for you on my table now;
it came half an hour ago. (Searches.)
Here !
Cann (opening cable):
M—m —m —m. Eh? What’s this?—
Look !
[the cable.]
Lombard St., London.
Can n —Treasurer —Sydney.
Cable received. Uncle dropped dead
heart-disease on reading request.
Holding you morally responsible for
death.
Sol Bull-Cohen.
Collect.
Mac Griffin (solemnly):
The only thing to stop us. My God!
WTiat luck !
( Curtain.)
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.
